Jens Stoltenberg: How Norway Built the World’s Largest Fund | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(122.0K)·27:52·9.2K views·2026-06-10
Jens StoltenbergFormer NATO Secretary General & Former Prime Minister of Norway Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management
TL;DRNorway’s Finance Minister and former NATO Secretary General, Jens Stoltenberg, outlines the strategic political decisions that built the country's $2 trillion sovereign wealth fund, emphasizing the urgent need to reform its ethical guidelines on…
defense investments and...

TL;DR

Norway’s Finance Minister and former NATO Secretary General, Jens Stoltenberg, outlines the strategic political decisions that built the country's $2 trillion sovereign wealth fund, emphasizing the urgent need to reform its ethical guidelines on defense investments and warning against domestic complacency.

The Three Pillars of Norway's $2 Trillion Success

  • Norway vs. United Kingdom: Despite producing similar volumes of oil and gas, Norway built a $2 trillion fund while the UK accumulated national debt. Stoltenberg attributes this divergence to:
      • High Government Take: Norway instituted a 78% tax rate on oil and gas revenues alongside strong direct state ownership.
      • Fiscal Discipline: A high overall tax level allowed the state to save 100% of its net resource revenues rather than using them to cover basic budget deficits.
  • The Three Key Decisions: Building the world's largest sovereign wealth fund required three highly controversial political choices:
      • How Much to Save: A legal mandate ensuring that 100% of oil and gas revenues are saved directly into the fund.
      • How Much to Spend: The 2001 "golden fiscal rule," which limits state budget withdrawals to the expected financial real return (initially estimated at 3%).
      • Where to Invest: The pivotal 1997 decision to transition the fund's assets from safe bonds into global equities.

The Defense Paradox and Reforming Ethical Guidelines

  • Suspension of the Ethics Council: Stoltenberg suspended the independent Ethics Council due to unintended, outdated consequences stemming from ethical guidelines established in 2004.
  • The National Security Contradiction: Under previous guidelines, the fund was prohibited from investing in major defense companies like Lockheed Martin and Boeing.
      • The Paradox: Norway relies on these companies for its own national security (e.g., purchasing F-35 fighter jets) and delivers air defense systems from these manufacturers to support Ukraine, yet the fund was legally barred from owning shares in them.
  • The Tech-Defense Integration: Defense and modern technology are increasingly integrated. Stoltenberg warns that if the fund cannot invest in tech companies that service the defense sector, it will struggle to remain a broad-based global index fund.
  • The Roadmap for Reform: An independent expert committee, chaired by former central bank governor Svein Gjedrem, is currently reviewing the guidelines. A proposal will be presented to the Norwegian Parliament in Spring 2025.

Investment Strategy, Tech Concentration, and Private Markets

  • Extreme Tech Concentration: The fund has been one of the largest beneficiaries of the global tech boom, generating 1,600 billion NOK from top tech holdings over the past five years. Today, the top 10 tech holdings account for 25% of the total fund.
  • Avoiding Political Market Timing: Despite high valuation risks in US tech, Stoltenberg strongly opposes political interference in portfolio allocation, arguing that politicians attempting to forecast stock markets would lead to severe financial losses.
  • The Private Equity Dilemma: High-profile private companies like SpaceX, OpenAI, and Anthropic present a challenge. While public listing offers necessary transparency, the current mandate means the fund can only buy into these companies once they IPO, potentially missing early growth stages.
  • Geopolitical Stress Testing: To mitigate the risks of geopolitical fragmentation and potential market crashes (e.g., a 1970s-style downturn), the government has under-spent the 3% fiscal rule, keeping withdrawals at 2.7% to 2.8% to establish a protective fiscal buffer.

Addressing the "Norwegian Disease" and Embracing AI

  • The Threat of Complacency: With 25% of state expenditures funded by investment returns, Norway faces a growing risk of complacency. This is reflected in high welfare and disability enrollment rates and struggles to increase work participation.
  • Labor over Oil: Stoltenberg stresses that human labor, not oil or financial assets, is Norway's primary source of wealth. Fiscal policy must prioritize mobilizing the domestic workforce.
  • Leading in AI Adoption: To combat labor constraints, Norway is leveraging artificial intelligence. According to Eurostat, Norway leads European nations in AI adoption. The Ministry of Finance has integrated AI models directly with central statistics to automate data modeling and policy analysis.

Notable Quotes

"The oil and gas wealth is not for the Norwegian state, it's for the Norwegian people. And by spending the financial return, we ensure that the Norwegian people today and in the future will benefit." — Jens Stoltenberg
"For me, that’s a paradox, that companies which are so critical for our security, we buy a lot from them but we cannot own 1% of their shares." — Jens Stoltenberg
"It's not the problem to be rich, but there is a challenge to not become complacent. And the main message from me as Minister of Finance is that the main source for our wealth is not oil, the main source for our wealth is labor, work." — Jens Stoltenberg
Hi everyone, I'm Nicolai Tangen, the CEO of the Norwegian sovereign wealth fund. Today I'm really excited because I'm here with a guest who has defined the last 30 years of Norway's place in the world. So, a warm welcome to Jens Stoltenberg. Thank you so much for having me. Now, you created the fiscal rule that turned Norway's oil money into the world's largest sovereign wealth fund. You've been Prime Minister twice, and you led NATO for 10 years. Now you are back as Finance Minister, running the fund you helped create. This is very, very interesting. It is a fascinating history, and of course there have been many decisions making this fund possible. Actually, when I became Finance Minister 30 years ago, in 1996, that was the year we made the first installment into the fund. Since then, I've followed the fund very closely. This week, you compared Norway and Britain, two countries who basically produce the same amount of oil. Now, we've got a big sovereign wealth fund while Britain has quite a bit of debt. What happened here? How did we prioritize differently? It's always very difficult to compare different countries. But the fundamental message is that the United Kingdom has produced more or less the same amount of oil and gas as we have in Norway. In Norway, we have a sovereign wealth fund of more than 2 trillion US dollars, while in the UK, there is no sovereign wealth fund. Of course, there are many differences; for instance, the UK is a much larger country with more people. I think the main difference, and the main reason why we have a fund and the UK doesn't, is that we decided to have a big government take as part of the oil and gas industry. We have a 78% tax rate and strong direct state ownership, so much of the cash flow goes directly into the state coffers. Second, we have generally a relatively high tax level in Norway. Therefore, when we started to earn net revenues from oil and gas, we were able to save everything. That has not been the case in the United Kingdom. There have been some pretty big calls made here. First, to save the revenues and invest in equities, and then of course, you also have the spending rule. What do you think are the most important ones, and were they obvious at the time? No, all of them were politically controversial. I think what we have learned in Norway is that if you want to create a sovereign wealth fund, you have to make three important political decisions. One is how much to save. The second is how much to use, meaning both how much to put in and how much to take out of the fund. The third decision is where to invest. Regarding the first question of how much to save, we decided by law that all the oil and gas revenues are saved. Every cent, every dollar earned, is saved into the fund. That was the first decision: how much to put in. The second important political decision is about how much to take out. For that, we created the golden fiscal rule back in 2001. That rule says that the only thing we can withdraw from the fund is the expected financial real return, which we have estimated to be 3%. Do you think you would have done that differently if you knew how big the fund would become? No, I don't think so. Do you think, for instance, there would have been a clause that the fund cannot account for more than a certain percentage? Now it's 25% of the state budget. Do you think, for instance, you would have capped it at 10%? No, I don't think so. Of course, I was Prime Minister when we decided that rule, so maybe I'm very biased. Still, the fiscal rule to only spend the expected real return has served Norway well for 26 years now. I will always be open to other models and to listen to alternative ways of doing this. Fundamentally, I think we need to understand that the oil and gas wealth is not for the Norwegian state; it is for the Norwegian people. By spending only the financial return, we ensure that the Norwegian people today and in the future will benefit from this wealth. But then, the third political decision is where to invest. We decided back in 1997, actually in this very room in the Ministry of Finance, to start investing in equities. In the 2001 white paper, it said that the money should go to three things: incentives for work and investments, knowledge, and infrastructure. Do you think that has been successful? Yes. First of all, those were not the only things we should spend on. The main purpose of the fund is to support the state budget—healthcare, defense, police, whatever it is. Second, we said that we should spend some on infrastructure, and we have spent very much on infrastructure. I think one of the challenges we face in Norway is that not all of our infrastructure investments are very profitable, if you look at the cost-benefit ratio. Still, we have spent a lot on infrastructure. As for research and education, we spend a lot on them, at least through government funding. Regarding taxes, roughly one-third of the total oil and gas revenues have financed reduced taxes. In particular, we reduced taxes on electric cars. One of the reasons why almost 100% of all new cars in Norway are electric is that we have removed almost all taxes on them, and that has been quite expensive. Absolutely. Now, the fund was set up to avoid the Dutch disease. Some people would argue that we've caught some "Norwegian disease" instead. How do you think about that? Well, the Norwegian economy is not perfect. We have problems related to, for instance, the fact that we have many people on different kinds of disability and welfare schemes. We struggle to increase the work participation rate. Of course, we are vulnerable just by the fact that 25% of our expenditures are financed by the fund, which is highly dependent on fluctuations in the financial markets. So yes, we have to be aware of those challenges. I think the most important thing we have to do in Norway is to look into how we can increase work participation and ensure that more people work. Therefore, we have implemented a big pension reform. We are also looking at how we can further reform our welfare system to encourage more people to work. What do you think it does to a country where you don't really have to prioritize, where you don't have to choose either this or that, but can have both? Compared to most other countries, we are extremely privileged. That's correct. But my message is that while finding oil and gas on the Norwegian Continental Shelf was lucky, managing this income better than most other countries with natural resource wealth was the result of deliberate political decisions. We saved every cent, we only withdraw the financial return, and we invested in equity. These are political decisions, not luck, that enabled the fund to become so big. Today, everyone supports this golden rule of only spending the financial return. But in the beginning—and I remember that well, because that was during the 10 years I was Prime Minister—it was extremely controversial that we didn't spend more. Back then, 3% of a small fund was not a big amount of money. Now, 3% of a large fund is a huge amount of money, so it is much more accepted today than it was some years ago. Do you think it's making the country complacent? At least there is that risk. We need to be aware of it, because we have seen other countries become too complacent when they have too much oil and gas revenue. Again, it helps that we save everything so that we only spend 3%. If we spent it as we earned it, we would have spent much more, and we would be left without any revenues now that production has started to decline. It is not a problem to be rich, but the challenge is to avoid becoming complacent. The main message from me as Minister of Finance is that the main source of our wealth is not oil; the main source of our wealth is labor and work. Therefore, we need to do whatever we can to mobilize more Norwegians to work more. Changing tack a bit here. Last year you suspended the Ethics Council. Why? Because it had some unintended consequences that were not foreseen by the Norwegian Parliament when they decided these ethical guidelines back in 2004 and adjusted them in 2024. The fund is still managed within an ethical framework. You know better than anyone that there is still responsible management. There are expressed expectations for the companies, the fund can be in dialogue with different companies, and in some cases, we also divest. Of course, the fund has to follow Norwegian and international law. The part we suspended was a system within the ethical guidelines where an independent ethical council gives advice to Norges Bank to withdraw from specific companies. And then you set up a committee to review this. What do you hope they will come up with? I hope they will come up with guidelines that ensure we continue to have a solid ethical framework around the fund, but one that works better than the previous guidelines did. What we have seen is that these ethical guidelines have led to us not investing in major defense-producing companies that produce key capabilities Norway depends on. Norway buys F-35s, but we cannot invest in Lockheed Martin, which produces them. We cannot invest in Boeing, which delivers key military capabilities not only to Norway but also to our NATO allies and to Ukraine. For me, it is a paradox that we buy so much from these companies critical to our security, yet we cannot own even 1% of their shares. Of course, you have more experience than anyone else, having been the NATO Secretary General. When you were NATO Secretary General and you saw that Norway had these rules in place, did you realize that this was becoming increasingly untenable? Yes. One of the reasons why I took the initiative last fall to address the ethical guidelines was the experience I had at NATO. As NATO Secretary General, I traveled around and urged allies to build up the defense industry and work more closely with these companies. Then I realized that the Norwegian Pension Fund had a rule saying we could not invest in the very companies I wanted more of. That was a contradiction and a paradox. In one way, it didn't matter as much when the fund was smaller and the world was more peaceful. Now, we have a full-scale war going on in Europe, in Ukraine, and we do what we can to support them. Yet, the key capabilities—like the Patriot missiles, interceptors, and air defense systems we deliver to Ukraine—are produced by companies Norway is not allowed to invest in. That is a paradox. But you have five political parties in the coalition. Do you think you'll get them to come along to your view? I cannot guarantee anything on their behalf, but I hope we will get a clear recommendation from this independent expert council, which is chaired by Svein Gjedrem, the former governor of the Norwegian Central Bank. Once we have their proposal, there will be a public hearing starting later this year. We will then present the proposal to Parliament next spring. I hope we can reach a broad agreement on revised ethical guidelines. Another paradox we see is that tech and defense are becoming increasingly integrated. As a broad-based international sovereign wealth fund, if we cannot invest in big tech companies that deliver services to defense companies, we will no longer be able to remain a truly broad-based fund. That is another challenge we have to solve. We've seen an ESG backlash in other parts of the world, while the Norwegian political parties basically have unchanged views. How is that opening up challenges? I guess we saw that with Caterpillar in particular, right? Yes, but the Norwegian government and I are very concerned about the environment, sustainability, and governance—all the important considerations of the ESG framework. However, sometimes the way this has been applied and understood has not functioned very well. Caterpillar is a prime example for the fund. Based on the guidelines decided by Parliament, the independent Ethics Council recommended that the fund divest from Caterpillar. Yet, Caterpillar is an important provider of trucks and equipment we use here in Norway, and the Norwegian government is a customer. A small number of those trucks are sold to the US government, which then sells them to Israel, where they are used to violate international law by destroying Palestinian homes. We all agree that this is bad. But the question is whether Norway should completely avoid investing in a company just because a tiny part of what they do contributes—through the US and Israeli governments—to bad things. If that in itself is sufficient to totally divest, then it is very hard to see how we can invest in big tech companies. Speaking of big tech companies, some people ask who the big gainers are from what is going on in the tech world. One of them is basically the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. Over the last five years, we made 1,600 billion Norwegian kroner from the top companies. Now, the top 10 companies in the portfolio account for 25% of the fund. What are your reflections on that? First, as you stated, hardly anyone in the world has earned more money from tech companies than Norway, because we have been invested in them for a long time. We have stayed with them as share prices increased, and the returns from those investments have been incredible. Of course, if something goes up, there is also a risk that it can fall down. I will not speculate about the likelihood of what will happen in the US stock market or with these specific companies, even though 10 companies make up roughly 25% of the fund's wealth. My main challenge is to address whether we should change the guidelines. Currently, the guidelines decided by the government dictate that we should be broadly invested in all listed companies, following international markets like an index fund. If I started to politicize that and say that I believe the US market will go down because it is so high, we would run into trouble. People have had that concern for many years, yet the market continued to increase; if we had made a decision to pull back four or five years ago, we would have lost a lot of money. I am afraid that if politicians try to forecast the market, we will end up in a very bad place. Therefore, I am confident we should remain a broad-based international index fund, with some active management by you and your people, and accept that there will be downturns. But do you think it makes sense to own 7,000 companies, given that the big ones are so important and that if something bad happens somewhere in the world, the fund typically owns a piece of it? Do you really need all these companies? Well, it actually used to be close to 10,000 companies, and we reduced it to 7,000 to minimize administrative costs. I am not ruling out further adjustments in the future. However, I still believe in the fundamental idea that we should be broadly diversified across different markets and companies, as that is the best way to get the best return over time with the lowest risk. This approach has served us well for all of these 30 years. I believe it remains the correct basic approach for a government-owned sovereign wealth fund in a democratic nation. Mr. Stoltenberg, we have some big, privately held companies coming to the stock market soon—SpaceX, Anthropic, OpenAI. Do you think the fund should be able to also own private companies? This is a very sensitive political issue in Norway. It has been a great advantage that we have a very high degree of transparency and easily defined benchmarks, which are much more straightforward to establish with listed companies. Still, I admit there is a dilemma because some of these big private companies will most likely soon become public, and only then will we buy into them. The paradox is that as soon as SpaceX, Anthropic, or OpenAI become public companies, Norway will invest in them because we follow the markets. Whether that paradox is significant enough to change our policy is far too early to decide. At the very least, we should be aware of this challenge: we end up buying them the moment they are listed anyway. We run stress tests which say, for instance, that a fragmented world is generally negative for markets. We are seeing rivalries between some of the superpowers, trade barriers, and so on. What are your reflections on how the current geopolitical situation is impacting the fund? We have to be prepared, as you have also stated many times, for the value of the fund to fall substantially. That would have a direct impact on the Norwegian state budget. We have just presented a revised budget to the Norwegian Parliament containing some of these scenarios. If we saw the same development in the stock market today as we did after the oil crisis in the 1970s, the value of the fund would go dramatically down, and it would take more than 15 years to get back to the starting point. Since we spend 3% of the fund on the state budget, it would heavily affect us. The way we try to prepare for that is by spending a bit less when the value of the fund and the stock markets are high. We have spent around 2.7% or 2.8% over the last several years, creating a buffer. There is no way to totally avoid the consequences of a downturn, but by being careful when the markets go up, we secure a cushion for when they go down. So, where do you think the biggest risk to the fund is now? To be honest, I think that is an extremely difficult question to answer. It is unpredictability that characterizes the world today—both financially and economically, but also in terms of security and geopolitical tension. Wars are bad for business, at least for global business. We have a very dangerous situation in the Middle East and a full-scale war in Ukraine. I hope there will be ways to end these conflicts, but wars are dangerous; they can spiral out of control and affect global markets and the value of the fund. It is not only the human cost of war we have to worry about, but also the economic cost. Additionally, I believe AI is changing the global economy and creating a lot of value. However, with this enormous increase in share prices, there is always a risk of a downturn. My task is not to speculate or forecast future stock prices, but to define the best strategy for Norway to manage this uncertainty. Once again, diversifying across many markets is the best way to reduce our risk. Technology companies' share prices have gone up because there is so much potential in these technologies. Do you feel that Norway is using AI enough? I think to say "enough" would be wrong. However, we are actually quite good at using AI. According to Eurostat, we are the European country that uses AI the most, so based on EU statistics, we are doing quite well. But given that we are highly educated, very digital, and not always too keen on working, should we not use it much more? Absolutely. There is great potential here. Whether the glass is half full or half empty depends on what you compare it with. If you compare us with other European countries, we are doing quite well and using AI a lot in Norway. But if you compare it with what our ambition should be, yes, we should use it much more. The issue is not only about using AI, but using it in the right way and within the right frameworks. In this ministry, we have just upgraded the way we use AI, developing a new system to integrate it into our daily work. We have connected the AI models we use here with the databases of the Central Bureau of Statistics and the Ministry of Finance. Now, I can just go onto my computer and generate beautiful charts and figures by accessing the enormous amount of data we are responsible for. The public sector could and should use AI much more. It is a great advantage that the average Norwegian is highly educated and comfortable with digital tools, so we should continue to stimulate the use of AI. Speaking of beautiful graphs and figures, you were out of the country for 10 years. What was the biggest change or surprise you experienced when you came back to the ministry? Well, the biggest change comes from a paradox: I am back in a ministry where I worked 30 years ago. I was Minister of Finance in 1996, and the biggest change since then is that back in '96, there was zero in the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund, whereas now it is more than 2 trillion US dollars. If you compare this to the last 10 years when I was away at NATO, I think the biggest political change is that we now have an even more fragmented political landscape. Being the Minister of Finance is in some ways more challenging now because we have to negotiate more and make more compromises to get things agreed upon in the Norwegian Parliament. So, Mr. Stoltenberg, just to round off, what do you think are the most important things to remember for the next 30 years? It is that oil and gas is good, but it is not enough to ensure that a country develops in a healthy way. The most important thing, as always, is that we are able to ensure that people work, that they are well-educated, and that we invest for the future. The oil and gas helps us, but it is not a guarantee. And if you were to give some advice to the people running the fund, what would you say? Continue as you are doing, because you do an excellent job. I am always proud when I work with you or meet you, and also when I travel abroad, to see the great respect that both you and the fund receive. You are a highly professional, well-managed fund that serves Norway incredibly well. Thank you, and thank you for everything you've done for the fund and for the nation. Thank you so much.

The Google Capital Company | Stratechery by Ben Thompson

Stratechery(15.1K)·13:20·1.7K views·2026-06-10
Ben ThompsonAuthor, Stratechery
TL;DRGoogle is raising $80 billion in equity—including a historic $10 billion partnership with Berkshire Hathaway—to fund its massive artificial intelligence infrastructure.
This marks a strategic transition where Google's high-margin advertising cash flow is being redeployed...

TL;DR

Google is raising $80 billion in equity—including a historic $10 billion partnership with Berkshire Hathaway—to fund its massive artificial intelligence infrastructure. This marks a strategic transition where Google's high-margin advertising cash flow is being redeployed into a capital-heavy, high-absolute-dollar compute business, mirroring Berkshire Hathaway's historic transition from See’s Candies to BNSF Railway.

The Berkshire Parallel: High-Margin vs. Capital-Intensive Businesses

  • The See's Candies Model: Bought by Berkshire Hathaway in 1972 for $25 million, See’s Candies required only $32 million in reinvestment over decades while producing $1.35 billion in pre-tax profits. While highly profitable with a 60% pre-tax return on invested capital, it was limited by its physical growth capacity, leaving Berkshire with excess cash it had to deploy elsewhere.
  • The BNSF Railway Model: Unlike See's, BNSF Railway is highly capital-intensive, consuming $3.8 billion in capex in a single year. However, it generated $5.5 billion in net income on $23.4 billion in revenue—producing more absolute profit in one year than See’s Candies has in its entire history.
  • The Google Parallel: Google Services (advertising) is the ultimate digital See's Candies, operating at a 45% margin and generating $40.6 billion in profit on $89.6 billion in revenue (Q1 2026). Google Cloud is the BNSF equivalent: lower margin (33% in Q1 2026), but growing rapidly due to AI, which has the potential to scale across the entire global economy.
      • Cloud Growth Metrics: In Q4 2019, Google Cloud revenue was just 6% of Google Services and ran at a loss. By Q1 2026, Cloud revenue reached $20 billion (22% of Services) and operating profit hit $6.6 billion (16% of Services).

Anatomy of the $80 Billion Capital Raise

  • The Funding Package: Google parent Alphabet is raising $80 billion via:
      • A $40 billion at-the-market (ATM) program starting in Q3 2026, partly to cover tax obligations on employee equity awards.
      • $30 billion in underwritten share offerings and mandatory convertible preferred stock.
      • A $10 billion direct equity investment deal with Berkshire Hathaway.
  • Why Issue Equity Over Debt?: Equity removes the risk of debt but dilutes future upside. Since Google already has $126 billion in cash against $81 billion in debt, the choice to issue equity suggests two possible scenarios:
      • Massive Debt Coming: Google is preparing to issue an even larger amount of debt later, indicating that the market continues to underestimate the absolute scale of compute demand.
      • Risk Mitigation: Google is uncertain about the ultimate ROI on its AI capex and wants to share the immense downside risk with Wall Street and Berkshire.
  • Berkshire's Motive: Led by Greg Abel, Berkshire Hathaway had $373 billion in cash and $25 billion in free cash flow in 2025. Facing a shortage of high-yield investment options, investing in Google offers Berkshire a massive vehicle to compound its cash at a high rate of return.

Google's Structural Edge in the Commodity AI Era

  • The TPU Advantage: Google has custom Tensor Processing Units (TPUs) that provide a sustainable cost advantage over competitors. If AI compute eventually becomes a commodity, Google is poised to operate as the most profitable hyperscaler.
  • The Cash-to-Compute Moat: In the AI race, distribution and transaction costs are free, meaning the ultimate winners will be those who can deploy the most cash to secure constrained compute capacity.
      • While competitors like Anthropic and OpenAI struggle with compute access and must pay premium rates to secure hardware, Google's massive cash-generation engine allows it to fund its own supply chains.
  • Market Signaling: The $10 billion Berkshire deal acts as a validator. For Google, it signals that expected AI demand is far higher than the market thinks, justifying aggressive infrastructure spend. For Berkshire, it represents a calculated bet on the future of compute infrastructure.

Notable Quotes

"We were paying them $10 or $11 a click... And anytime you're paying somebody $10 or $11, every time somebody just punches a little thing where you got no cost at all, that's a good business, unless somebody is going to take it away from you." — Warren Buffett (on Google's early advertising model)
"Time is the friend of the wonderful business, the enemy of the mediocre... It's far better to buy a wonderful company at a fair price than a fair company at a wonderful price." — Warren Buffett (1989 letter to shareholders)
"Might we one day look back and realize that Google Services provided the cash flow to build a business with relatively worse margins but absolutely higher dollars, much like See’s helped fund BNSF?" — Ben Thompson
The Google Capital Company was published on Tuesday, June 2nd, 2026. What does the most beautiful business model of all time look like? First, imagine that your supply is free. Second, imagine that your customers willfully compete against each other to raise your prices. Third, imagine that your users decide which of your customers gets the privilege of paying you. All you have to do is build a bit of infrastructure to make it all happen, pay a nominal bit of depreciation on that infrastructure, and make billions of dollars on some of the greatest margins in the history of business. I am, of course, describing Google—a company so good that Warren Buffett, the legendary investor, could never quite bring himself to invest in it. Buffett explained this at the 2017 Berkshire Hathaway annual meeting: "We were their customer very early on with GEICO, for example, and we saw these figures—they are way out of date now, but as I remember, we were paying them $10 or $11 a click, or something like that. Anytime you're paying somebody $10 or $11 every time somebody just punches a little thing where you've got no cost at all, that's a good business, unless somebody is going to take it away from you. And so we were close up seeing the impact of that. But, you know, you've almost never seen a business like it." One of the characteristics of an aggregator like Google is the way in which they maximize absolute value at the expense of relative value. For supply, i.e., content on the web, Google dramatically increases the number of visitors, even as the value of any one visitor who comes from Google is worth much less than a visitor who visits directly. For an advertiser, the value of one click makes up for thousands of impressions of an ad that make no difference. For a user, Google helps them discover what they are looking for amidst the overwhelming abundance that is downstream from distribution being free. In every case, the aggregator increases quantity at the expense of relative quality, confident that the absolute amount of quality will be more in the long run. What is interesting is that this is the exact inverse in terms of why these companies have been valued by investors. The best tech companies are asset-light, predicated on maximizing zero marginal costs. Yes, they spend a lot of money on R&D and on the infrastructure to make markets happen, but they don't actually participate in those markets. Simply taking a skim and keeping the vast majority of that skim is what gets Wall Street excited. In other words, it was the relative amount of money made that was generally more important to the market than the absolute amount of money. Berkshire Hathaway and Productive Capital Berkshire Hathaway was, before Buffett acquired it, a failing textile business. Buffett originally invested because the stock was worth less than the liquidation value, and he ended up owning it outright after a dispute with management. It was a decision he regretted, as noted in the company's 1989 letter to shareholders: "If you buy a stock at a sufficiently low price, there will usually be some hiccup in the fortunes of the business that gives you a chance to unload at a decent profit, even though the long-term performance of the business may be terrible. Time is the friend of the wonderful business, the enemy of the mediocre. I could give you other personal examples of bargain purchase folly, but I'm sure you get the picture. It's far better to buy a wonderful company at a fair price than a fair company at a wonderful price. Charlie understood this early. I was a slow learner. But now, when buying companies or common stocks, we look for first-class businesses accompanied by first-class managements." One of the first-class businesses Berkshire Hathaway acquired was See's Candies in 1972. Buffett explained in the 2007 shareholder letter that they bought See's for $25 million when its sales were $30 million and pre-tax earnings were less than $5 million. The capital then required to conduct the business was $8 million. Modest seasonal debt was also needed for a few months each year. Consequently, the company was earning 60% pre-tax on invested capital. Last year, See's sales were $383 million and pre-tax profits were $82 million. The capital now required to run the business is $40 million. This means we have had to reinvest only $32 million since 1972 to handle the modest physical growth and somewhat immodest financial growth of the business. In the meantime, pre-tax earnings have totaled $1.35 billion. All of that, except for the $32 million, has been sent to Berkshire, or, in the early years, to Blue Chip. The problem with See's Candies is that there is nothing to be done with all of that profit. If it's privately held, then its owners end up with more cash than they know what to do with. And if it's public, then the job is to figure out how to return that cash to shareholders through some combination of dividends and stock buybacks. What Berkshire Hathaway did, however, was use that cash to grow. After paying corporate taxes on the profits, we have used the rest to buy other attractive businesses. Just as Adam and Eve kick-started an activity that led to 6 billion humans, See's has given birth to multiple new streams of cash for us. The biblical command to be fruitful and multiply is one we take seriously at Berkshire. One of the businesses Berkshire Hathaway used the See's profits for was on the opposite end of the spectrum in terms of capital utilization: BNSF Railway. Railways require a lot of capital to operate. BNSF consumed $3.8 billion last year. They also make a lot of money. BNSF's net income was $5.5 billion on revenue of $23.4 billion. To put that in perspective, the total amount that Berkshire Hathaway has made from See's Candies is probably less than $3 billion. The last disclosure was over $2 billion in 2019, i.e., less than BNSF made last year. So, which is the better business? Google Cloud's Runway In Q4 2019, the first year that Alphabet disclosed Google Cloud revenue, Google Services—the high-margin, beautiful business I described at the beginning—made $43.2 billion in revenue and $13.5 billion in operating profit. Google Cloud made $2.6 billion in revenue and lost $1.2 billion. Google Cloud revenue was 6% the size of Google Services. In Q1 2023, Google Cloud made a profit for the first time. In that quarter, Google Services made $62 billion in revenue and $21.7 billion in profit. Google Cloud made $7.5 billion in revenue and $0.2 billion in profit. Google Cloud revenue was 12% the size of Google Services, and its profit was 1% the size of Google Services. In Q1 2026, Google Services made $89.6 billion in revenue and $40.6 billion in profit. Google Cloud made $20 billion in revenue and $6.6 billion in profit. Google Cloud revenue was 22% the size of Google Services, and its profit was 16% the size of Google Services. Google Services is, needless to say, a much more scalable business than See's Candies. The growth just over the last seven years, more than doubling revenue and tripling profit, is astounding. And yet, at the same time, Google Cloud is growing faster. While its margins are worse—33% last quarter as compared to 45% for Google Services—they are expanding more rapidly. The bigger question is how big those numbers can go. Google Services’ advertising business is inherently high margin, but advertising is definitionally but a fraction of the overall economy. Google Cloud’s growth, meanwhile, is AI, which many people think/worry/hope might take over the entire economy. In other words, might we one day look back and realize that Google Services provided the cash flow to build a business with relatively worse margins but absolutely higher dollars, much like See’s helped fund BNSF? Berkshire Hathaway and Google Equity The context for this discussion is recent news from Bloomberg. Google parent Alphabet Inc. is raising $80 billion through a package of equity offerings, including an investment deal with Berkshire Hathaway, as the company races to fund its ambitious artificial intelligence spending plans. The undertaking includes a $40 billion so-called at-the-market program to sell shares from time to time beginning in the third quarter, according to a statement Monday. The company will also offer $30 billion in underwritten offerings of shares and mandatory convertible preferred stock, as well as the $10 billion deal with Berkshire. Together, the transactions represent one of the largest equity deals of all time, and they bring an unexpected twist to a blockbuster year for initial public offerings. First off, a decent portion of the ATM program, launching in the fall, is going towards paying tax obligations on Google Equity Awards, which are quite large thanks to the stock's run-up in value. That leaves equity being issued now, particularly the $10 billion to Berkshire Hathaway, which is fascinating for a number of reasons. The first question is: why did Google issue equity instead of debt? Debt is, all things being equal, the preferred instrument for investment. The proceeds of the latter pay off the former, and existing equity holders reap all the benefits. Equity, on the other hand, removes the risk of debt, but at the cost of giving up a share of future profits. Google has to date funded its massive AI-related capital expenditures with free cash flow, and while the company does have around $81 billion in debt, that is more than balanced by $126 billion of cash. In other words, Google's capacity to issue more debt, and to reap the financial benefits of doing so because interest is tax-deductible, is substantial. This leads to what may be the Occam's razor explanation: Google is also going to start issuing a lot of debt, which is to say that everyone continues to underestimate the amount of demand there is for compute. Of course, that's not far off from a more bearish interpretation: Google is uncertain about the return on investment of all that capex and would prefer to share the risk, along with the upside. If there isn't a substantial debt issuance down the road, then this might be the right answer. The second question is: why is Berkshire Hathaway suddenly, after all these years, interested in Google, and at only a slight discount to its all-time high price? Does it really just come down to the fact that Buffett is no longer making investment decisions, and Greg Abel, his successor as CEO, is? In fact, you can make the case that Abel is actually just replaying Buffett's strategy, only this time Berkshire Hathaway is See's Candies, and Google is BNSF. At the end of last quarter, Berkshire Hathaway had $373 billion in cash, and $25 billion in free cash flow in 2025. How many companies could actually employ that cash in a way that generated a high rate of return? It's hard to imagine a better option than Google. The company is not only investing in AI, but has optionality in terms of outcomes. Its Services business benefits from the investment. It is in contention at the model layer with Gemini, and it can sell capacity to the frontier labs. Moreover, that capacity has a sustainable cost advantage because of TPUs, which means that in a world where compute becomes a commodity—as hard as that is to imagine right now—Google is the hyperscaler that is poised to make the most profit. It is worth noting that $10 billion is a relatively small amount of money to both companies. To that end, perhaps the primary utility is as a signaling mechanism. On Google's side, the signal is that the expected demand is actually far greater than anyone thinks, and that the company is ready and willing to fund supply using all means at its disposal, including equity. For them, Berkshire Hathaway's investment is an endorsement of this view and a validation of the wisdom of the investment. On the flip side, if the signal is correct, then Berkshire Hathaway is getting a deal in putting its cash flow machines to work building the future. Cash: The Ultimate Commodity A couple of months ago, when Anthropic was clearly ascendant, OpenAI backers tried to make the case that actually OpenAI was in the driver's seat because the frontier lab had secured more compute. I made the case in "Mythos, Muse, and the Opportunity Cost of Compute" that this was not at all dispositive. OpenAI is betting that this compute constraint, and the deals they have made to overcome it, will matter more than Anthropic's current momentum with end users. I'm less certain that this will be dispositive. When it comes to AI, distribution and transaction costs are still free—the two preconditions for aggregators—which means that the winners should be those with the most compelling products. Those products will win the most users, providing the money necessary to source the compute to serve them. Consider Anthropic's deal to secure a meaningful portion of TPU supply, which, given the capacity constraints at TSMC, is ultimately an example of taking supply from Google. I suspect that Anthropic can take more, including already built hyperscaler and neocloud capacity. Yes, that compute will be more expensive, but if demand is high enough, the necessary cash flow will be there. That thesis was proven correct just weeks later when SpaceX ponied up the supply Anthropic needed, and yes, it was expensive. This deal is a perfect example of what really is basic economics. First, if demand exceeds supply, then prices should increase. At the same time, prices are elastic. If they are lower, there is more demand, and if they are higher, there is less demand. In this case, while there is broad demand for computing, Anthropic has arguably the most demand. Furthermore, Anthropic has the most willingness to pay, not just because they are making meaningful revenue, but also because they have the capacity to raise money in the pursuit of winning in AI. Implicit in this analysis was that there was enough compute capacity in the world to be bought. What happens, however, when and if there isn't? What if the ultimate battle, the one that determines who gets compute, becomes a matter of who can bring the most cash to bear? And what if that advantage compounds such that the company with the most cash capacity ends up with the most compute capacity, which you already know they will sell, in addition to using themselves, driving the ability to generate more cash? In that world, what company would be your best bet? We now know which one Berkshire Hathaway is betting on.

From $50M Startup To AI Powerhouse: Jennifer Tejada’s PagerDuty Playbook

Forbes(2.1M)·43:24·332 views·2026-06-10
Jennifer TejadaCEO, PagerDuty Maggie McGrathEditor, Forbes Women & Host
TL;DRPagerDuty CEO Jennifer Tejada discusses how modern enterprise resilience hinges on proactive AI-driven digital operations, noting that as system incidents have risen by 43%, maintaining brand trust and operational health requires both technological vigilance and executive self-care.

TL;DR

PagerDuty CEO Jennifer Tejada discusses how modern enterprise resilience hinges on proactive AI-driven digital operations, noting that as system incidents have risen by 43%, maintaining brand trust and operational health requires both technological vigilance and executive self-care.

The Evolution of PagerDuty and AI Operations

  • Scaling the Business: Since Jennifer Tejada became CEO nearly 10 years ago, PagerDuty has evolved from a private, founder-led startup with less than $50 million in ARR and 200 employees into a public enterprise serving two-thirds of the Fortune 100 and half of the Fortune 500.
  • The Strategic Timeline of AI Integration:
      • *2016 (Early Days):* Leveraged proprietary machine learning and analytical AI to consolidate and correlate system telemetry data, automating the diagnostic phase.
      • *Generative AI (PD Advance):* Introduced a few years ago to allow responders to query incident status and customer impacts using natural language.
      • *Agentic AI:* Recently launched five native agents—including an agentic site reliability engineer (SRE), scheduler, and insights analyst—to drastically reduce human toil in incident resolution.
  • AI as an Operational Risk: Tejada defines AI as a major new operational risk layer. To navigate this, companies must balance rapid implementation with strict safety guardrails, avoiding using risk as an "excuse to go slow."

Building Enterprise Resilience and Navigating Chaos

  • A Rising Threat Landscape: System incidents have increased by 43% across PagerDuty's platform in the last year, driven by the rapid deployment speeds made possible by code assistants and developer automation.
  • The High Cost of System Failure:
      • *Financial Burden:* The average system incident costs a PagerDuty customer roughly $800,000, excluding the long-tail economic effects.
      • *Damaged Brand Trust:* Customers vote with their feet when applications lag or fail. Drawing on her early days at Procter & Gamble, Tejada notes that when a brand's backend technology fails, customers do not blame the vendor or supply chain—they blame the brand itself.
  • Proactive Preparedness: "Hope is not a strategy." Companies must transition from being reactive to being proactively resilient. This involves scenario planning and running tabletop exercises with board directors to ensure they can execute crisis playbooks under public scrutiny.

A Non-Traditional Leadership Blueprint

  • Executive Resilience and Self-Care:
      • *Sleep as Infrastructure:* Tejada rejects the Silicon Valley trope of operating on four or five hours of sleep. She prioritizes eight hours of sleep and regular workouts, asserting that physical health is the foundational infrastructure of executive performance.
      • *Authentic Boundaries:* Tejada actively cancels meetings or organizes schedules around sleep and fitness needs, choosing hotels based on wellness amenities (like yoga mats) and speaking openly about it to remove the stigma.
  • The Transition of CEO Mindset:
      • *First CEO Role:* Characterized by a fear of making mistakes, which resulted in over-preparation as a coping mechanism.
      • *Second CEO Role:* Learned to let go of the fear of not knowing everything. Tejada practices radical transparency and directness with her team, calling out organizational weaknesses early rather than "gilding the lily."
  • The Hovercraft Mindset: Tejada attributes her leadership success to a high level of intellectual curiosity and a "hovercraft" style—the ability to pivot rapidly from 60,000 feet down to 6 inches to solve complex business operations.

Career Transitions and the Power of Unconventional Decisions

  • Consumer Brand Foundations: Started her career at Procter & Gamble working on physical brands like Pringles, Folgers, and Jif. This hands-on, non-glamorous experience established her customer-first empathy.
  • Quantifiable Tech in the 1990s: Left P&G for i2, a supply chain automation company. She fell in love with tech because of its tangible value proposition (e.g., proving to clients that software cut supply chain inefficiencies from $10 million to $5 million).
  • The Sydney M&M Choice: To decide whether to move to London or Southern California with her partner, they placed three colored M&Ms in a cup and let a flight attendant draw the winner. Green won, leading them to Sydney, Australia. To this day, green M&Ms serve as a symbol of luck and peace for Tejada, appearing at board meetings and public events like her IPO.
  • Active Board Service: Serving on the board of Estée Lauder since 2018 as the first technologist in their boardroom. She leverages her digital transformation background while keeping her "consumer brain" sharp, believing board service is a powerful vehicle for executive growth.

Notable Quotes

"Chaos is inevitable... humans are imperfect, software is imperfect, AI is imperfect, and the systems that we use to run businesses and deliver brand experiences have become increasingly more complex. They’re not manageable by humans." — Jennifer Tejada
"When a brand fails, when something behind the scenes that supports a brand and its customer experience fails, the customer doesn't blame the supply chain, the electric company, the technology platform, the technology vendor... they blame the brand." — Jennifer Tejada
"Your health is the infrastructure of your performance as an executive. And to pretend it’s not, to like be proud of how poorly we could treat our bodies and still succeed, you wouldn't see an elite athlete do that." — Jennifer Tejada
"If you’re not annoying 10% of people, you’re probably doing it wrong." — Jennifer Tejada
Hi everyone, I’m Maggie McGrath, Senior Editor at Forbes. On this episode of C-suite Unscripted, we sat down with Jennifer Tejada. She is the CEO of PagerDuty, a publicly traded company that helps other companies manage their tech emergencies. We talked about how chaos is inevitable in the age of agentic AI, and also why she feels it’s so important to get eight hours of sleep and to say publicly why she does this. But I think my favorite part of this conversation was the part where Jennifer talked about the time she used M&Ms, the candy, to make a career decision. Let’s dive in. You have been CEO of PagerDuty for 10 years, almost—your anniversary is later this year. I’m wondering, just to give our viewers a sense of what you do, can you kind of compare and contrast where the business was when you took over as CEO compared to where it is today, including there was an IPO in the middle. Yeah, I mean the business was very small when I joined. It was founder-led, it was venture-backed, it was private, it was less than 50 million in ARR, we were less than 200 people, and it was very much a self-serve, bottoms-up developer solution that helped developers manage their products and services more effectively when they weren’t working the way they expected them to work. And the idea was that you would get ahead of a technology emergency before it became a big problem for the business and importantly for customers. What I found really interesting and compelling about that was so many companies that I’ve worked with—I mean digital operations, digital transformations been my playground from a career standpoint—were going through these massive transformations from brick-and-mortar customer engagement, physical brand deployments to more of a digital brand experience. And there’s a lot that has to happen behind the scenes to ensure that every digital brand experience is perfect and wonderful and has the consumer or the customer coming back over and over again. And PagerDuty was collecting a lot of data on why things break, how they break, where the dependencies are, what’s required to diagnose them and fix them, but wasn’t putting it to work with AI. And so even back in 2016, I had this vision that we could take that proprietary data set and put it to work in service of automating a lot of the diagnostic and the triage and ultimately the resolution of what we now look at as major incidents. So the business, we grew it over time. I mean, there in the early days people wondered, "Could it be a standalone business?" And I think my perspective, having worked in a lot of other industries both in software and actually on what I would call now my customer side, the enterprise business, I just saw this massive market—like a total addressable market of large enterprises, the most important brands in the world that are still going through this digital transformation and now being asked to almost overnight convert their businesses into AI-native businesses. So it’s also been this really interesting leadership journey because it hasn’t been just about growing the company and pivoting our strategy and expanding the market that we can serve and demonstrating that we have the enterprise resilience to serve the largest companies in the Fortune 100 or the Global 2000, but also the most innovative AI natives and startups. It’s been about growing our leadership and demonstrating that we not only serve our customers through our products and services, but also by providing grounded advice on how to make the leadership transition that has to accompany the technology transition. And now that every company is a technology company, what we do—digital operations and AI operations—is essentially enterprise operations. So it’s been actually quite a rewarding journey because my vision hasn’t really changed, but the pace of technology has changed so much to make it more interesting and more intellectually challenging every year. So many follow-up questions, but I want to dig into the technological emergencies that you referenced, or when things break. Because we, for this series, have talked to cybersecurity CEOs, we’ve talked about malicious actors who are going after companies and demanding ransom and really wreaking havoc. How much of your job is helping companies guard against those external threats versus maybe it’s just something breaks, as you said, when they’re implementing a new AI technology and your technology then has to come in and disentangle an own goal, so to speak? I think the most important thing for a large enterprise or a mission-critical software provider to understand is that chaos is inevitable. These major issues where something doesn’t work the way you anticipated it to work, which then leads to a significant business impact, significant brand disruption, customer disappointment—these things are going to happen because humans are imperfect, software is imperfect, AI is imperfect, and the systems that we use to run businesses and deliver brand experiences have become increasingly more complex. They’re not manageable by humans. They require a lot of telemetry, again a lot of diagnostic, a lot of learning and understanding to pick apart what the driver is. And the PagerDuty platform doesn’t discriminate between whether the genesis of an issue is because of an external bad actor or an internal malfunction or simply just the collision of multiple events that turn into kind of a domino effect and become a major incident. What we’re all about is identifying that anomaly early, getting to a diagnosis quickly, and as automated as possible resolving that issue before it impacts the end customer, before the blast radius expands to financial impacts or customer impacts. And also in service of learning. Sort of like if you go to the emergency room, you learn something about your body and you’re going to maybe change your behavior after you’ve healed and come out of that part of your journey. It’s the same thing with incidents. Every incident is a huge opportunity to learn. That chaos leaves us with phenomenal lessons where we can actually action what we learn and prevent more fragility in the future. I think now boards, audit committees, CEOs all not only understand the importance of enterprise resilience, but they’re prioritizing it. You’re seeing investment move to these areas, and there’s a leadership challenge associated with it, because with enterprise resilience also comes operational resilience and leadership resilience. You have to be prepared for the unexpected. You have to anticipate these emergencies because how you respond to them is what demonstrates your commitment and your care for your customers. Whether you’re talking to your customers or you serve on boards, you’re an investor yourself, you kind of have a pretty wide purview on technology leaders, business leaders in the country. What’s your sense of how attuned they are to these risks and the need to mitigate them? Are you still in the convincing phase when it comes to potential customers, or is all the interest inbound? People generally underestimate the blast radius of a major incident, right? I think we’re so used to customer forgiveness. You know, a mobile network is down and after three days maybe consumers forget about it, it’s no longer a hot news story. But what happens when that consumer’s contract comes up for renewal? You know, do they have a long memory around that reliability, that resilience that they expected? I think as the barriers to entry in consumer products has come down, or consumer applications, customers vote with their feet and they vote a lot more quickly than they have. I mean, you only need to stand outside of a building in any metropolitan area and watch people toggling between their rideshare provider, their food delivery, the shopping that they’re looking for in a short period of time. And if their transaction gets stuck, they don’t wait. They just switch to another application, right? And that switching cost is low for the consumer but high for the brand. The other thing—I learned this at Procter & Gamble in my very early days—is like when a brand fails, when something behind the scenes that supports a brand and its customer experience fails, the customer doesn’t blame the supply chain, the electric company, the technology platform, the technology vendor, the person—they blame the brand. They take it out on that brand relationship. And trust is essentially our new currency as an economy, particularly as we’re releasing agentic agents out into the market, as we’re using more and more AI. Like trust, there’s a very high value placed on trust now, and enterprise resilience really drives a lot of that. So I think CEOs understand it. I mean, I’m being asked by a lot of CEOs to talk about how do I operationalize trust and build my resilience posture so that when these things happen, I can assure the best possible outcome. That’s a proactive stance versus just "help me be more responsive" or, "gosh, I hope this doesn’t happen to me." Hope’s not a strategy. Like we know that. It’s not a good strategy anyway. Hope is not a strategy. Now you said earlier that chaos is inevitable when we’re stacking technology or bringing in new systems. I want to tie that to the dollar value risk that you were just alluding to. Obviously there is money lost when there is a technological breach or when something breaks. But as a consumer I have to say, I expect a certain degree of something’s going to go down, there’s going to be a data breach, some bad actor on the dark web has my phone number, I know this based on the spam texts that I get. So I’m resigned to it and I don’t know that I’ve exactly shifted my behavior. And I’m just one person. But what do you place as the potential dollar value? Is there a maximum value figure that you cite when you’re talking to folks about this could cost your company TK billion dollars? Well, what we know is that just in the last year major incidents are up 43% across our platform, and we’re serving two-thirds of the Fortune 100, half the Fortune 500, more than half of the Forbes 50 AI leaders. And when you step back and you say, okay, incidents are up 43%, that’s not very surprising because of the velocity with which we can now deploy new technology into production, into the wild, using code assistants, you know, just with automation in the software development lifecycle. That’s not surprising. The challenge is that the average incident costs our customers $800,000. Now look at how many incidents a customer has a year, and now we’re talking about millions of dollars associated with the initial disruption, and we’re not measuring the long tail of challenges associated with it. I agree with every generation there’s a lower expectation around data privacy, around perfection, but there is an expectation around a delightful experience. You know, like in e-commerce, one of the things I pay a lot of attention to is shopping cart abandonment. Like how many shopping carts get abandoned because somebody’s distracted, a process takes too long. So it’s not things going down, it’s your journey being disrupted and you moving on to something else because there’s lots of things vying for your attention, right? Same thing with transactional disruption. You know, in financial services, fintech, when transactional velocity slows down because of a technology issue, a security issue, money’s not moving where it’s supposed to move. Consumers can’t access their capital, they can’t make the trades they want to trade, commodity contracts don’t get completed or closed. So these are big financial issues that you may not notice day to day, but when you start to accumulate them, they have a very significant cost. Having said that, like we human beings are very adaptable, we can normalize some of these threats and challenges, and you know, I would argue from a consumer perspective and from a leadership perspective, we need to have a more vigilant, resilient posture because whether it’s bad actors or system complexity, it’s getting harder, not easier. And the bad actors have the same access to the great new technology that comes with AI that we do. So you have to be moving as fast or faster than them. And a big part of leadership now is anticipation and scenario planning. What could go wrong? What would I do in that circumstance? How would I operate in that circumstance? And what’s the outcome I would expect? How would I communicate that to my customers? When would I communicate it to my stakeholders? A lot of resilience is about practice—tabletop exercises, running through these scenarios with your board, actually practicing what happens when the proverbial stuff is hitting the fan. Because it’s one thing to say like, "I have a playbook," it’s another to say, "could I execute it in the moment that really matters when I’m under public scrutiny?" In talking about the way that PagerDuty has grown since you’ve become CEO, you mentioned AI when referring to the early days. So obviously we know that this technology was not only just invented two years ago, but the rapid scale over the last few years is different than what we saw in the early days. But can you clarify, was AI part of the business plan from day one of your tenure? Absolutely. I mean, the reason I joined the company was to leverage that data set to automate. Initially we used machine learning and analytical AI to do things like consolidate and correlate information coming into the platform instead of humans having to do that. Then we used AI to automate workflows, to help our customers recognize that an incident that they were experiencing right now looks very similar to an incident they’ve resolved previously, and here’s what they did and what they could do to resolve this incident. And then even automation to run the runbook that does the resolution itself. We then graduated to generative AI and we were able to move pretty quickly to launch our product PD Advance a couple of years ago because our data was already well-engineered for it. So now instead of asking people and working in Slack or Teams, you can just ask the platform: "hey, has this ever happened before? Who’s the best person to pull into this response? How long has the response been going on? How many customers are impacted? What’s the relative nature of the impact?" These are things you can just in natural language ask the platform. And then this past year we released five agents, including an agentic site reliability engineer, an agentic insights analyst, an agentic scheduler that helps to put together the response team for a major incident response. And so we’ve gradually been reducing some of the toil that human responders have to undertake. And a responder can be anybody from a software developer to the CTO to the head of public relations to the chief legal counsel to the CEO. I mean, I have a lot of CEOs who call me after they’ve been quote-unquote paged during a major incident. I even had CEOs call me and say, "We had a major incident, I wasn’t paged, what happened?" I’m like, "You need to buy a license for that." So I would just say that AI has been core to how our product works and how we’ve automated the process, helping our customers not only detect that they have a major problem coming their way, but potentially prevent a small set of issues from storming to become a major business-impacting event that has a whole long tail of implications for the company. It’s your job as CEO to not just be reactive but to be proactive. But the pace of agentic AI development on the technological side has surprised even me and my colleagues at Forbes. So I’m wondering, as you sit here today and talk about what PagerDuty is doing, is there anything that you can admit that you’re surprised to say that you’re doing with AI? I’m surprised at how quickly some of our employees have embraced AI to do some really magical things in the business. Some of the best ideas have not come from our technologists, but actually some of our functional experts who identify a big pain point in, you know, how to build marketing content or how to engage with customers for lower-value transactional conversations, how to do research, how to get to an answer faster, how to run or simulate scenarios as opposed to actually run the full experiment. Like we’re seeing these come through in product marketing and marketing and finance, in human resources, even our executive admin team in scheduling. Like they’re all finding ways to not only lift their own personal productivity but fundamentally change the way they’re getting jobs done. And that is a little scary, kind of daunting, and at the same time like it’s really exciting. There’s a huge premium on good judgment. If you just simply do what the generative AI or the bot or the agent wants you to do, sometimes that’s not a good idea. So there’s some babysitting these agents to be done, there’s trial and error, making sure you have guardrails in place, and still making sure that every employee in the company understands the culture of care for our customers’ data, for our customers, our employees, our stakeholders’ security, and aren’t inadvertently creating insecurity in our environments. So doing it safely. And frankly, you know, for for me as someone who finds this industry really interesting, AI is the new operational risk layer in business. We don’t have all the answers because we actually don’t fully understand the potential of what it’s going to do and therefore what the risks are. And so again, like for leadership, this is about having a growth mindset, being open to not knowing at all but trying to learn it all, and surrounding yourself with folks that are on the front edge of experimentation. That’s why we have made a very significant effort to work with native AI startups and the frontier LLMs, the largest infrastructure players in the AI ecosystem. We’re learning so much from our customers who are using PagerDuty to build their own enterprise resilience but also learning from them like how they think about their business differently. We don’t know the full potential. I don’t hear that from a lot of CEOs. I feel like a lot of your peers use lofty language to talk about what AI can do. Is there anything that frustrates you as you look across Silicon Valley or beyond about the way that AI and its potentials are being talked about, or maybe not talked about? There is a lot of bluster out there. Back in the old days there was an audio cassette tape company and they would be like, "Is it real or Memorex? You can’t tell anymore" because of the quality of the sound. Like there’s a lot of "is this real or is this product marketing?" How does this work? Of the jobs to be done, how does the AI actually work? I think risk is a two-sided coin when it comes to AI. On one hand, we have a responsibility to society, to our stakeholders, to our customers, to our shareholders to anticipate risk, to identify and prioritize it, and to have mitigators in place for it and to constantly be—I’ll use the word—vigilant to continue to sort of strengthen our posture as it relates to resilience. And on the other hand, using risk as an excuse to go slow is a formula for losing. I have a lot of empathy. Like, my leadership discussions with my customers, with CEOs, with CTOs is evolving from "What are your recommendations on technology best practices and operations best practices?" to "How does my leadership need to evolve? How are some of your other customers handling the challenge of—your board wants you to move fast and become AI-native quickly and build competitive advantage and fend off competitive disadvantage that could come from moving too slow? Your people are worried about being replaced and in some cases may be resistant. A lot of our experiments aren’t paying off in terms of real true productivity gains yet. So like, how do we move quickly to unlock the largest value and return on investment from what is not inexpensive, our AI investments?" And so there’s like a leadership mindset that I think requires you to constantly step back and look at the big picture, to stay much more tapped into not just what’s happening in your industry or your market or your company but what’s going on on the horizon—like what can you learn and how can you be applying it. And every CEO needs to know how to use these tools and needs to have experience byplay, needs to understand what their infrastructure is, needs to know the unit cost of compute, because these have such a significant impact on our business. That’s not a technology problem; this is a business problem, it’s a financial problem. And if we can’t put the right guardrails in place, it also becomes a societal problem. So yeah, I mean, I’m really proud of what our agentic SRE can do and the way it can uplift the work of a team and how much more quickly it can resolve an incident. But I’m actually more interested in how do we redefine business operations as AI operations and what does that look like, and how does leadership need to change, how do we prepare leaders for a different reality than the one they’re used to? As I listen to you answer my questions, I’m struck by the fact that you mention learning and evolution quite a bit. How important is it to you as a CEO to be curious or have a learner’s mindset? Is that a fair conclusion to draw about your leadership strategy? Yeah, my team would probably say it’s like the best thing about me and the worst thing because I’m constantly ideating, I’m constantly asking questions. I can go from 60,000 feet to 6 inches very quickly, like a hovercraft in that regard. You know, I will tell you one of the things I learned early in my career when I was a new manager was one of the most important things you can understand about a person in your care or in your team is what drives them, like what motivates them. And I like to be on a steep learning curve all the time. I enjoy being the man in the arena, so to speak, because I like that every day is different and my intellectual curiosity appetite is always fed. And I think that learning is a currency that you can constantly be accumulating; you don’t run out of things you can learn. And so for me, because that motivates me, being in an industry that’s constantly changing and having a customer base that spans from financial services to e-commerce to, like I said, the most innovative AI natives in the world, essential industries, travel and hospitality—like you think of the challenges that we see across the global community of business, whether it’s geopolitical or it’s how do we deal with the energy requirements that AI has, there’s no shortage of things for me to learn and try and figure out and prioritize. Where are the biggest problems to be solved and how do we lend ourselves as a way to help automate solving those problems or helping our customers avoid them, prevent them in the first place. As you think about your leadership journey, you started as the CEO taking over for a founder-led company, which comes with its own challenges. I mentioned earlier there was an IPO in there, now we’re in a booming age of agentic AI. You also said earlier that there’s learning within chaos that can happen. So I’m wondering, is there a critical moment that you look at in your CEO journey that fundamentally shaped the way you approach being a CEO? My first CEO role, I always hear people in Silicon Valley talk about imposter syndrome. And I don’t really have imposter syndrome, but my first CEO role I was afraid of making mistakes. I had like—I woke up every day with the fear that something would come up and I wouldn’t know how to handle it. And my instinctive response to that was to over-prepare, was to like try and anticipate every single possibility that could possibly manifest and be prepared for it. And by doing my homework, you know, that was like a coping mechanism to manage the fear. In my second CEO role I realized, I was able to sort of reflect and look back and go, "I could never know everything, I could never be prepared for everything." And the emotional energy I gained by letting go of that fear and being open about it and saying to my team like, "I don’t know the answer to this, can you go figure this out?" or "Hey, if this happens, I don’t think we’re well prepared for it." And that can be very jarring for people, but it is a shift in the way my team’s used to a level of directness that they might not get in other places. But I find that being direct about where I think our strengths are, where I’m concerned, where I think we’re not prepared, is far better than gilding the lily and trying to have them feel all falsely confident, right? But it’s not for everybody; it’s not for the faint-hearted. We’ll get to your career history in just a minute, but I also want to ask you about something I’ve heard you talk about in interviews about how you maintain your humanity in a very demanding job. And something I’ve heard you say is very important to you—you will cancel workouts, you will cancel hangouts—is sleep. I don’t hear a lot of CEOs talking publicly about "I need seven or eight hours of sleep." Why is it important for you to not just get the sleep but say it publicly? It’s fair to say I’m not like a lot of CEOs. You know, I didn’t fit the mold when I took on my first deep tech enterprise software CEO role. There weren’t a lot of people who looked like me in that job at the time. And so I’ve learned that what works for some of my peers doesn’t work for me, and I have to be very specific about how I build my own enterprise resilience or my own executive resilience. I used to be one of those people who was very proud of the fact that I could operate on, you know, four or five hours of sleep. But when I stepped back and looked at like what was the actual quality of my operation, I wasn’t optimizing for the right things. And when I became a mother things changed, when I became a CEO things changed. You know, my travel schedule requires a certain level of robustness from a health standpoint. And if my health fails, it’s not just bad for me or my family, it’s bad for the company. So being proactive was a shift I made about five years ago. And a combination of things—like, I’ve changed the way my day starts, so I make sure that if I need to sleep a little later because I have to stay up a little later or because of the time zone change, etc., I’ll organize around it.
