I will cancel meetings. I will not miss a workout if I can avoid it. We were talking earlier, I mean, I choose my hotels based on who puts a yoga mat in my closet. I think these things—your health is the infrastructure of your performance as an executive. And to pretend it’s not, to like be proud of how poorly we could treat our bodies and still succeed, you wouldn’t see an elite athlete do that. And I grew up as an athlete. So like, I actually know the difference between optimizing for your nutrition, your strength, your sleep. But it was easy to rationalize, "Oh, I don’t need that sleep, these things are not important." And I just learned the hard way that wasn’t the case. You’re making me feel better about an email I sent earlier today. Someone was asking about an evening meeting and there were options between meeting at [X] vs meeting at [X] PM. And I said, "Let’s do the 6 o'clock option, I would like to be in bed by 10 PM." And I sent it off without really editing myself and then I spent the rest of the journey—I was in transit—thinking, "Oh shoot, that was too too candid, that was too too needy, I shouldn’t have said that." And now I’m going to stop second-guessing myself. I mean, and maybe there are people who respond to it that way. But if you’re not annoying 10% of people, you’re probably doing it wrong. You’re optimizing for the lowest common denominator. So in my view, like, I can’t please everybody, I can’t be perfect, nor should I try. It’s actually an inefficient use of energy and resources to perfect that last 2 or 5 percent. It’s really about like prioritizing our most important customers, our strategy where we’re going, our people, our talent. Talent is hard to replace when it’s really high talent. I remember Andre Iguodala in a fireside we did—Warriors amazing basketball player and leader and now tech investor—reminded me of something one of his coaches used to say, which is, "Do your job." It’s really simple. Like, you have a job to do, you need to do it. But how you accomplish it is up to you. I think it’s easy to get swept up in the noise that’s in our social feeds, on the news, etc. You have to reflect on your own requirements and what’s important to you. And then now build a micro-customized plan for yourself to optimize how you show up every day. And you can, there’s so much technology to support us. Well, I think it’s really interesting to see where people come from and what they learn in each of those roles. And I think our audience does too, because if anyone wants to be a CEO, your resume kind of offers one potential path. Was your first job out of college at Procter & Gamble? Yes. What made you want to work there? My first job at Procter & Gamble was a happy accident. I was planning to go to grad school, I was planning to study in the space my dad had worked in—healthcare administration. And healthcare is still an industry that I deeply love and have a lot of interest in. The industry deregulated during my second semester of my senior year and there just weren’t going to be opportunities that resembled what I was dreaming for. And so my dad and others suggested like, "maybe you should interview for some jobs," and it was kind of late in the game. Thankfully, because I played golf in college and I had been in a number of leadership positions, P&G reached out to me. I had no idea what they did. In our career center we had the Dewey Decimal System; I had to go read microfiche to learn about P&G. I read their printed annual report before my first interview. But what struck me in my first three interviews with them was just the consistently high quality and high integrity of the people. They’re smart. They were leaders in their own right before they ever stepped into their first job. They’re committed. And also the opportunity to participate in what I still think is one of the best leadership academies in the world. I mean, if you look around at the number of CEOs that came out of P&G, it’s pretty impressive. And you also worked on Pringles while you were there, which is a fun fact. I mean, I had some crazy jobs in my leadership rotation there. I worked in the food and beverage business in inner-city Cleveland on the Pringles brand, the Folgers brand, Jif peanut butter. It was really interesting, at the coal face of, you know, where our brands interact with our consumers. There was nothing sexy about it. I turned down opportunities with banking companies and consulting companies to do this and found myself scraping off gum and shelves for Sunny Delight in a Cleveland grocery store and wondering what I was doing. And yet one of the things I discovered about myself is I’m an experiential learner. And one of the things I took away from that role is you can never lose sight of who your customer is, the power they have to determine your success, and what they need. And so if it’s the dairy cooler at a six-store grocery chain in Cleveland or it’s the CEO of a large investment bank, it kind of doesn’t matter. Your job is to understand them, to empathize with their pain, to be able to prioritize how you can best serve them. So you took that learning and then transitioned to the world of tech. Was that a hard decision? I was frustrated with the sort of structured, methodical pace at P&G after five years. You know, and much like my total career, my P&G career was very much like—I think four of the six jobs I had there were experimental; I was the first person in the role, they were testing something. And I would always put my hand up to jump off the normal ladder to try something new. And it’s the culture of innovation. I don’t have the same risk profile as a lot of people do; I don’t worry so much like, "Oh, am I not ticking every box in the order on the same pace as everybody else?" I’m like, "That could be really interesting, I want to be a part of that change or that innovation." And that’s been, I think, a hallmark of how I made career decisions. So I met a technology leader on a plane following a customer visit and they were describing what they were doing, and I was already looking at other consumer technology companies. I was very aware in the mid to late 90s that the internet wasn’t a digital billboard, that it wasn’t going to be static, that it was going to change everything. Just like I’m very aware that AI isn’t incrementally making things better, it is massively disrupting a lot of workflows in our lives and in our work, and I wanted to be a part of it. And I made the decision to go to a supply chain automation company which was kind of off-brand because I was a consumer marketer and salesperson. It makes sense; there’s supply chains involved with all of P&G’s products. Yeah, but what I liked about it is it’s very quantifiable—the value that you can create. i2 at the time had a concept called the i2 value proposition and we would go and measure obsolescence and inefficiencies in someone’s supply chain and put a monetary number on it and then, when we implemented our software, deliver milestones against that financial goal. And I loved the tangibility of that. I loved the fact that I could say, "when I got there this cost them $10 million, now it only costs them five, and it’s this much faster." So the intellectual curiosity side of me was again well fed; my appetite, you know, was well fed in that job. And I also was the only woman on the leadership team. A lot of what I’d learned about running a business, about managing a team, about running a performance review, dealing with the street at P&G served me well in a startup essentially that was scaling really, really fast. And that’s where I found out that technology as an industry is a culture of innovation and a place that I wanted to spend my time. I want to be on the forefront of evolving business and evolving society. I don’t want to be the tail trying to wag the dog. So you were at i2 for four years and then you went to a telecom company. Well, I sort of accidentally moved to Australia. How does one accidentally move to Australia? It’s not exactly over state lines. So, I mentioned like athletics has been a part of my life for a long time and, much like my job, I like trying a lot of different sports. So I played tennis in high school, golf in college, and in my early career I got into yacht racing, you know, racing sailboats. And I’m small, I’m 5’4”, and I wanted to race big boats, and the only job that you can do on a big boat as a small person that’s fun is really trimming or driving. So I decided I had to get certified in this if I was going to be able to compete at the level I wanted to compete at, and the best place to log 5,000 nautical miles quickly is Australia. I went there and I met my now husband of 20-plus years in a yacht club bar. And he lived in London, I lived in Southern California. We could not agree where to live—he wanted to stay in Europe and I wanted to stay in California. So we put three M&Ms in a cup and let an airline stewardess choose where we would live, and we moved to Sydney. You put M&Ms in a cup? Yeah, it was easier; it removed the emotion of the situation. It was very like objective and we moved to Sydney and we thought, "What the heck?" At the time the .com bubble had burst, the tech industry was kind of in a chaotic place. I had done very well at i2 and was ready to try something different, so we moved there. But there was not a very big technology industry in Australia. So I ended up taking a job with a large telecommunications company, Telecom New Zealand, and helping them revitalize and reposition and sort of pivot their Australian business and also help them with their overall brand and marketing. And it was a really good lesson because I think so much about experimentation in your career is learning what you love and what fills your cup so to speak, but also learning what you don’t like. Because I think most people are better at doing something they love or are very interested in than just doing a job. And I learned I do not do well in a slow-moving environment. I become frustrating for other people. I like to be part of something where you’re constantly unaccepting of the status quo—like, it’s harder on my team because I am never satisfied. I’m always looking forward at the horizon on where we need to be and how we get there. And in a slower-moving, more bureaucratic place, like, that’s not a good place for me. Have you used the M&M decision-making tool in the years since? No, but M&M karma has been very good for me. I carry M&Ms with me everywhere—there are M&Ms in my purse out in the lobby. My dad was a big M&M guy, he died in my early 20s, and so I always have a bag of M&Ms along with me in case I just need a little pick-me-up. What color was the winning? Green. You’re wearing green today. Green was for Sydney. Green was for—that’s incredible. And it’s funny because it’s sort of followed me when we were doing the IPO. One of the bankers when we sat down on our first sell-side analyst discussion the day before pricing, there was a bowl of green M&Ms in front of me. And then William Lauder kindly—I told the story in his leadership class that he teaches at Wharton, he has me as a guest and I told the story about M&Ms—and the next board meeting when I sat down in my chair at Estée Lauder was this beautiful jar of green M&Ms. And it just brings me kind of peace and joy, and yeah. You are on the board of Estée Lauder, I should note. We were going through kind of chronologically, but just since you mentioned, you joined that board in 2018? Yeah. What made you decide? Had you aspired to a board seat? Because I know that’s sometimes a decision that people struggle— Yeah, I’ve been a board director for more than 20 years. Because you see a lot of the same problems that you’re trying to solve day to day in your operating job, but through a different lens. Operator empathy is at a very high premium in board meetings. I think CEOs more than ever need coaches and mentors and therapists on their board as well as good fiduciaries. And I’ve just found that surrounding myself with other great CEOs and directors has really helped support my own learning and development as an executive. Estée is a phenomenal company, similar in a lot of ways because I have taken over for a number of founders. It was one of the original founder-led businesses; they build magical brands. The family plays a very important role in both the company’s legacy and its future, and it’s one of the best management teams and boards you’d ever have the opportunity to sit in a room with. So it’s been a great learning lab for me, and also like I felt like I uniquely had the opportunity to add some value because I was the first technologist in that boardroom. I knew a lot about cybersecurity before, you know, that was something they were focused on. As a sitting CEO, I have a lot of empathy for management when they’re in the room. So I’m constantly celebrating management when they come in and make presentations. I’ve served—I’m the chair of the Nom and Governance committee, or Nom Governance and ESG, and so that’s provided an opportunity to help steward a change in leadership from the previous CEO to our current CEO, and that’s been a really rewarding experience, hard but rewarding. And I’m just really proud of what that board’s done. I actually am always receiving requests to join boards, and I’m very picky. And at the time that Estée came up it was a lot of infrastructure software companies, software application companies coming, and I’m like, "I do that all day long, I’m an expert in digital transformation, I would like to tap into my consumer brain and not lose track of that." And so I was looking for a consumer business going through digital transformation. And Stacy Philpot Brown, she probably doesn’t know this, but she told me at a party—I said, "How have you found the right board, not just any board seat, but like, I don’t want to be on just any public board," and she said, "You just keep saying over and over again explicitly what you’re optimizing for." And I did, and one day one of my banking partners came to me and said, "Hey, I heard you’re willing to do something that’s not tech, and I have this weird left-field thing. If you wouldn’t mind, I’ll introduce you to William Lauder." And he was the executive chair at the time, and William and I had a 2-hour 45-minute breakfast. And that was the beginning of my long relationship with the Estée team. I just got chills. I love when there are stories about how things fall into place, but intentionally falling into place. You had a set of goals. And I want to go back to your resume because I’ve heard you talk about a role where you were not CEO, you were I think number two. Was that when you were Chief Strategy Officer at Mincom? I had strategy, product, corp dev—you know, I had a lot of things. But the CEO at the time kept saying, "You should be CEO." Yeah, so this is Greg Clark, and you know, he’s a hard driver. He's one of the few executives I know who’s also split his adult life between Australia and the United States. The CEO Must Be the Chief AI Officer

Y Combinator(2.3M)·54:07·14.4K views·2026-06-10
Pedro FranceschiCo-founder and CEO, Brex
TL;DRThe current AI wave represents a foundational platform shift equivalent to the invention of electricity, requiring CEOs to act as "Chief AI Officers" who rebuild their organizations' core workflows around agentic loops rather than simply retrofitting legacy processes.

TL;DR

The current AI wave represents a foundational platform shift equivalent to the invention of electricity, requiring CEOs to act as "Chief AI Officers" who rebuild their organizations' core workflows around agentic loops rather than simply retrofitting legacy processes.

## The CEO as the "Chief AI Officer"

  • Systemic Restructuring: Pedro Franceschi (Co-founder & CEO of Brex) argues that AI implementation cannot be outsourced to product or engineering teams. The CEO must act as the "Chief AI Officer" because only they possess the organizational authority to rebuild cross-functional operations from first principles.
  • Re-Founding from First Principles: Instead of applying AI on top of existing processes, companies must design workflows from scratch as if they were founded today.
      • *The Brex KYC Example:* Rather than just automating back-end Know Your Customer (KYC) reviews with an agent, Brex redesigned its onboarding funnel to score and qualify leads for free at the very top of the funnel, shifting risk-management upstream.
  • The Virtual Employee Model: Rather than aiming for a single, chaotic company-wide AGI, organizations should deploy specialized virtual employees (agents) with narrow domains and clean APIs.
      • *The "Customer World Model":* Brex ingests every customer touchpoint—from dashboard clicks to support emails and executive flight issues—into an agent that provides account executives with real-time, comprehensive context.

## "Freeing the Claw" and Secure Agentic Loops

  • Esalen vs. Foxconn: Developers frequently stunt AI agents by over-engineering rigid coding harnesses and conditional statements (treating the LLM like a "Foxconn factory" worker). The most effective AI products are simply agentic loops equipped with tools and left free to execute tasks (the "Esalen Institute" approach).
  • Network-Layer Security via Crabtrap: To safely run highly autonomous agents in enterprise environments with financial data, Brex built and open-sourced Crabtrap, an HTTP proxy security layer.
      • *LLM as a Judge:* Crabtrap proxies and records all outbound HTTP traffic from an agent. It uses an independent LLM to audit traffic against a predefined policy, automatically approving benign requests.
      • *Production Efficacy:* For Brex's recruiting agent, "Jim," 98% of network requests are automatically approved by the policy, and only 2% are routed to the LLM judge for evaluation.

## The Economics of "Token Maxing"

  • Aggressive Token Consumption: Founders must overcome "token shyness" and aggressively spend on API calls ("token maxing") to intimately discover the actual limits and failure modes of current models.
  • The S-Curve of Global Adoption: Franceschi highlights the massive, untapped upside of AI using a global usage breakdown:
      • 84% of the world has never used AI.
      • 16% have used a free chatbot at least once.
      • 0.3% pay $20/month for premium access.
      • 0.04% (1 in 2,500) actually utilize agents.
  • Token Attribution with Magpie: To manage and understand the ROI of mounting inference costs, Brex built Magpie, an internal tool that attributes every dollar of token spend to a specific customer product, internal operational service, or employee.
  • The Future of Capex: Even as token costs decrease by 10x, corporate consumption will scale 10x to stay on the bleeding edge, making inference a dominant, long-term corporate expense.

## The Human Bottleneck: Empathy and Theory of Mind

  • Signal Outside the Training Data: The primary role of the founder in an AI-dominated world is finding "signal" that does not exist in model training corpuses. This requires deep customer empathy and "theory of mind" to understand what users are experiencing but failing to verbalize.
  • Minimal Surface Area: Historically successful startups (e.g., early Stripe as an API-only service, early Brex as a terminal-only MVP) succeeded because their narrow product footprint forced founders to focus entirely on nailing a single, high-leverage user interaction. AI should not be used as an excuse to build bloated, undisciplined product surfaces.
  • Self-Reinforcing "Dream Cycles": Agent infrastructure should include automatic correction loops. At Brex, when an expense agent runs into a friction point or error, it triggers a bug report. An internal agent then attempts to refactor the codebase and prompt files to ensure the new "eval" passes without breaking existing tests.

## Notable Quotes

"The CEO needs to be the Chief AI Officer. It's not an engineering team thing, it's not like a product team thing... you have to understand the bounds of the technology better than anyone." — *Pedro Franceschi*
"Electricity was invented in December [with the release of advanced models like Claude Opus]. We are now five or six months after electricity was invented, and most people are still playing with candles and questioning, 'Who needs light?'" — *Pedro Franceschi*
"The execution is gone and the models are going to do that better. The wisdom to choose is still the missing bottleneck." — *Pedro Franceschi*
You wake up, whatever problem you have in your life, why can't you solve it with AI? And just, like, start there. I think the CEO needs to be the Chief AI Officer. Like, it's not an engineering team thing, it's not like a product team thing. It's like, you have to understand the bounds of the technology better than anyone. I think a good proxy for how to spend your time is: what are things that only you can do and the models cannot do? You have to sort of refound the very concept of what the company's self-identity is. Tell us a bit more about your personal OpenClaw journey, because before you came for lunch, I had it installed, but I was like way too scared to do anything with it. We were all scared. You were all scared. Yeah, don't get me wrong. Like, we deal with financial services data, we spend a lot of time figuring out how to be mindful of security and protection. But and yet, I think people are a little bit more risk-averse than the technology probably requires them to be, given where the technology is. And when we started using OpenClaw personally, I started doing it on a lot of my own personal setup. Basically what I did in the V1 was, I'm going to give it read access to everything and just create, like, OAuth tokens to my email, to Slack, and to everything, to just literally not write. And I was kind of shocked how far it got me. And the next question that we spent time on at Brex was, okay, like, how do we actually get it to write into our systems? And everybody, including our security team, was like, "Well, we cannot do that for all the reasons that we know." And then basically where I spent, I don't know, probably four weeks of my time was: okay, let's solve the hardest problem, which is security. And we ended up realizing that the only way to actually do something about it was to do something at the network layer. And if you treat the agent like, you know, the agent has its own wills, desires, and they go to the Esalen Institute for agents instead of the Foxconn factory. Instead of the Foxconn factory. They will try to do things at a network boundary that could not be the right ones. And we decided to actually just focus on that layer. So a lot of folks were, you know, and we saw Nvidia and others on NimbleClaw, let's build these like open shell forks that have controls over, you know, what tools the model calls. And the reality is, yeah, you can do all of that, but you can also just make an HTTP request wrong. So we focused on that layer and then we built this thing called Crabtrap, which we open-sourced probably about two months ago, which is the actual way we use to secure agents at Brex in production. And the basic premise is you analyze, you HTTP proxy the entire network boundary of an agent. And the idea is, when a request goes through, that becomes auditable and you basically can use another agent to analyze the traffic and create a policy to let traffic go through or not. Surprisingly, or unsurprisingly because these models are trained on, you know, hundreds of billions of web documents, HTTP traffic is actually—I would say probably—the way the models reason, more so than anything else, because they literally learn on the web. So the ability of the model to watch, like, a thousand requests and make sense of what's happening was way higher than we anticipated. So we actually built that, put that in production at Brex, and after you record the traffic of an agent operating for a day, you can build a pretty good policy that, you know, sets things that should be automatically approved. And for things that the agent isn't really sure, you can just use an LLM as a judge. And the LLM determines: is this request something that should be approved or not based on the policy for what that agent should be doing? For example, we have a recruiting agent at Brex called Jim. We have a policy for Jim and, you know, all the traffic goes through that same policy. 98% of requests go through automatically, 2% use an LLM. So we sort of got that problem solved to a degree that we got comfortable experimenting much more aggressively and sort of freeing the claw on the enterprise, which is really hard inside Capital One. So I would say if we found a way to experiment with these things—and granted, we don't do the most aggressive things with this stuff yet, we don't use it on, like, customer data to the degree that we want one day to do, and there's boundaries to how we do it—I don't see any reason why a YC company shouldn't be at the bleeding edge of this stuff. Yeah, I mean, I think your intuition around the proxy at the network level ended up being quite prescient. I think a lot of the stuff that I'm seeing kind of around the OpenClaw ecosystem at the moment at least, or just agent ecosystem, is essentially doing that. We're seeing that, like, with credentials, credential brokering—like AgentVault is doing a lot of that. I think you had mentioned the first version of Crabtrap included a credentials vault. Why did you decide not to include that? I think it was just: let's just do one thing really well. And, you know, at the end of the day, I think there's going to be a lot of solutions that do that. You could do credential brokering in other tools already. But the LLM as a judge was for us the determining capability to say: do you trust this in production or not? Our security team at Brex, who are very rigorous and very good at what they do, for a long time were like, "Well, you know, not really." To getting them to a "Yes, we actually believe this is enough" was a big unlock for us. And look, I always say this—we're not in the business of building HTTP proxies, we are in the business of being at the bleeding edge of what you can do with AI. And to get to the bleeding edge required us to build this proxy, that's why we did it. Hopefully, a YC company is just going to build a better version and we're just going to go use it, but at the end of the day, that's the journey that took us to sort of being at the bleeding edge in that way. And how much of this was you sort of pushing this forward, and how much resistance did you get internally? And just how did you get everyone else AI-pilled? I mean, we got—I think there was a lot of excitement about it. But the way I describe AI adoption inside most companies is, I think there's like, sort of three tiers. There's tier number one, which is your token maxers—like your engineers that are pushing a bunch of code and typically living inside coding harnesses. And those are sort of well known; we know who those are. Then you have the sort of average engineer that is building a few things but is not a token maxer to the same degree, and probably has a tenth of the productivity. And then you have like the entire rest of the company. The entire rest of the company typically is interacting with AI in what I call Google search mode, which is a chatbot with a few MCPs, or a G Suite equivalent where, yeah, you have a few tools from Google, but at the end of the day, it's really just like a search. And where our thesis was, if you think about the value that AI creates for a token maxer, for example, a lot of the value comes from the harness. And the thesis was: how do you actually build an equivalent harness for other teams that are non-technical? Our whole sort of thinking behind it was like—that's a lot of what OpenClaw created, which is this ability that you can self-bootstrap a lot of the capabilities of the agent by the way you edit your skills and markdowns and sort of set up the environment around the agent. How far can we get this ability for the agent to self-bootstrap a capability without anyone actually going and coding it by hand? The analogy we use internally for the company-wide adoption of AI is, we don't believe in the, "Yes, give people a few MCPs and let them go," because I think what people really want is a way of saying, "Okay, this is actually a virtual employee almost." It is on Slack, it has an email, I can actually invite it to a meeting, it can join a meeting and take notes, and you're trying to replicate that as much as possible. So how do you build the infrastructure to support that kind of use case? And I think the harnesses will look a little different and probably more like OpenClaw than a coding model. To me, there's this, like, the AI pill test, in my opinion, is: whatever problem shows up in your life, do you default to AI first or not? Like DoorDash in the early days, similar, right? It was just literally... So the surface area was so small with the customer. I always tell people, if you can't minimize your surface area and solve the problem with a very clear set of boundaries, you haven't found the right problem to solve. And you can, of course, find how to compress the problem into a smaller surface area using AI, and that's really valuable, but I don't think you should use it as an excuse not to do that because, "Well, I can just build so many other things." Would you still use that pattern now? Oh, 100%. I think one of the hardest things of building a company is talking to customers—and not just having the conversation, but how to extract the sort of unspoken signal from these conversations. And I think, to me, the 'can AI solve this' lens—like, whatever problem shows up in your life, can AI go solve that? And you think about building a successful company: why can't you prompt your way into that? The reason is very simple: it's because there's signal that the models weren't trained on. And the signal is when you go talk to a person and they tell you about a problem they have. They're not going to give you the answer shaped into a prompt that you can put into an LLM, and that LLM is going to go and output the product that's going to win and be a billion-dollar company. They're going to tell you a very sort of local optimum answer based on their worldviews, their constraints, and the way they see things. And I think the job now is to have the wisdom to choose what you want. Before, the wisdom was not just to choose; it was to choose and know how to execute it. The execution is out, right? The execution is gone, and the models are going to do that better. The wisdom to choose is still, I think, the missing bottleneck, and to me, that all comes from which signals are not in the models. Say, like, pre-AI you had personal bandwidth to explore three ideas in parallel. The way I would probably approach it is, let's pick a broader universe of things for early, initial exploration. But to me, the lens is: okay, why can't AI solve it, and which signal is not in the model? And I think the signal is typically the customer. And then when you go talk to the customer, I think I wouldn't parallelize that problem. I would be like, "Okay, let's try to get in the headspace of this person." And I think there's—it's so easy, and we did a lot of exploration with, like, synthetic customers and building customer world models and things like that. But I think what I've seen is, you have to remember LLMs are not magic. LLMs are trained on a very specific corpus of information, optimizing for a very specific set of benchmarks and outcomes. And I think the biggest pitfall of LLMs is you have no sense of how much training data the model has seen for the exact thing that you're asking it. You look at very large companies with very large budgets that could be token maxing—where the economic thing for them to do would be to token max—and they spend like, I don't know, 10,000 a month. And you're like, "You should probably be spending 10 times more, or 20 times more, or 100 times more." That's still surprising. And I think the reason is again, sort of similar to the point in the beginning: we did this exercise two and a half years ago where I sat down with a lot of the engineering and product leaders in the company, and we had this question, which was: if you started Brex again in 2024—and the answer would be even more different now—what would we do differently? And turns out, like, everything. You start going down this route, and it's kind of maddening because you're like: okay, we have this completely old way of even thinking about the fabric of the company, and the way we build the product, and the way we build our processes internally. The first best answer is, "Yes, we wish we had started now." The second best answer is, "Let's go do something about it and change the way we do things," right? And I think a lot of our approach in terms of adopting AI has also been: how do you pause and say, "Okay, there is a discontinuity—not just in how we solve the problem, but in what the definition of the problem actually even is," and sort of take a step back and rethink it? There are millions of examples of that, but one example which is kind of funny is we're redesigning our KYC process. Whenever we onboard a customer, we have to do all these checks to KYC the customer. KYC historically is something where you can automate, like, 80% of it, and 20% is manual. And of course, the original impetus for anyone is, "Let's build an agent that does it." Yes, we can go do that, but what we decided to do is actually say: let's redesign the entire process end-to-end. When you redesign the entire onboarding process, what you realize is there's a very important thing that happens in the beginning of the funnel, which is deal qualification. Like, is this customer even remotely qualified to be a Brex customer? But when you have KYC for free, you can KYC a lead versus a customer, so you start to have risk orientation up in your funnel, and that changes who you even target because you know who's going to qualify. And the same thing is true for credit to some degree. So now the bounds of the problem have changed, and I think a lot of our competitors had this approach of saying, "Oh, I have this entire old process, let me go and, like, latch on AI on top of it," or "latch on AI on top of our product." I think the biggest discontinuities in a positive way that we've had were when we said, "Hey, let's keep this old way here, put it in a corner, and how would we design it if we started the company today from scratch?" and then just doing that. It takes a little bit of founder energy to do that, but I think it's the only thing we've seen working to really sort of inflect. I think that reminds me a lot about—sort of, way back, I don't know if you ever tried to compile Arch distributions of Linux. The culture within power users of Arch Linux versus Ubuntu is very different. Very different. I think the Ubuntu people kind of feel more like people that try ChatGPT. Stuff kind of just works out of the box; there's some stuff that you can get up and running. There are still not a lot of people that use Linux, by the way, which is what I think feels like where AI is. But with Arch, you're like super hardcore. And I think that's what OpenClaw and Hermes feel like, because you have to really customize it to your own use case, maintain your skills, have all the markdowns, and if you get it working, you can build something awesome. One of the most impressive things I've seen people build with Arch—actually, I don't know if you know Valve, the steam engine? The operating system that runs—that makes that feel like a Nintendo Switch is actually built on top of Arch. They customized all the drivers, over-the-air updates, it works with all consoles, it works with all sorts of hardware out of the box, but they super-duper customized it. And I think this is kind of what's happening: if you get your OpenClaw to work really well for you, you could kind of build your own custom Nintendo Switch for whatever you need to do. Yeah, I always have this thing that I tell people, which is funny, which is: think about your time two years ago. I feel like you're working a lot more now than two years ago, right? And probably the same for everybody here. So then the argument is, "Oh, but what about the productivity? Where is the productivity?" right? And I was talking to the CFO of a very large public company this week, and she was telling me, "We see all this token consumption, and we're trying to measure product velocity, and we're seeing more lines of code pushed. So yes, maybe that's the way to measure the ROI, but is it really there? Because people are spending so much on tokens." And I think this analysis—like, yes, of course, having a sense of ROI on tokens is important—but I think it misses the point that you're standing in the timeline of history and it's six months after electricity was invented. Imagine someone saying in the 1800s, "Oh, my electricity bill is so high now, like, gosh, let's use a little less. Let's push this steam engine to come, like, maybe 20 years later because of the cost savings..." Yes, of course, don't bankrupt your company on tokens. It's actually a perfect analogy because, I don't know if you know this, but when electricity was first invented, it didn't work very well, and the ROI was actually bad. And so if shortly after the invention of electricity some accountants had done this analysis, they would have been like, "Oh, this electricity thing is never going to be a thing; the ROI sucks." Why did people stick to it? It wasn't the cost savings; it was just because people were curious about it. And I think the point of why, you know, like—I was yesterday until 2 a.m. playing with slash-workflows and Opus 4.8 and all that—is because I think I'd be doing the exact same thing if I wasn't making any money, because you just see the possibilities. You see what you can do with technology, and that just drives people to behave differently. And I think that, to me, is the ultimate litmus test. It's a good separator. And sure, tokens are so expensive, but they're going to be, I think, over the fullness of time probably free, if you project 100 years down the line almost, compared to electricity. Now, we don't think about electricity costs in our day-to-days, unless you're in a data center. But I think there's something similar, for sure. We talk to a lot of founders of later-stage companies who wish that their companies could be as AI-pilled as possible, and you run this big company now with all of these employees. I'm curious what you've done to bring the rest of the company along with you on this journey, and if you have advice for other people or other CEOs. Well, I think there's still a lot to do. I think the CEO needs to be the Chief AI Officer. It's not an engineering team thing, it's not a product team thing—you have to understand the bounds of the technology better than anyone. I would argue that unless you really experience the limits of the technology every day, it's really hard to even understand what it can possibly do. Oh, you know why? It's because nobody can say no to the CEO except the board, and the board won't be in the weeds, per se. That is 100% true. When you go think about—like, you know, the whole example of KYC that we were saying. The KYC team would never think of using the KYC technology to score a lead. The only people that can think about the organization of the system itself is if you have the context of the whole. And to me, the single most important question that any CEO needs to answer is: forget about the competitive landscape. Imagine you could get the state of the technology today and transport it to the day you started your company. The opportunity would still be the same, but the possibilities of how to build a company are totally different. How would you do it? And then diff this versus what you have, and first suffer in silence for a little bit, because you will—I mean, I do every day. But then the second thing is: okay, what do you do about it? How would you do it if you were starting from scratch? You'll be the one figuring out, okay, how do we design our onboarding process, or how we design our growth engine, our customer acquisition, the way we talk to users, and the way we synthesize the data? All of that would be redesigned from scratch. So I think it's almost like you have to sort of refound the very concept of what the company self-identity is, and the way the functions and people's sense of success get structured. AI is an umbrella that I think has, like, three things in the way we talk about it internally. There's product AI—the product we actually ship to customers. There's operational AI, which is things that directly affect our ability to serve customers at scale, like customer success, risk, onboarding, operations, etc. And then there's corporate AI, which is how people work internally. The three agendas matter, and they matter in different ways depending on the timing of the company. I think people sometimes sort of pigeonhole themselves in one of the three. But in reality, I think you have to take a step back and be like—you know, the same thing we were talking about earlier—why can't you solve everything with AI? At a limit, that's the question, and then sort of start from there and start problem-solving around that question. It's a turnaround; I think you have to assume that if you're a big, large company that's not AI-native, you're doing a turnaround, to some degree. On the corporate AI sort of leg of that stool specifically, do you buy into sort of the Jack Dorsey view of every company is essentially trying to build its own little company AGI? I do, but maybe in a slightly different way. I do think the domain specificity matters. So, like, I don't believe in the, "Oh, I'm going to have a single company model that has every piece of data in a single place, with no judgment or lens into anything." The way I think about it more is the virtual employee analogy, so to speak. How do I build an agent or a virtual employee that is exceptional at understanding everything that matters about this customer? That is a well-defined problem with clear boundaries, clear APIs of who needs and depends on that data, and who interacts with that data. That is self-contained. Then there's another agent that can be: "Okay, given all the customers that we have and the problems they have, how do I manage my product roadmap?" That can be a separate agent, but that builds on top of this customer world model. Like a virtual exec team, basically. Exactly. Functional and domain knowledge still matter, right? These things are not going to go away. And I think the way knowledge is structured is still true, right? That doesn't change that much. And—and you— You should separate the agent and the systems that are actually emitting code from the system that is talking to customers, and the system that is reasoning about the conversations with customers and translating them into a product roadmap. These should be three separate things. We're kind of at the Tesla stage for AI where, like, I don't believe in anything that doesn't have real usage. It's like, yeah, I build this great model, and I'm like, "Okay, how many people are using it? Is it actually displacing the need to hire a person inside a company? Is it actually displacing the need to spend literally hours? How many hours is this thing saving?" And I think a lot of times people say, "Well, you know, it's a cool model." And I'm like, "Yeah, but like, that's not going to cut it, right?" Once you have that orientation, like with our customer world model, our client sales team now runs on it. So I know it works. I'm actually having lunch with a customer tomorrow, and I don't know the state of that account as well as I probably should. The customer world model answered the question for me, and I now have a report including things that the team didn't know about, that came through support tickets—like an executive that was traveling had an issue at an airport with their card. So all these things... Total information awareness. Total information awareness, right? That is a well-defined problem, that is working, and I can trust this building block as part of my company model as a whole. And you can have evals on it. I think we should talk about evals; there's a bunch of learnings on this and how to build evals into the fabric of the company. But anyway, I think it's more of like, you have to decompose the problem a little bit. Yeah, my favorite thing about evals is just running cross-modal evals against each other. So one of the things that we're doing that—I, I, I—is related, but I think is really fun, is: how do you have every single human interaction in the company become an eval when you have an AI agent? For example, you have the onboarding onboarding agent doing something, and then you have a team that actually goes in and looks at KYC exceptions that the model can't figure out. How do you make that a breaking change? And, and, okay, like, this this manual interaction will become an eval case. You know, we have an expense agent in Brex. Whenever someone has a conversation with the agents that flags an issue, or or a bug, or or or something that feels like the conversation didn't go as smoothly, that creates a bug. That bug triggers an agent that's going to go in and modify the codebase and the prompts and everything to make that eval pass. And if that doesn't break, then the engineer is going to go in and figure out how to make that pass. Because the goal at the end, I think, is to make the whole thing a self-reinforcing, self-learning system, right? And I think a lot of what I see with with companies is they spend a lot of time getting an agent working, but never thinking how to make the agent improve every day. And I think that's, like, always the biggest unlock. You need a dream cycle. You need a dream cycle that sees everything every night, and then it's like, "Oh, what's going on there? I need to put this over here. What actually happened? Is there a pattern? How do I root-cause this?" So how do you bake the dream cycle into the product, and into the agents, and into the things you ship? My favorite thing right now is I'm building, like, three or four agents for my friends. Oh, interesting. And some of it is, like, this is user research for me for G-Brain, because it's like I have one, it's working really well. I have 350,000 Markdown pages in there now. What a crazy—like, I thought it was this wild, you know, pie-in-the-sky thing, and it's like, it's happening. It's going to happen in our lifetimes. You know, I remember when when Neuralink came out, and I used to think about it. I was like, "I, I don't get it." Like, I was like, "Yeah, of course I get it conceptually, but but why is it a thing?" And then now you use AI and you're like... "Yeah, makes sense. Makes sense. I'm the bottleneck. Typing is so slow." I don't know if you use a lot of dictation; I, I use a lot. My most used developer developer UI right now is like voice memos to OpenClaw. I've said this before—like, it was maybe accidental, but I actually just really love the fact that like Telegram works so well with OpenClaw. It's forced me to just put more stuff—like, make the agent more intelligent so that you can do more stuff via voice memos. You have to sort of fight the natural instinct as like a traditional developer where you're like, "Oh, like, I can't quite do this," or "It doesn't do this, so I need to go, like, build more client, or more UI, or like, more functionality for it." Foxconn. Exactly. No, just let it do what it wants to do, give it some context, and it'll just think about, you know, "Oh, like, actually, what about this?" I think a lot of the work, to your point, is how you organize the context for the model. And, and you can use the model to help, but but that, that is the, that is the bottleneck for for most things. Once you have the context in there, it's actually you can do some pretty crazy stuff. Like my favorite new feature I made for G-Brain: LSD. I saw G-Brain LSD. Yeah, LSD, yeah. Lateral Synaptic Drift. So you just bump the temperature on the search. It's not just that. So you have the vectors, right? And so, you know, if you think about what conventional ideas are, like most people give you like, "Oh, well, this idea with this idea," and it's like kind of like in this cone. LSD mode actually says you cannot combine concepts that are within this cone. They actually must be orthogonal or just, like, ran—like, feeling seemingly random. And then it'll try, like, you know, randomly hundreds of these combinations, and then it'll rank order them into the ones that are actually the most coherent. And then if you do like a hundred of them, actually, like, the top five tend to be banger tweets. You know what's crazy is like Alfred—I didn't tell Alfred explicitly to be dry. When I actually had, like, ChatGPT generate, like, the soul file, it was just like, "Based on everything you know about me, all the interactions here, like, generate, like, a soul.md for, like, my OpenClaw agent." And it was so unerringly, like, accurate about, like, kind of what I would want from, like, an agent, and I was like, "Oh, damn, these models know a lot about us." My OpenClaw got really interesting once I, I, I just ingested my 60-gig Google Takeout. I mean, you have to write a bunch of haiku code to, like, only get the emails that are actually real, but you know there's like—it extracted like 4,000 emails out of like 60 gigs that actually matter. But like, those are like, oh, actually, like a lot of your thinking and, you know, the consequential moments of your life. So, Pedro, thank you so much for being with us. I mean, you're by far one of the most AI-pilled, farthest out on the edge, but also very practical CEOs who is, you know, playing with this stuff and actually building it yourself. What would you say to people watching who are founders, who want to be founders? You know, I, I think that you are sort of the model for the way people should start companies and run them, with AI as your SLM buddy. I really can't stop thinking about the electricity analogy, which is, you know, you're standing—there's a 200-year timeline of human history, there's a point in time where electricity was invented. It sucked in the beginning. You're six months after that point. What do you do differently, knowing everything that would be true about electricity? Knowing that data centers would one day consume electricity, and and even AI, right? Well, you'd do a lot of things differently, I think. So I think that's one of—just, just marveling at the possibility of the exact moment in time we're in now. I think the second is, like, have a post-it on your computer, which is: you wake up, whatever problem you have in your life, why can't you solve it with AI? And just, like, start there. And in 80%, yeah, you can use a chatbot, but the 20% that you can't, figure out why and go build something that makes you solve that problem. Less so because of the—because of the immediate usefulness that solving that thing at scale will have, but because it gives you a texture and a feel for the possibilities of the technology, which are really hard if you're not playing with it every day. And, and maybe the third thing is, like, I think it's like just measure your token consumption and how much you're just pushing the, the limits of the company, and starting at the premise of, like, "Okay, why can't it just be one person? And these things to me are, number one, which problems are worth solving, and two, the sort of the choice thing we talked about. And the second thing is, okay, given that these—given these choices, what are the limitations of an LLM that they still cannot do and I have to go in and do that—those things myself? NVIDIA's Jensen Huang on Building the Dynamo of the Intelligence Age

Sequoia Capital(213.0K)·41:21·38.2K views·2026-06-10
Jensen HuangFounder and CEO, NVIDIA
TL;DRComputing is undergoing a historic shift from retrieving pre-recorded data to generating real-time, highly customized intelligence.
This shift is powered by NVIDIA's "AI factories" and spans a five-layer industrial investment cake that will elevate human labor rather than...

TL;DR

Computing is undergoing a historic shift from retrieving pre-recorded data to generating real-time, highly customized intelligence. This shift is powered by NVIDIA's "AI factories" and spans a five-layer industrial investment cake that will elevate human labor rather than replace it, effectively closing the global technology divide.

The Paradigm Shift: From Retrieval to Generation

  • Reinventing 60 Years of Computing: The modern computer architecture, established 64 years ago with the IBM System 360 in 1964, is a retrieval-based system where files are saved and pulled from data centers.
  • The Shift to Real-Time Generation: Modern computing is shifting to real-time generation. Instead of retrieving pre-recorded files, computers will dynamically generate every pixel, text response, and video based on the user's unique context, query, and history.
  • The Rise of Agentic AI: AI has transitioned from basic translation tools (e.g., text-to-image) to agentic systems capable of multi-step reasoning, planning, and task execution.
      • Collaborative Agent Ecosystems: In the future, the internet will host hundreds of billions of autonomous digital agents working collaboratively across companies, self-driving cars, and robotic manufacturing systems.

The Modern Dynamo: The AI Factory

  • The Energy-to-Intelligence Analogy: 300 years ago, Siemens developed the dynamo to convert physical motion (atoms) into electricity (electrons). NVIDIA's AI factories perform the modern equivalent: they take in electrons and output digital "tokens" of intelligence.
  • Industrial-Scale Hardware:
      • The Server Rack: NVIDIA's primary unit is a $4 million rack containing 72 chips, weighing two tons, and consisting of 1.5 million parts.
      • Gigawatt Factories: Building a gigawatt-scale AI factory requires approximately $50 billion in capital. However, a single factory of this scale is estimated to generate $300 billion to $400 billion in intelligence, offering extremely rapid returns on investment.

The Five-Layer Cake of AI Investment

Jensen Huang outlines a five-layer industrial layout for the current $1 trillion ecosystem investment wave, which is scaling toward a projected $20 trillion annual intelligence industry:

  • Layer 1: Energy: The foundational layer. It represents a massive, multi-generational funding opportunity for sustainable energy providers, including nuclear, wind, solar, and hydrogen.
  • Layer 2: Silicon & Physical Computing: Chips, specialized networking systems, switches, and silicon photonics.
  • Layer 3: Infrastructure: Data center land, power access, shell facilities, and operational services—all of which are currently in critically scarce supply.
  • Layer 4: Models: Large foundation models (e.g., OpenAI, Anthropic). Beyond human language, models are being trained to understand any structured medium, including the physical world (physics and climate), 3D space (robotics), and biology (proteins and genes).
  • Layer 5: Applications: End-user applications utilizing AI to solve vertical industry problems. This layer received $100 billion in venture capital investment last year alone.

Redefining Labor: Tasks vs. Purpose

  • Task Automation vs. Job Purpose: Fears of mass unemployment mistake routine tasks for the broader purpose of a job.
      • The Radiology Example: Despite predictions that computer vision would eliminate radiologists, automating image analysis made radiologists more productive and hospitals more profitable. This led to increased hiring to meet patient demand.
      • The Software Engineering Example: AI automates code generation (typing), but a software engineer's true value lies in system design, innovation, and problem-solving. Consequently, engineering hiring remains at record highs.
  • Job Elevation: Rather than eliminating roles, AI acts as a skill elevator. For example, a furniture salesperson can become an interior designer, or a carpenter can easily generate complex custom kitchen blueprints for clients.
  • Democratizing Computing: Historically, only about 2% of the population knew how to program in languages like C++. Generative AI closes this technology divide by allowing anyone to program a computer using natural human language.

Notable Quotes

"You may or may not lose a job to an AI, but you will absolutely lose a job to someone who uses AI." — Jensen Huang
"We learn the language of human biology. We learn the language of the physical world, physics, climate, weather... The machine that was invented 300 years ago is called the dynamo... atoms in, electrons out; [NVIDIA's factory is] electrons in, numbers out... And those numbers could be reformulated into all kinds of different intelligence." — Jensen Huang
"Coding is not their job, solving problems is their job... There were engineers before software coding, I promise you that, there will be engineers after. AI is not going to eliminate jobs, AI is going to elevate your job." — Jensen Huang
Thank you so much, Jensen. So we are in the middle of a massive AI revolution. It is probably bigger and faster than even the Industrial Revolution. And you have called out what's happening right now as the largest infrastructure build-out in human history. At the center of that build-out is the AI factory. And the company enabling all of that is NVIDIA. Can you tell us what is an AI factory and why is it the best investment for any enterprise in the next decade? Okay, so you could, you could understand AI in a, in a particular number of ways. The way that you understand AI probably most is through a chatbot, through a web browser. You're interacting with it, you give it a prompt, it says something back to you. And even those of you who have been using AI for some time, you've seen in the last couple, two, three years a very significant evolution, an improvement in the capabilities of AI. Two years ago you heard about ChatGPT. And ChatGPT basically is a computer software that understands the input you give it. It can perceive, understand information, and it can translate and generate the information into something else. Okay, so you can give it a prompt and you can say, "Here's this PDF I gave you, I would like you now to summarize it." It went from text to text. You could also tell it, "Here's a PDF I gave you, I would like you now to generate an image of that story." It goes text to image. You could go from image to text, meaning you could give it a picture and you can ask what's happening inside this picture. It goes image to text. Does that make sense? Anything to anything else. And AI in the last—two years ago, two years ago—was largely able to do this translation. We call it generation, generative models. Okay, generative AI. But the, the thing that is a very big deal inside that word generative AI is in order to do something even more valuable than generation, perception, understanding, and generating is thinking. Well, you can't think if you don't generate words. And so, the—first, the foundation of generative AI gave us the ability to generate internal thoughts. Thinking, reasoning, step-by-step reasoning, problem-solving. It also allowed us to do another thing that is now very important, which is generate intelligence to control something else, to generate control to use a tool. Does that make sense? To use a browser, use a spreadsheet, use Photoshop, use PowerPoint, use something. Use AutoCAD. Use another tool. Now that tool today is digital, but someday that tool will be mechanical. So if I generate a command to a mechanical system, that would be called robotics. If I generate commands for a machine with a steering wheel, that would be called self-driving cars. Reason and do work. Now two years ago, when AI was able to understand you and generate information, that was interesting, novel, a little cute. Valuable meaning it can generate information, it can generate useful work, and it can be paid for it. We're basically paying AI a lot of money today, making it the fastest-growing software business in the history of mankind. Because now it's doing useful work and we can pay them to do it. That's an interesting conversation. But you should go upstream, meaning: industrially, what does that mean? And so the first thing you realize is this. Go back to first principles. I told you just now that AI is software and it's being produced by a computer. Now what happened to the computer that has made it possible to do this? Well, the big idea is about—if you think about the computer as we know it today, it really emerged about 64 years ago. IBM System 360 was the biggest announcement of computing, and 64 years ago IBM was the most valuable company in the world, okay? And they created the modern understanding of computers. Everything that we can describe about a computer was really described in 1964. For 60—for 40 years, largely it has remained the same. And what happened was, what happened was that info—that form of computing is called pre-recording. You write down your story, you save it to a file. You write a program by hand, you save it to a file. You take a picture, you save it to a file. You record music, you save it to a file. You make a video right now—we're on streaming right now—but somebody's going to record it, you're going to save it to a file. And when you want to use it later, you retrieve it from the disk drive. Does that make sense? And the retrieval process is done intelligently. So that's why everybody's retrieval of a news story is a little bit different. It's called a recommender system. But basically, computers as we know them today are retrieval, retrieval-based systems. Which is the reason why these buildings are called data centers. They store data. Notice they don't call them computer centers, because they're not doing much computing; they just store data that you retrieve based on what you touch on your phone. Well, what happened now? I give it a new prompt, that's called a query. Between the context and the query, it will think—understand it first, reason about it, and it will produce an output based on that context and that query, based on the circumstance. Everything I'm saying to you right now is being produced in real-time. And it's because my explanation is based on the fact that I realize all of you come from 60 different countries, 128 different families, you all have many different backgrounds—some of you probably came from the computer industry, most of you probably did not.
