Jensen Huang on Vision, Risk, and the GPU | Only In America

Hoover Institution(1.2M)·39:38·359.5K views·2026-06-10
Jensen HuangFounder and CEO, NVIDIA
TL;DRNVIDIA founder and CEO Jensen Huang shares his journey from a nine-year-old immigrant to the head of a multi-trillion-dollar company, illustrating how America’s unique foundations of freedom, risk-tolerance, and first-principles thinking made the…
accelerated computing...

TL;DR

NVIDIA founder and CEO Jensen Huang shares his journey from a nine-year-old immigrant to the head of a multi-trillion-dollar company, illustrating how America’s unique foundations of freedom, risk-tolerance, and first-principles thinking made the accelerated computing and artificial intelligence revolutions possible.

The Immigrant Journey and the "Only in America" Spirit

  • Fleeing Instability: Born in Taiwan, Huang moved to Thailand before being sent to the United States in 1973 at age nine alongside his ten-year-old brother to escape political unrest.
  • A Kentucky Education: The brothers attended a remote boarding school in Oneida, Kentucky (population ~600). As one of the first Chinese students in the town, Huang navigated cultural barriers and biases while embracing American mainstays like youth sports and fast food.
  • Parental Sacrifice: Huang's parents eventually joined them in Tacoma, Washington, starting with nothing. His mother worked as a maid and his father as an engineer, sacrificing their savings to provide their children with educational opportunities.
  • The Immigrant Mindset: Huang attributes his career-long drive to the immigrant experience, characterized by having low expectations, deep gratitude, high dreams, and a constant, positive desperation to succeed.

Education, AMD, and the Silicon Valley "Soup"

  • Academic Beginnings: Huang studied engineering at Oregon State University, where he met his classmate and future wife, Lori.
  • The Stanford Pipeline: After graduating, Huang took a job at Advanced Micro Devices (AMD). The company sponsored his Master’s degree at Stanford University, allowing him to work and study simultaneously over an eight-year period.
  • The Ecosystem Advantage: Huang describes Silicon Valley and Stanford as an interconnected "soup" of ideas, technology, and business strategies that fundamentally shaped his perspective on the industrial applications of computer science.

The Founding of NVIDIA and the GPU Revolution

  • Challenging the CPU Hegemony: Founded in 1993 by Huang, Chris Malachowsky, and Curtis Priem, NVIDIA challenged the prevailing tech wisdom that the CPU (general-purpose computing) was the only necessary processor.
  • Solving the Chicken-and-Egg Problem:
      • Market Entry: To convince developers to write software for a completely new chip architecture, NVIDIA targeted 3D graphics for video games—a high-volume, computationally demanding market.
      • Early Breakthroughs: This focus led to programmable graphics processors like the GeForce 3, which contained 57 million transistors (more than Pentium III and IV processors combined).
  • The Virtual Headquarters: Reflecting its engineering culture, NVIDIA’s modern campus building in Silicon Valley was designed and optimized entirely in a supercomputer simulation before construction to maximize energy and sunlight efficiency.

Scaling AI and the National Strategic Imperative

  • First-Principles Scaling: NVIDIA realized that graphics processing is fundamentally a simulation of the physical world executed in parallel. This realization allowed them to expand GPU architecture into scientific simulation (seismic processing, molecular dynamics) and eventually deep learning.
  • A Decade of Grit: NVIDIA funded and developed deep learning capabilities for ten years before the market paid attention, driven entirely by internal belief and first-principles reasoning.
  • The Five-Layer AI Cake: Huang breaks down the AI ecosystem into five critical tiers:

1. *Energy, Power, and Shell:* The basic physical infrastructure.

2. *Silicon:* The specialized chips (NVIDIA's core domain).

3. *Infrastructure:* Cloud hosting and networking services.

4. *Models:* Large language models and foundational software.

5. *Applications:* Practical deployments in healthcare, defense, cybersecurity, and manufacturing.

  • National Policy Warning: Huang warns that while the U.S. leads in AI, leadership can quickly shift during technology inflections. Policymakers must avoid hindering the top "application layer," as the nation that best implements AI applications will ultimately exploit this industrial revolution.

Notable Quotes

"The entrepreneurial spirit, the immigrant spirit is rather similar actually... If you don't work hard every single day, you will perish. I am certain my feelings about NVIDIA and my constant desperation to do better is exactly the same feeling my parents had." — Jensen Huang
"During technology change, during world change is the only opportunity for greatness. Status quo... it's really hard to make a difference." — Jensen Huang
"I can't imagine another place where this is possible. NVIDIA, genuinely, is an only in America story. In a lot of ways, I'm an only in America story." — Jensen Huang
My parents had nothing, sold everything, came to America. That was the beginning. Along the way, the jobs I had, the schools I went to, the opportunities I had led to here. And here we are, NVIDIA being the most consequential technology company in the world. This can't possibly happen anywhere else. America leads the world in technological innovation. This innovation, taking place all around us in Silicon Valley, is possible here because of our American freedoms. But to take advantage of the opportunities that freedom affords us, it requires taking risk and facing the unknown with courage and determination. Jensen Huang, the founding CEO of NVIDIA, had a vision, took significant risk, and now leads an iconic American business driving change at the heart of global technology. His journey as an immigrant is fascinating, and it has so much to tell us about our country and what makes it exceptional. America presented the opportunities that enabled Jensen's risk-taking to change the world. Now, for Only in America, my conversation with Jensen Huang. The amazing Condi Rice. I'm in uniform. How are you? Good to see you. Nice to see you. Welcome. Thank you. What do you think about our new building? I think this is amazing. This is, this is a building that is only possible with science and a supercomputer. This was completely built in a simulation first. You're kidding. This building is designed so that we make maximum use of this incredible space called California. And so we decided let's use the space for the beautiful sunlight and the weather. And this entire space is very energy efficient. So we use the maximum amount of sunlight. And that's why there are light wells on the roof. But when you have light wells in the roof, it is extremely difficult to remove the heat. And so notice where the triangles are—the light wells are only where they need to be. I see. Uh-huh. And the roof looks flat to us from here, but is undulated to track the sun. So it lets sun in just enough, but doesn't let any more sunlight in than is needed. Otherwise we'll have to remove the heat. It would be so hot. So it's completely efficient then? Completely efficient. We simulated this building every single hour of the day through a simulation of the entire year. And we moved everything until it was perfect. Perfectly harmonious mathematically. Well, I would expect that of you, Jensen. That's an engineer trying to be an architect. But I like it. I like it. Yeah. And the undulation, this one creates a baseball cap. And the reason for that is because you could see during, in the afternoon when the sun's about to set, it comes right into the building. And so I sloped the walls to move the offices further back in. And so this is the baseball cap of the building. And it fits right into the beautiful design. If you make me look too put together, everybody knows it's fake. I've got to be a little wiki-wonky. Something's got to be a little off. There it is. Yeah. Can we still call you Madam Secretary? No, you're going to call me Condi. I tell, I tell people Madam Secretary was a while ago. Dr. Rice was my father. And Professor Rice is with my students. So I think that leaves us with Condi. My goodness. That's a lot of good titles. Jensen Huang, thank you. Thank you for having me here at this beautiful NVIDIA campus. You and I have known each other for a very long time. And I have a lot of ties, Stanford ties and the like. So I'm really looking forward to our conversation. But let me start with this young kid. What, nine? Uh-huh. Comes to the United States of America. America is in many ways one big immigrant story. A lot of very different stories, but one big story of people who come seeking a better life, people who believe that their children are going to do better. So talk about arriving here. It couldn't have been, couldn't have been easy. I was born in Taiwan. When I was five, my, my father got a job in Thailand to help start an oil refinery. And so we moved to Thailand. We were there for about four years. In 1973, there was a coup, as it happens every now and then. And my parents thought it was unsafe for us to be there. My older brother was one year older than me. He was 10, I was nine. My parents wanted to send us out as soon as possible. And so they got our uncle who lives in Tacoma, Washington to take us for a little while. My first impression was, was I've never stood in a house with carpets before. And it was the strangest feeling. I felt like I was walking on my bed with my shoes on. And just everything, from cereal and the morning television, Speed Racer, and in the afternoon, Partridge Family. You know, all the candy, the Snickers bar, everything was—I couldn't imagine what this amazing country was. And everything was so beautiful. The cars, everything was just incredible. And we were in Tacoma, Washington for about three months. And then they sent us to the most affordable, accessible boarding school in America because my parents couldn't afford much. A wonderful school. They welcome students of all backgrounds, many from, from difficult homes, some international kids. And that's how we were able to come. They sponsored us and allowed us to come to the United States and stay in that boarding school. And I was there for about two years in Oneida, Kentucky. I have never heard of Oneida, Kentucky until this particular moment. If you look, if you look at Oneida, Kentucky in Google Maps, the, the beautiful thing is there's nothing around it. Uh-huh. It's just one little dot. I think when I was there, it was probably population 600. It's probably population 600 now. Beautiful, incredible. I had a wonderful, wonderful childhood there. It was very difficult and very scary coming to the United States by yourself. But it's amazing what my older brother did, frankly. I just followed him around. He was 10 years old. Could you imagine a 10-year-old bringing along a nine-year-old, traveled all the way from Thailand, never been to the United States, landed in Tacoma, Washington, and went to, went to Kentucky, but layover in Chicago? Oh my goodness. The airport, Chicago airport is gigantic. It is. And so we had to go find a connecting flight and he, you know, a 10-year-old did all that by himself. So my older brother's incredible. That's amazing. So this was risky in a lot of ways, but your parents felt that the greater risk would be to stay where you were. How were you treated? Were people friendly? Were they surprised? How, how did that part of it go? You know, 1973 United States, in Kentucky, there were still biases and nobody had, the school and everything, seen a Chinese kid before. Right. Wonderful kids there, but, but none of them had ever seen a Chinese before. Uh-huh. And so there were all of the things that, that comes along with, you know, being a stranger in a town where nobody's ever seen something, someone like you before. And so there's a lot of bias and of course things like that. But kids are kids. And so for us at the time, you know, the United States was amazing. And I was part of the swim team and I was part of the soccer team and the food was interesting—sausage and gravy, who wouldn't like that? You know, hamburgers. And after the swim meet, the coach just took us to the most amazing restaurant in the world. And the food came in boxes and the menu was all lit up and we sat, we sat in a restaurant that seemed like a spaceship. And it was McDonald's. McDonald's, right. You know? And so, so I think it's really all about expectations in life. You know, when you came from an even more difficult circumstance. That's the thing about immigrants is that, that when you come to, when you come to America, you came because of choice. You wanted to be here. Uh-huh. My, my dad sent us here, but in fact, when I was four years old, he had the opportunity to come to be trained in the United States. And turned out the place he came to was New York. Oh, my. Could you imagine to come to the United States and it's New York City, from Taiwan in the 60s? And so he, he always said that he wanted his family to eventually come to America, to come to this incredible place. So the thing that's really great about immigrants is you came with very little expectation, you came with great hopes and dreams, and you appreciate everything. You come with a sense of gratitude for where you are and the new opportunities. Exactly. And and this miracle that you're witnessing. I remember very clearly what it's like to see America for the first time. This miracle and all these people and how welcoming they are. To see it through those eyes. But then of course you go from this little kid in Kentucky, you're on the swim team, soccer. Sounds like you were doing okay and people like you. I was a good athlete. Yeah, I was a good athlete. And so we met our parents back in Tacoma, Washington. They, they had nothing. And literally they put all their belongings on the plane. So they had nothing and they came with, you know, literally a suitcase. I mean that story, the immigrant story. And they saved their way to, to build a life for their kids. My mom was a maid at a Catholic school, and my father was an engineer. They saved everything they had. My dad bought a green truck, a, a van. And there were no, there were no seats in it. So he put a carpet in the back and put some milk crates. We got into, into that green van and he drove us all the way from Oregon down to LA so that we could go see Disneyland. And that was the one vacation of our family. How did you end up going on to college and then Stanford, and how does working at Denny's, which we have to cover at some point, fit in? I know, I, I must be Denny's best ambassador. Yeah, I think you are, definitely. You know, I, I love Denny's so much. It never occurred to me to be able to afford or, or you know, go anywhere for college. And so, so it was, it was always just my plan that I would grow up in Oregon. And I loved math, I loved science. And when you're in high school and you love math and science, you're, you're only going to have three other friends who also love math and science. Right. And so the two or three friends of ours, we were all in a club. So we were in a math club, we were also in the science club, and then we were in the computer club. All together. Right. And so and then afterwards, after clubbing, we would go, we would go play, played arcade games, you know, played pinball or go play at arcade games. And that was, that was growing up. My best friend, Dean Verhoeven, he, he said, "You know, I'm just going to go to Oregon State University. My, my brother went there, my parents went there," and I said that sounds great. Oregon State had a good engineering program. That was just good happenstance. I loved the school and it turned out that there was another kid there, Lori, and she was one of three girls in a, in a class of 250 other boys. Right. Right. And she was, you know, she was—I was the youngest kid in school. I was only 16. And she was a year and a half older than me, and I was, I was determined to, to go, you know, meet her. And so I, you know, statistically we weeded out everybody by getting myself maneuvered into her lab class. So now I've reduced the population from 250, competition from 250 to four. That's quite clever. That was very clever. Oh, that was very strategic even when I was a kid. And well, I knew odds. And then, and then, you know, I gave her the ultimate pickup line. I asked her if she wanted to see my homework. Uh-huh. And so that, that, that was so smooth and that was really smooth of you to do. I cinched it. And so, so so we've been together ever since. That's great. So that's how you met. Yeah, we did. Yeah, we met in college. So you're in Corvallis, Oregon State. You are doing well in school. How does Stanford come onto your radar screen? I always wanted to go to a great school. You know, I always imagined, you know, getting a master's and but I never imagined that I could afford it. And so during Oregon State, the recruiters came from Silicon Valley. And so I took a job at AMD. And that was a really good choice because the work was really interesting, and I wrote really great colleagues. And they had this, this program where you could go to Stanford and work at AMD and they pay for everything. I see. And so and so it was an incredible thing. So when I was being, when they were pitching the offer to me, I said, I said, "Hang on a second. I can, I can work at AMD, you can pay me a great salary, and you're going to pay me to go to Stanford at the same time?" And they said, "Yep." You said that sounds good. And that sounds like a dream come true. So I took that job. And then of course a year later Lori graduated, we got married. And then life started. So I took some classes, took some time off, took some classes, took some time off. And altogether it must have taken me some eight years to graduate. I'm probably the longest-running student at Stanford. Nobody paid Stanford more for the education. And along the way Spencer and Madison came and along the way I founded NVIDIA. And literally during my entire tenure, you know, during that time of, of being at Stanford, my life happened. Yeah. When you're, when you're going to school, you think that the schoolwork is awfully academic. And the reason for that is because you're not sure whether there's any purpose and any benefit in learning this. But the benefit of working and going to school at the same time, especially Stanford, I could see so much of the principles being taught and how important it is in everything that I do today. And so it was a great privilege to have all of that happening to me at the same time. My family, my kids, my company, Stanford, you know, working, all of it kind of in one giant soup. People talk about Silicon Valley and the ecosystem. But you just used a really great term. It is really kind of a big soup, isn't it? And you meet very interesting people and they change your thinking about something and that leads to something. It sounds like Stanford was a place like that for you. It completely shaped my attitude and my perspective about computer science and its impact to industry. Right. And its, and industrial strategies. That entire intersection between technology, applications, the fundamental science, and the strategies of computer science really for me formulated during that time. Right. And it was really cool. Now talk about the founding of NVIDIA. You know, the early-day founder stories are really fascinating because you have to take, you have to take a chance. You take a risk. You, you don't know if it's going to work out. But, but what was the kernel that made you and your co-founders think, "You know, maybe we have something here?" During that time, it was at the beginning of the PC revolution. It was the beginning of really Moore's law, the beginning of the CPU era. This incredible technology engine that really changed everything. All of, all of that in Silicon Valley was all about CPUs. Everything was about general-purpose computing. Everything was about CPUs. Everything was about Moore's law. Everything was about the PC. And there were two simultaneous ideas that, that Chris, Curtis, and I were considering. And one idea of course is every application that's interesting and meaningful, can it be run on a CPU? And and we, we believe that, that there were so many interesting problems to solve, whether it's real-time computer graphics, which is at the time one of the hardest problems to do in computer science, or simulation or other things beyond that. A normal computer like a CPU can't possibly be the right format. And so we imagined that there would be a way to accelerate the CPU, offload the work that is not suitable for general-purpose things. It's kind of like in your house you only have one tool, one tool in the kitchen, or you go into your garage there's only one tool in your garage. You come to your company there's only one tool. You know, there's, there's the right tool for the right job. And we, we believe that there's, there is a different tool that could augment the CPU and make that computer essentially a supercomputer. Right. And so that was one idea. The second idea was, was how do we even get this company started? The problem is the CPU at this point, general-purpose computing as of today is probably about 64 years old. Uh-huh. And so the nature of the computer industry is that if you create an architecture that produces benefits to applications, the installed base goes up, the sales goes up, applications use more of it. This positive virtual cycle would cause the two to reinforce itself. And so in fact, over the course of last 64 years, there's so many applications are built on top of the CPU. How do you even cause the application developers to consider another architecture? Another architecture, right. That chicken and egg problem is incredibly hard to solve and has never been solved. In fact, there's, aside from NVIDIA today, really everything else runs on CPUs. Right. And so the idea that we would get this new architecture to be adopted by developers was incredibly hard. And so the question is, what's the first application? Right. And so which brought NVIDIA to its second idea, which was maybe the first application is computer graphics. Uh-huh. But the problem is computer graphics was such a small market at the time. Right. It was just Silicon Graphics. Right, right. And Silicon Graphics was such a, it was a large company, but by the standards of computer architectures, very, very small. Right. And so the question is how do we find an application that would on the one hand need this architecture, on the other hand be sufficiently high volume to cause this architecture to proliferate? Right, right. And so we thought maybe 3D graphics for video games. Computer games are not only fun, but they perhaps more than any other application push the edge of computing power. Most PC upgrades for consumers are driven not by the grownups in the house, but by the kids who want a more powerful gaming machine. All right, so you've got the brand new GeForce3 graphics card here. That's exactly right. The GeForce3 is the latest generation graphics processor from NVIDIA. So this product actually includes a 57 million transistor graphics processor. To put that into perspective, that's more transistors than a Pentium 4 plus a Pentium 3 put together. I just described a business plan. Yeah. That is impossible to fund. And it was, I still remember explaining it and everybody's going, "Yeah, but you know, this and that, this requires this be solved, you have multiple chicken and eggs." And yet Silicon Valley right here, Sand Hill Road, would finance me. Sequoia Capital and Sutter Hill, right, would be investors in this company. And that, that we would be able to attract the brightest minds, the brightest computer scientists in the world to come work here. Yeah. But we were just determined that on first-principled basis, the general-purpose computer cannot possibly be the only computing platform. Right. And that there were too many interesting problems that we could solve if we were to introduce this new idea. What's key about this is it's programmable. That's exactly right. For the first time, the graphics processor is as programmable as the CPU. We have an instruction set, just like the Intel processors have an instruction set that you can program Microsoft Word or Windows with, we now have an instruction set that the game programmers can program to create special effects on our processor. So you literally were swimming upstream on this. People you were trying to convince. For 30 years. For 30 years. Yeah. But then something happens and the problem that you've solved, it turns out, has many other applications. NVIDIA! The best way to play the netbook explosion. That's right. N-V-D-A. First of all, the company understands the netbook opportunity. Quote, "There's more and more people proving that they want to be able to take their computer with them." NVIDIA came to us many months ago and talked to us about an amazing graphics part that they wanted to build. And we said this is fantastic, but we'd like to use it in a notebook. Can we work together on this? And we've been working together with NVIDIA for many months. And they've created something really great. I remember listening to you at Stanford at a talk and you said, we just kept trying to solve hard problems. Computer graphics is basically a simulation of the world. Right. It's a simulation problem. In a lot of ways artificial intelligence is a simulation of the mind, simulation of the brain. And so the computation of simulation can be done not completely but largely in parallel. And so the, the architecture, the processor that you want to use for simulation versus the architecture used for task execution. A recipe is step one, step two, step three. In the case of simulation, the world is happening concurrently and in parallel all the same time right now. Right. And so on that first principle, you would think that simulation of the world, whether it's quantum, Newtonian, or otherwise, should be something that is a different architecture than recipe execution and instruction execution. And so that's the big, big idea. Now the question then is how do you manifest that idea in technology? Number one. Number two, how do you go find applications for it? And so we found of course the first application, computer graphics. The second application was seismic processing or inverse physics, CT reconstruction, ultrasound, seismic, very similar problems. The next problem we found was molecular dynamics, Newtonian physics. And, you know, on and on and on and we just kept finding one problem after another. And then one day some researchers, one at Stanford, Andrew Ng, Geoff Hinton at the University of Toronto, Yann LeCun over at New York University. They were all trying to solve for a similar problem, which is deep learning. Uh-huh. And they reached out to us and I, you know, being alert, I realized that this is a problem that we could really make a contribution to. And I was happy to help. And and because of the work that we did together, it achieved a level of computer vision capability that no one had ever imagined. And because of its success, it triggered even further introspection. Why does it work? What else can it do? How far can it go? What is the implication to computer science? What is the implication to the whole industry that we're built on? And so step by step by step, we broke everything down to its first principles and then we rebuilt back where NVIDIA is going to be in that world, how do we apply all of the techniques that we know, how do we navigate step by step so that on the one hand we could pursue this unknown future. And so all of this is about reasoning, vision, strategy, discipline, patience, belief, at the core of grit. We suffered our way here. Right. You know, we suffered every single step of the way, we suffered our way here because nobody believed in it. Right. So we had the benefit of building all of this for a decade before anybody even paid attention. The hard part of course is that you're endeavoring something that has no positive feedback, no external motivation. How do you stay motivated and how do your employees stay motivated? Because there was this period of time where NVIDIA was, it was not on the tip of the tongue of anybody here in the valley. All kinds of other things were going on. Yeah. How did you stay—not just you stay focused, but how did you keep a workforce believing and people believing, engineers believing that this was—it must have been a tough inspirational speech at some time. How do you do that? First of all, you, we have to believe in what we're doing. We have to go back to our core values, as you know, in almost everything great that's done, you have to go back to your core foundations. You have to demonstrate that you, you are determined to go pursue that, that you see that future in your mind's eye even though nobody else can. You see that future in your mind's eye. You have to tell the story so that everybody else could see it in their mind's eye. And you have to believe it yourself. You could say that about America, you could say that about Stanford, you could say that about NVIDIA. America has core foundations. You know, we spoke a little bit about it. The American Dream has a core foundation. There's a reason why it exists, there's a purpose that drives it, there's a promise of it. There are pillars that keep it up. Your purpose, if you decide to go into medicine, is to care for people. It's different than studying a radiology scan. I think that one of the challenges is to define what is AI. Mm-hmm. That is probably one of the most—there's a lot of different ways to think about the technology, but one way to think about it is just like a five-layer cake and we have to win every single layer. Mm-hmm. The first layer is just energy. Energy, land, power, and shell. Yeah. First layer. Second layer is the chips layer where I'm at. But the third layer is the infrastructure layer which is kind of like cloud services. The next layer above that is the AI model layer. This is where everybody talks. Yes. Right. But it's not the only layer. Everything, everything about that layer is very important. But ultimately the most important layer to our nation is the layer above that, which is the application layer. Right. Using AI for healthcare, using AI for military applications, using for defense, for cybersecurity, using AI for transportation, for manufacturing. That is what's going to drive our industry forward. But I do think that our nation also has to be very alert that this is a very important time. And although we are ahead, although we are the world leader, during an inflection in technology is exactly when leadership can change. And we have to make sure that as we come up with policies, we don't hinder the most important layer, which is the highest layer, the application layer. Right. That is the layer that whoever advances that layer most will exploit this industrial revolution the most. Somebody comes here from Taiwan, they're nine, they end up in Kentucky of all places. Yeah. NVIDIA Jensen Huang 젠슨 황 CEO 서울대학교 방문 스피치

서울대학교AI연구원(12.3K)·36:34·3.7K views·2026-06-10
TL;DRSpeaking at Seoul National University (SNU), NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang outlined the shift from traditional software coding to "agentic" AI and robotics, highlighting Korea’s unique positioning to lead this revolution, while previewing the upcoming…
Fall 2026 launch of the "RTX...

TL;DR

Speaking at Seoul National University (SNU), NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang outlined the shift from traditional software coding to "agentic" AI and robotics, highlighting Korea’s unique positioning to lead this revolution, while previewing the upcoming Fall 2026 launch of the "RTX Spark" Windows laptop powered by the "N1X" chip.

The Paradigm Shift: From Software Coding to Agentic AI

  • The New AI Formula: The computing paradigm has shifted. While the previous generation relied on the formula of code + operating system = application, the new era operates on LLM + harness = AI.
  • Translating Legacy Systems to AI: Traditional operating system concepts have re-emerged under new names:
      • OS has evolved into the harness (orchestration, scheduling, and I/O).
      • Memory Management is now KV Cache.
  • Agentic Capabilities: AI agents are evolving from basic models to systems capable of long-term memory, reasoning, planning, and tool use (such as Excel, AutoCAD, and Photoshop). By learning from "skills files" (e.g., robotic manuals), agents can continuously improve and write new memories to become smarter with use.
  • Physical AI: Digital AI agents placed inside physical bodies represent the transition into the robotics era, where robots automatically learn how to control motors and sensors by reading digital manuals.

Korea’s Competitive Edge in Physical AI and Robotics

  • Uniquely Integrated Industry: Jensen Huang noted that Korea is one of the very few countries to simultaneously excel in all four pillars required for the robotics era: electronics, mobile cloud, mechanical engineering/manufacturing, and AI.
  • The Return of Mechanical Engineering: While the last 40 years favored software engineers, physical AI and robotics will restore mechanical engineering to the forefront.
  • The Role of AI Physics: AI is transitioning from digital tasks to emulating physical properties. AI physics enables massive-scale physical simulations, such as up-resolution weather forecasting down to a 0.5-kilometer scale using a single GPU—a task previously thought to require supercomputers a billion times larger.

NVIDIA's Philosophy of Non-Consensus Innovation

  • Building Without a Market: True innovation requires building for markets that do not yet exist. Jensen Huang detailed how NVIDIA’s landmark systems were built without customer requests:
      • DGX-1: Built without a customer base, the first unit shipped outside of NVIDIA was gifted to OpenAI in 2016 to train GPT.
      • Xavier/Orin: Designed eight years ago as the world's first robotics processors when there was no active market demand.
  • First-Principles Thinking: Innovators must reject copying trends, instead reasoning about the future from first principles and maintaining belief in technologies even through early commercial failures.

Product Preview: The N1X Chip and RTX Spark Laptop

  • Reinventing the PC: To modernize the 40-year-old personal computer architecture, NVIDIA partnered with Microsoft's Satya Nadella to co-design a new Windows-based system.
  • The N1X Processor: The core engine of this new PC architecture is the code-named "N1X" processor, a highly complex chip that took three years to design and five years in conceptual planning.
  • The RTX Spark: Launching in Fall 2026, the RTX Spark laptop will be the commercial release of the N1X architecture. Key features include:
      • The first Windows laptop featuring native CUDA capability.
      • The world's first native operating system designed specifically for agentic systems.
      • High-performance capabilities for gaming, developer workflows, and local AI agent execution.

Notable Quotes

"In my generation, we had to write code. We typed the code into a computer... In your time, it's large language model, LLM, plus harness equals AI." — Jensen Huang
"For the last 40 years, it was the time for software engineers. Now it's time for mechanical engineers again." — Jensen Huang
"To do something no one asked, to build for a market that doesn't exist, and to keep believing in it even though in the beginning it was a failure... that is in fact precisely the feelings of an innovator." — Jensen Huang

Senators John Fetterman and Dave McCormick: Bipartisanship, Money in DC, Datacenters, Graham Platner

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·43:23·23.4K views·2026-06-10
John FettermanUS Senator, Pennsylvania Dave McCormickUS Senator, Pennsylvania
TL;DRPennsylvania Senators John Fetterman (D) and Dave McCormick (R) advocate for a pragmatic, bipartisan approach to national governance, emphasizing that preserving the Senate filibuster and expanding AI and energy infrastructure are critical to keeping America competitive against China.

TL;DR

Pennsylvania Senators John Fetterman (D) and Dave McCormick (R) advocate for a pragmatic, bipartisan approach to national governance, emphasizing that preserving the Senate filibuster and expanding AI and energy infrastructure are critical to keeping America competitive against China. They warn that extreme political polarization and ideological tribalism threaten economic mobility and weaken the nation's global standing.

Bipartisanship, Coalitions, and Defense of the Filibuster

  • Pennsylvania as a National Microcosm: Both senators highlight Pennsylvania’s role as a political bellwether where successful politicians must build broad, diverse coalitions across rural and urban divides.
    • Coalition Dynamics: Senator McCormick notes that despite national union endorsements of Democrats, two-thirds of the rank-and-file working class (electricians, steamfitters, pipefitters) in PA voted for him, mirroring the coalition that supported both Senator Fetterman and Donald Trump.
    • Diverse Turnout: This winning coalition increasingly includes high Latino turnout and the highest African-American voter turnout in Pennsylvania in 30 years.
  • The Necessity of the Filibuster: Senator Fetterman issued a stark, public reversal on his previous stance regarding the Senate filibuster.
    • Admitting Error: Fetterman acknowledged that he and the wider Democratic Party were wrong to demand the elimination of the filibuster in 2020 and 2022.
    • Protecting Governance: Both senators agree that the filibuster is a crucial "last line of defense" against total tribalism, forcing the majority party to negotiate and preserving minority rights in governance.

AI, Energy Infrastructure, and the Blue-Collar Boom

  • The Energy-AI Nexus: The senators argue that the global AI race cannot be won without a massive expansion of domestic energy production.
    • The Energy and Innovation Summit: Hosted by McCormick and attended by Fetterman and Governor Josh Shapiro, the summit secured $92 billion in committed investments.
    • Homer City Transition: A prime example of this boom is the Homer City coal plant conversion, transitioning to natural gas to yield 4.4 gigawatts of power—3.4 gigawatts of which will power a new data center complex, with 1 gigawatt returning to the public grid to help lower consumer energy prices.
  • Pushback Against Data Center Moratoriums: Fetterman strongly opposes proposals within the Democratic Party to place a moratorium on data centers.
    • A "China-First" Policy: Fetterman explicitly labels anti-data center policies as "China-first," warning that if the U.S. does not build this technological infrastructure, China will. He notes that some domestic opposition is influenced by CCP-aligned misinformation campaigns.
  • Economic Rebirth via Skilled Trades: The expansion of data centers and natural gas fracking has triggered a blue-collar boom in Pennsylvania.
    • High-Wage Opportunities: Seasoned welders and electricians in their late teens and early 20s are starting at salaries exceeding $100,000 a year.
    • Logistics Multiplier: The trucking and logistics industries estimate that approximately two logistics jobs are created for every single direct job inside a data center.

Rejecting Tribalism, Extremism, and "Rage Bait"

  • The Trap of "Trump Derangement Syndrome" (TDS): Fetterman criticizes his party's tendency to define its platform entirely in opposition to Donald Trump.
    • Distracted Governance: Fetterman argues that reflexively opposing anything Trump supports—humorously noting that "if Trump comes out for ice cream and lazy Sundays, we're gonna hate that shit"—distracts from critical global issues, such as stopping Iran from acquiring a nuclear weapon.
  • The Rise of Extremist Candidates: The discussion highlighted the baffling viability of highly controversial candidates, such as Maine's Graham Platner, despite severe personal and political scandals (including a Nazi tattoo and sexting controversies).
    • Ideological Drift: McCormick and Fetterman express deep concern over mainstream Democratic leaders (such as Chuck Schumer and Elizabeth Warren) campaigning for far-left candidates, validating a harmful drift toward socialism, Marxism, and borderline anti-Semitism.
  • Political Self-Preservation: Fetterman states he is prepared to face a primary challenge from the progressive wing of his party rather than compromise his centrist, pro-capitalist, and pro-Israel beliefs. He maintains a 93% voting record with the Democratic line but refuses to engage in "rage bait" politics.

Tackling the K-Shaped Economy and Capitalist Values

  • Addressing Wealth Concentration: With Pennsylvania's median income sitting at $52,000, McCormick stresses that working-class families feel left behind by globalization and the asset-heavy gains of the top 10% over the last decade.
  • Private-Sector and Localized Solutions: McCormick advocates for non-governmental wealth redistribution incentives:
    • Invest in America Accounts: Encouraging private investment and generational wealth-building.
    • School Choice: Championing the Working Families Tax Cut Act, allowing individuals to redirect up to $1,700 annually toward local school choice initiatives.
  • A Defense of Capitalism: Fetterman strongly defends capitalism as the only economic system proven to elevate human standards of living globally, flatly rejecting the rise of self-described socialist and communist candidates in local elections. He also defends job-creating billionaires (citing Jeff Bezos) from partisan attacks by politicians who have never run a business.
  • The Absurdity of Campaign Spending: McCormick's recent Senate race cost a combined $500 million ($200M raised on his side, $300M on the opposition), while Fetterman's 2022 race cost $330 million. Both agree that spending hundreds of millions of dollars solely to destroy reputations is an indictment of modern American politics.

Notable Quotes

"The entire Democratic Party, including myself, we were so wrong about the filibuster... thank God we had people that stood there, whether it's Senator Manchin or Senator Sinema. History has vindicated their wisdom." — Senator John Fetterman (D-PA)
"We can't win the AI race without energy... In Pennsylvania, we've been losing people for 20 years, we can't get our kids to stay. This [AI and energy boom] is like a rebirth. It's amazing what's happening, but we gotta break through the misinformation." — Senator Dave McCormick (R-PA)
"I know what pays the bills as a Democrat now. Literally, campaigns are based on 'f*** Trump' in their campaign commercials and their emails and outrage. It's constant rage bait. I refuse to engage in that." — Senator John Fetterman (D-PA)
Now a look at the state of Pennsylvania. It's one of the most important states in this presidential race. Biohub: The Future of Biology is Open-Source with Mark Zuckerberg, Priscilla Chan, and Alex Rives

No Priors: AI, Machine Learning, Tech, & Startups(83.3K)·56:21·7.5K views·2026-06-10
Mark ZuckerbergCo-founder, Chan Zuckerberg Initiative & CEO, Meta Priscilla ChanCo-founder, Chan Zuckerberg Initiative Alex RivesCo-founder and CEO, EvolutionaryScale
TL;DRThe Chan Zuckerberg Biohub has committed $500 million to its Virtual Biology Initiative, bridging frontier AI with physical wet labs to build open-source "world models" of biology.
Highlighting this effort is the launch of ESM3, an open-source AI engine that has...

TL;DR

The Chan Zuckerberg Biohub has committed $500 million to its Virtual Biology Initiative, bridging frontier AI with physical wet labs to build open-source "world models" of biology. Highlighting this effort is the launch of ESM3, an open-source AI engine that has predicted 1.1 billion protein structures and successfully designed therapeutic-level antibodies digitally.

Evolution of the Biohub: Accelerating Global Science

  • The Century Goal: Originally founded ten years ago by Mark Zuckerberg and Priscilla Chan, the Chan Zuckerberg Initiative (CZI) established the Biohub with the ambitious goal to cure, prevent, or manage all disease by the end of the 21st century.
      • While early conversations with Nobel-winning scientists were met with skepticism, advancements in AI have made this timeline seem increasingly realistic—and perhaps even conservative.
  • The Open-Source Philosophy: Rather than acting as a traditional biotech firm seeking to cure specific diseases directly, Biohub focuses on building long-term, open-source tools to empower the broader academic and clinical communities.
      • *Silo Elimination:* The strategy directly addresses systemic bottlenecks in science, where tools developed in individual labs are often lost when researchers graduate, or where valuable data is kept behind institutional walls.
      • *CELLxGENE:* Born out of CZI’s early funding of the Human Cell Atlas, this simple single-cell transcriptomics annotation tool has evolved into one of the world's largest public corpora of single-cell data, now serving as the foundation for modern transcriptomic models.

Bridging Frontier AI and Wet-Lab Biology

  • Hierarchical Biological Modeling: Biohub is modeling biology systematically from the micro to the macro scale.
      • *Hierarchical Steps:* To understand complex dynamic systems (such as the immune system or multi-cell communication), models must first accurately simulate proteins, then individual cells, and finally systemic interactions.
  • The Closed-Loop Data Strategy: Unlike large language models (LLMs) that train on massive amounts of existing internet data, biological data at scale does not exist. Biohub overcomes this by coupling AI development with state-of-the-art wet labs to generate proprietary data.
      • *New York Biohub:* Focuses on cellular engineering to record real-time biological activity inside the body.
      • *Chicago Biohub:* Creates specialized devices to measure and model systemic inflammation, which is tied to a vast array of chronic illnesses.
      • *San Francisco Biohub:* Features a structural biology center with powerful cryo-electron microscopy (cryo-EM) to visually validate molecular structures.

ESM3: An Open-Source Frontier Protein Engine

  • The ESM3 Launch: Biohub recently released ESM3, a language-model-based "world model" of protein biology trained on billions of protein sequences.
      • *Unprecedented Scale:* ESM3 operates on a Pareto-optimal frontier of speed and accuracy, which the team used to predict the structures of over 1.1 billion proteins.
  • Emergent Protein Design: Because ESM3 is trained to understand the underlying grammar of protein biology, capabilities like advanced molecular design emerge naturally.
      • *Zero-Shot Antibody Synthesis:* Researchers used ESM3 to digitally design single-chain antibodies targeting PD-L1. Out of hundreds of thousands of digital trajectories, they synthesized and tested only 96 physical proteins in the lab, successfully identifying nanomolar binders—the affinity threshold required for therapeutic drugs.
  • Democratizing Research: By open-sourcing ESM3, Biohub has turned high-barrier laboratory processes that traditionally require screening millions of physical antibodies into accessible, digital computer simulations.

Transforming Drug Discovery and Rare Diseases

  • Addressing the Clinical Trial Bottle Neck: Traditional drug development takes 15 years and costs upwards of $1.5 billion, with the vast majority of capital lost to late-stage clinical failures.
      • *Toxicity Prediction:* Biohub aims to use comprehensive single-cell atlases to digitally predict off-target side effects (such as renal toxicity) before human trials by simulating how drugs interact across all cell types.
  • Unlocking the "Long Tail" of Rare Diseases: Venture capital-backed biotech firms often ignore rare diseases due to poor financial economics. Biohub's open-source tools empower self-organizing patient registries and biobanks (such as the *Rare as One* cohorts) to drive their own therapies.
  • The Next Frontier (The Virtual Cell): Biohub’s primary mid-term research target is the creation of a "Virtual Cell."
      • *Generalization Capabilities:* This model will integrate proteomics, genetics, and transcriptomics to accurately simulate how cells behave and respond to entirely unseen chemical and biological interventions.

Notable Quotes

"The theory isn't that we're going to cure the diseases, we're not. It's that we want to help accelerate the pace of progress for the whole scientific field." — Priscilla Chan
"Our vision is not that there's going to be like some central superintelligence that solves all of science. I think like people are really important and I think will be more important in the future, and giving people more tools to be more productive is going to be like a critical part of any kind of positive future." — Mark Zuckerberg
"We folded over 1.1 billion proteins and predicted their structures, and we didn't design a model for antibodies, we didn't design a model to be able to bind one particular target, we just designed a model that could understand proteins and you kind of get protein design as an emergent property." — Alex Rives
We just want to give tools to the whole scientific community. We want to understand how biology works. I want to understand the genetics of this person. I want to understand the risks they have to different illnesses. My goal is to be able to treat the individual as an individual, understand the mechanisms, and be able to intervene. We'll have a bigger impact by getting this in more scientists' hands quicker by doing it as open-source projects instead. It's not just like there's some factory somewhere that you can pay to produce the data. You actually need to invent new, novel scientific approaches. The theory isn't that we're going to cure the diseases—we're not. It's that we want to help accelerate the pace of progress for the whole scientific field. We folded over 1.1 billion proteins and predicted their structures. We didn't design a model for antibodies, and we didn't design a model to be able to bind one particular target; we just designed a model that could understand proteins. If we could design a protein to actually change the physiology, then we can actually cure someone. Today on No Priors, we're joined by Mark Zuckerberg, Priscilla Chan, and Alex Rives. We'll be talking about Biohub and all their various efforts to now start applying AI at scale to do world models of cells and different levels of interactions across biology. Mark, Priscilla, thank you for doing this. Yeah, thanks for having us, this is fun. Alex, congratulations on the new mission. Thank you. You guys made Biohub your primary philanthropic effort and then committed $500 million to this virtual biology initiative. Can you tell us a little bit about why you did that, and how you went from "we should fund this" to "this is like who we are"? So, Biohub in its current form we're super excited about. We feel like it's a really good fit for who we are, what we bring to the table, and what we can achieve together. But this work started 10 years ago when we were thinking about how we can give back. Mark wanted to build an organization that could cure, prevent, or manage all disease by the end of the century. And we had a series of hilarious meetings with scientists where, like, famous Nobel Prize-winning scientists were just laughing at us. Was that your starting line? "We're going to cure all disease?" No, no. No, no, and to be clear, we don't think that we're going to be the ones curing the diseases. Our goal was always to build tools that could accelerate the whole scientific field. That way, the scientific field collectively could cure all the diseases. But still, you know, people thought that by the end of the century was a stretch. Now, I think it's actually too conservative. And so we kept being like, "Okay, well..." We had this series of funny, awkward, educational conversations where we're like, "Okay, but like why? Why do you think it's impossible?" We were just being the person in the room who's like, "Well, I don't know why, you tell me." Finally, we got people to say, "Fine, if you really must know." And we're like, "You know we do, it's important." They were like, "Well, we work in silos, and when you publish, information doesn't get shared; it gets locked up for long periods of time, and we don't have tooling." They gave the example that we build a great tool by one postdoc in a lab and it lives on their computer, and when they graduate, the tool is gone. What we heard was that it was very hard to build shared tools to move science faster and build a shared knowledge base to quickly move science faster. That's sort of where we began in thinking about, "Okay, if those are the problems, what can we contribute?" Yeah, I mean, so the original Biohub model was basically to focus on long-term tool development by bringing together engineers and scientists across multiple universities to focus on long-term tool development. And it basically worked. We started off with CZI doing a number of different things, and I think over time we just felt like, "Okay, the science piece is really working," and we just kept on investing more and more and more in it until now it is basically the primary and main thing that we're doing. And we've expanded the original San Francisco Biohub to a handful now. At this point, there's New York and there's Chicago. The real focus and the unifying theme at this point is the virtual biology initiative around taking the unique datasets that are able to be generated in order to model effectively, starting with the smallest pieces of proteins, but then eventually cells and whole biological systems. But that's kind of how we've evolved. It is this idea that some of this is an AI problem and you want to build a frontier AI lab, but you need to couple that with a frontier biology effort that can do the work of basically being able to understand and get the data that you need to actually be able to build these models. Because unlike language models, where there's just like a lot of data out there on the internet, that's not really the case with biology. I mean, there are obviously a bunch of different datasets that exist that academia and scientists have generated over the decades, but a lot of the stuff that I think we want to put into this doesn't exist, right? It's like you want to be able to visualize things that people haven't been able to see before, which is why we're doing the imaging work. You want to be able to record things that are going on inside the body, which is why we're doing the kind of cellular engineering work, right? You want to be able to measure things like inflammation in ways that haven't been possible, which is why the Chicago Biohub is focused on building those kind of devices and being able to do that. That will fundamentally create new types of datasets that will allow new types of models. I think it is just a very exciting thing. Going back to what you're saying, if the scientific field primarily needs kind of tool development that now is going to empower scientists across the field to be able to do their work faster, that's what we think we can provide through this kind of long-term focus on tool development. But I think there's a fun through line on where we started, bringing us to our work with Alex that he is driving now. Our very first Request for Application, or RFA, here was around single-cell sequencing, and we wanted to look at sort of like the RNA that is transcribed in individual cells. We thought that was possible, but it was still pretty early on in understanding how different cells were expressing their DNA, to the point where at the beginning we were just funding methods—like getting people to describe how to do it so that others could share that methodology. And then that became us funding the Human Cell Atlas, which is now one of the largest databases of single-cell transcriptomes. It was getting hard for scientists to annotate the data, so we built CELLxGENE, which was like a very simple annotation tool that scientists could use to make use of that data. Then a community came around CELLxGENE, built around CELLxGENE, and started contributing more and more data that we had nothing to do with creating or funding or making happen in the world. And now CELLxGENE is a corpus of knowledge that a lot of the transcriptome-based models are based off of and is used regularly by the scientific community. But still, there were always critiques like, "This is just stamp collecting. You're just gathering bits of knowledge, bits of data, and we're not going to be able to pull scientific knowledge and wisdom and insights out of it." And we're like, "Well, we didn't have an answer for a while." And then imagine our delight when large language models became a huge topic of conversation that could make sense of large amounts of data. For me, it was like, what if we could actually understand how biology worked? What if we could move it from a discovery-based science to an engineering-based science where we could systematically understand how living beings and living cells worked, and be able to understand why things go wrong? And so when we saw that moment, we were like, "This is it. Something really big could help here." Alex, you started Meta's ESM, but you were on the path to—you know, you'd assembled a team at Evolutionary Scale, and you'd raised venture, and you were making progress on your models. What was the pitch from Mark and Priscilla where you said, "That's actually the right way to go after the mission"? Well, I think for me it was really kind of the moment when I understood that they really saw this as an integration of frontier AI and frontier biology. I had developed conviction that this is really a new era of science that's just beginning, kind of what's going to be possible with artificial intelligence. We're in the age of information theory at scale, and we have these systems that can basically predict the next token and learn world models from that; they can learn biology from the data. And so, I think that it just was really clear that to build that next kind of institution for the next era, you would really need to have frontier artificial intelligence, you would have to have frontier biology, and you would need to start to put those things in feedback and really have models that are learning from the biology. You'd need the right scale and the right people. And so, this just really felt, I think, like the way to do that. There's a variety of different models that you all have been working on, and I think it's kind of interesting because some of the earliest breakthroughs in biology were things like AlphaFold. That was a Google model that showed that you could do protein folding at scale in a really interesting way that people didn't realize was very tractable, and this was pre-sort of the really big transformer waves that came later. And then you're working on a variety of different things at different scales, right? You're doing incremental molecular modeling and protein folding, you're doing cell-based stuff, and you're thinking about interrogating larger scale systems in biology. How well do you think that extends from sort of the micro to the macro? You mentioned starting with building blocks and building up, but modeling cellular behavior is very different from modeling protein folding; the data's very different, and the modeling's different. I'm just curious, do you think it's all similar in terms of it's just data and you train stuff? Or do you think it's actually there's some differences in terms of how you actually have to deal with these systems? I mean, there are probably some differences. I mean, you can probably talk more to the specifics around this, but I think each layer is going to end up being somewhat qualitatively different. But you need to be able to understand the protein interactions in order to be able to understand how cells work. So you can't just go straight to cells in a way without understanding the protein modeling. And then if you're trying to understand something like the way the immune system works or a bunch of cells interact together, then it's tough to do that without first understanding cells. I mean, you might be able to at like a very high level of abstraction simulate a system, but if you really want to understand how it's going to work, you kind of want to build the simulations at each level hierarchically. So that's basically the approach that we're going through, starting with the building blocks and the protein. But yeah, I mean, I think that there's going to be different types of data that you want to collect for each. The modeling techniques, I think we'll see, will all keep on advancing across the board, but I do think that a big part of the strategy is this view that you need to build it up hierarchically. And, you know, one of the things that's unique about us in the space is we're very intentional that the AI efforts and the wet lab efforts are a single effort, and we've done a lot of work to bring them together. The really neat thing that we can do is really try to pull and gather data that helps us connect across sort of the hierarchy. You know, you can look at transcriptomics with space within a cell and look at where it's localizing. We can look at translucent zebrafish and look at the development across different cells and when the brain develops. We have sensors that allow us to look at cell-cell communication and different molecules, and so we can be strategic about the types of experiments and data we want to collect that helps us bridge across these. That makes it so that there's some connective tissue that helps drive the modeling—the modeling magic that happens on the backend. The reason I ask the question, by the way, is I used to be a biologist, so I have a PhD in biology and I worked in wet labs for almost a decade and everything else. Are you looking for a job? We can talk about that later. We're always hiring. I'm like Danny Glover, you know, in Lethal Weapon—I'm almost at retirement. But I think one of the things that was always lacking was this integrative nature across the different layers of biology. The developmental biologists would work on their own, the molecular biologists would be doing different experiments, and so that's why I was curious. Typically, there's a reductionist view of biology and there's a systems view, and those people didn't really work together deeply. One of the exciting things about what you're doing, actually, is how you're bridging that, and so that was kind of the basis for the question as well. Yeah, and if I could add something there, I think that we're in the age of this kind of information theory in biology. There are levels of complexity and hierarchy in biology, and each level is made up of and constituted by the lower levels. And so, as you want to have that kind of more complete description and you want to have systems that can really generalize and begin to actually answer experimental questions digitally that you could ask in the lab, you need to have the right basis for modeling at every level. And so, I think what's really unique about what we can do is, as Priscilla and Mark were saying, really build information at each of these different layers, collect them, collect those connection points, but then also really do it at the scale that will reveal that underlying information architecture. That's going to be really critical to actually be able to build digital representations that can answer new experimental questions. One of the things that inspires me most about this effort is really what Priscilla said, which is like, "Well, there's so much we actually don't understand about biology, and what if we could?" Which I think is actually very different from lots of other incredibly interesting and useful AI problems we attack, where like trying to replicate human behavior and like a lot of that data's, you know, on the internet or captured, and without pretending to understand all human behavior, you can predict a lot of it. I thought one of the most interesting things in your release was actually the mechanistic interpretability stuff you alluded to, which is, can we actually extract new knowledge from what the model believes is happening? Can you talk a little bit about that? Yeah, I'm really excited about that. So, I think in mechanistic interpretability, kind of traditionally it's been applied to large language models with the goal of understanding, you know, what is the representation space of a large language model, how does it compute things, and does that really connect to our intuitive understanding of the world. And so, there's this really rich toolkit that has been developed to start to be able to ask those questions. So, what does that mean for biology? One of the classes of models that we train are these protein language models, so they're really trained on the codes of proteins. And so, anything they learn about biology is kind of emergent. We've seen that they can learn things like biological structure and biological function, and that's just kind of emergent from this token prediction training task. So, as we think about mechanistic interpretability in those models, we're really seeing the unknown, because the models have been trained on billions of protein sequences—they've been trained on both known and unknown biology. And yet, they're developing these representations that start to kind of capture things that we can really see correspond to that reductive picture of biology that's been built up over the centuries. So, you can start to connect the dots between proteins where we kind of really don't know anything about them with proteins where we do know something, because there's that kind of underlying structural grammar that's linking them in the representation space of the model. And at the extreme, it could be that we're going to understand systems in the body that we didn't before, or the mechanism of action for a new treatment, because we can ask the model—right, interrogate that representation. That's right, the hope is that you kind of really learn the underlying basis for how it's making the predictions, so you open up the black box and you can actually understand kind of the biology that the model is representing. Asking for a friend, you guys all believe in venture-backed companies as a way to have impact on the world. What was it like, collecting data on zebrafish or the span of the data or the wet lab work or just the scale—like, what makes this a better fit for this big nonprofit ecosystem effort versus a venture-backed company? Well, I think we just want to give tools to the whole scientific community. And I mean, in order to have the biggest impact, part of it is just we're—I mean, it's not actually clear that we couldn't run it as a business if we wanted to. I just think that we'll have a bigger impact by getting this in more scientists' hands quicker by doing it as open-source projects instead. So, yeah, I mean, I think that that's kind of the approach. But I don't know, it's an interesting question. I'm not sure that—I mean, obviously, you were doing it as a for-profit company, a bunch of the modeling before, and you run into certain issues. I mean, you have to raise a large amount of money in order to build the compute clusters. I think in a lot of ways, the data is actually even more of a constraint because if you look at the scale of these models compared to language models, they're smaller, but they're smaller because the amount of data is less. In order to get the data, it's not just like there's some factory somewhere that you can pay to produce the data. You actually need to invent new, novel scientific approaches to be able to do, for example, the type of cellular engineering we're doing in New York or the types of devices in Chicago. Which is why, when we're talking about this concept of frontier biology and frontier AI, the frontier biology is you need to do real science to advance different biological methods in order to be able to observe the things that create the data that go into the model. So, it's not just like an off-the-shelf thing that you can create. Now, that's a pretty big effort. I don't know that there are like that many things like that that are done as biotechs. I think it's just the scale of the ambition of what we're doing, the time horizon over which we're committed to doing it. I think part of the theory is like, if you're building tools that are this complicated, you kind of want to have a 10 to 15-year time horizon on building out these efforts. And then the scale of capital required—I mean, I guess there's no rule that said that you couldn't do it as like an incredibly well-funded startup, but I think that this just made more sense. And then it also is simplifying strategically to not have to think about how you're going to make money with the different things. I mean, we just want to get the models in people's hands, we release them as open-source, and I think that that's like a very valuable thing to do. And again, the theory isn't that we're going to cure the diseases—we're not—it's that we want to help accelerate the pace of progress for the whole scientific field. As the person least experienced with making money here, I would say that the sort of neutral, nonprofit nature of our work actually helps harness more people to enter this effort. And to actually achieve the mission of understanding the totality of human biology and to cure, prevent, and manage all disease, you actually do need the entire academic and biotech industry to come together and to work on this in a sort of unified way, in part because there's a lot of talent out there, and it's not helpful to exclude any talent from the effort. And there's a super long tail of diseases. There are the common ones, and even with the common ones, I think if you unbundle heart disease, cancer, and neurodegenerative diseases—even if you unbundle like dementia or depression—there are many, many, many subcategories that become more and more niche, and that's not even looking at the long, long tail of rare diseases. Those often get orphaned and don't get brought along when we're looking at what the most efficient way to impact the lives of many is. But if you sort of decentralize the effort and put the tools in many people's hands, you start getting people who are like, "You know what, I am super interested in spinal muscular atrophy, and that's something I care deeply about." And if you put the tools in that person's hands, they're going to be able to make progress. Whereas, if you had to focus your efforts and make big bets, you probably wouldn't because it's just a niche, individual, small-group disease. That, in turn, if we can understand that disease process, helps us unlock knowledge about a lot more of how the human body works. Do you have any thoughts or predictions in terms of what disease areas this work will impact first? I know it's very hard to be predictive about these things, but just given the nature of the work and the nature of the models, are there areas you're most optimistic about in the short to medium term? That's actually not how we think about it, at least. The way I think about it is like, we want to understand how biology works. The ideal world is you would say, "I understand the genetics of this person." So, I want to think about people at the individual level. I want to understand the genetics of this person, I want to understand the risks they have to different illnesses, and I want to understand the mechanistic connection between, say, a gene variant, a protein, and a disease process. Because if you understand that through-line, then you can design a protein or design a drug bespoke to them and actually make an intervention. Right now, I'm sure we've all had experiences being sick, and if you have something that's even remotely non-standard, you go into PubMed, you look up a paper, you look up the supplement, and then you start going through the methods and you're like, "Am I represented in this paper?" And we're just making guesses. We really have no mechanistic understanding; we're saying like, "Okay, you're kind of like these people that we studied, and this drug kind of impacts the pathway that we think is implicated, let's try and see if anything happens." And time passes, and sometimes it works, and sometimes it doesn't. So, my goal is to be able to treat the individual as an individual, understand the mechanisms, and be able to intervene. And there are different diseases that are at different stages of filling out that whole through-line. And so, for some diseases, you just want to understand which genetic gene variants actually cause disease and which don't, and that in itself can be super empowering to patients. And beyond that, there are some diseases where we understand the chain, we just can't intervene and change a specific protein function. That's super exciting, too. If we could design a protein to actually change the physiology, then we can actually cure someone. But to me, that is just as exciting as contributing to our understanding of how someone gets sick in the first place. No, that's a very exciting vision because you're basically saying you can bring generalizable tools to provide very personalized things for each individual person, and that's the power of the approach. You have these big models that you build that can then apply anywhere. I know that you mentioned earlier that you're going to try and cure or prevent all diseases within a hundred years, and you mentioned, "Hey, it could actually be sooner now given all the advances in AI." Do you have some thought of when we think we'll be closer to that goal, or some...? I mean, I'm optimistic it'll be sooner. The thing that's complicated is that it's a dynamic system, right? So if you fix something, there will obviously be future things that you need to work on. I don't think that the current set of things that we're aware of are going to be the only things that need to get worked out, but I don't know, I think that the progress with AI is obviously very exciting on this. The other thing that I'd say, just adding to what you were saying a second ago, is we really look at more kind of systems than specific diseases. So, for example, one area that seems really important to understand is inflammation. We talked about this a bunch; this is a big focus of the Chicago Biohub, and there's a lot of data on that. At that level, it seems quite clear that it's connected to a bunch of different diseases. But rather than studying the specific diseases, we think that by trying to understand inflammation more broadly, that will make it so that other companies that can then use these tools can work on specific therapies. Another example is the immune system, which I think is a very good case to study for some of the work that we're doing in cellular engineering. When we're kind of laddering up from proteins to cells to whole dynamic systems within the body, I think that that one makes sense. It's sort of privileged—the cells can travel around through the body and all that. So, obviously that has a big part in addressing different diseases. How do you make the immune system function better? But exactly how do you connect that last mile? I think that is going to be more of something that biotech or other academics individually studying things will be better suited to do. So, this is kind of how we think about building out the toolset that just helps accelerate all these other folks. Whether the timeline is 10 years, or hopefully, less than a hundred now, I think it's useful for maybe your average doctor or patient—everybody's a patient—to think about like what's externally visible in the progress here. You worked with patients for a long time at UCSF. What should doctors look out for? What should people look out for if you're actually accelerating progress? This is the part where I'm super excited about the progress, especially with this launch that Alex and his team have put forward. And I think it's very clear that science is going to start moving pretty quickly, and I think the thing that's less clear to me is exactly how we translate to the clinic and what that looks like. And I think what has to change is actually the way we do clinical research. My hope is that we're really shortening the distance between bench research and patient impact, but there's a lot of steps there.
