So we're dipping our toe in understanding how the deployment of research needs to change given how quickly research will be progressing, but that one is still shaping up. Maybe I could say something about our most recent launch, because I think it also illustrates some of those points. Oh, yeah, please, we should actually explicitly talk about that. Yeah, definitely. Yeah, so, because I guess it was just a week ago, about now, we announced the new ESM3. This is basically an open system for scientific discovery in protein biology. It's a world model of protein biology that's been trained—it's language model-based, so it's been trained on billions of protein sequences, kind of learning these emergent representations of protein biology. And then we can use it to make predictions of atomic-resolution protein structure, and we can use it—and it's really fast, so it's blazing fast—to illustrate this Pareto-optimal frontier of speed and accuracy in structure prediction. And so, this allows us to characterize really vast stretches of the protein universe. We've folded over 1.1 billion proteins and predicted their structures, and identified features connecting all of them through mechanistic interpretability. But I think the thing that I thought was most exciting about this model is that it's this really general model of protein biology. And so, you can use it as a world model; you can actually really start to search the space of the world model to design new proteins. It's really hitting state-of-the-art across pretty much every structure prediction benchmark, and especially on protein-protein interactions and protein-antibody interactions, which is really critical for therapeutic design. And so, what we found is you can actually now use the model to design proteins and design single-chain antibodies. You can do all of this digitally, and then, in a small number of experimental trials—basically like a 96-well plate—select from hundreds of thousands of trajectories digitally, actually synthesize 96 proteins, test them in the lab in a really short, easy experimental cycle, and we found nanomolar binders there. And so, that's really the level for therapeutic activity. It's really showing that you can have these kind of general-purpose models. We didn't design a model for antibodies, and we didn't design a model to be able to bind one particular target; we just designed a model that could understand proteins, and you kind of get protein design as an emergent property. And then I also think it illustrates the power of open science and open source, because we released this as basically an open discovery engine. And so, really, anyone can build on it. It takes what are these really intensive laboratory experiments—where you have to screen through hundreds of thousands or millions of antibodies in high-throughput screens in the lab—and you can really just kind of spin up an instance in compute and now be able to generate antibodies. You should say more about sort of like we took that data when we did an antibody screen and then we validated. We looked at PD-L1 in cells, and then we looked at it under the cryo-EM, and sort of how all that complemented and validated what you were seeing in the models. That's right, yeah. I think it's really critical to actually go and characterize these molecules in the lab. We have a structural biology center centered here, we have incredibly powerful cryo-EM microscopes, and so we're really able to look at these proteins biophysically and functionally. And so, we designed proteins for several therapeutically relevant targets, and we were able to confirm their function in cellular assays... It's delightful when it works the way it's supposed to. Yeah, it's amazing. We were also able to look at the structure as well, so you can see atomic resolution kind of at the binding interface is correct. I know a lot of your work is really focused on basic research and kind of building out the fundamentals. A lot of that seems to be gated on some regulatory issues, some of it's recruitment, and it's a variety of things. I mean, at least my hope in building this comprehensive model of how cells work is actually also being able to predict off-target effects. I think you can do some of that actually with biological models. Because right now, some of the off-target effects are we just didn't know—you know, your kidney cell also expressed this receptor, and then when we tested in human, we see it happening and we see renal toxicity. And so, being in a position where if you have a single-cell atlas that looks at all the different cell types—some of which actually were not predicted before we modeled them—you can start looking at which cells actually do have receptors for the target you thought you were exclusively targeting, and be able to predict some of these downstream effects before we get into the human trials. And I think that that's actually one of the more exciting applications of a transcriptome model: to understand actually how the different cells will react when you intervene and do something. And you know, when you think about delivery mechanisms and patient care, that's where you start having to be creative. You asked like, "What disease do you want to cure first?" Yeah, maybe something just to add to that also, because you described the conventional drug development process, right? Yeah, the recruitment could also change. One of the interesting things from science and engineering is that often, you know, you can hit your head against the wall on the common problems and, in this case, diseases, but a lot of times you learn a lot more about a system from finding some kind of rare or weird side thing that's happening—an edge case, yeah. An edge case. So I don't know, I think that that's always been kind of an interesting part of this that actually connects pretty well to this, because now you're going to be able to enable a long tail of new kind of ideas to get tried and enable them to potentially get tested more easily. Barney Hussey-Yeo in conversation with John Collison

Stripe(107.0K)·39:31·710 views·2026-06-10
Barney Hussey-YeoFounder and CEO, Cleo John CollisonCo-founder and CEO, Stripe
TL;DRCleo has scaled to $400 million in ARR by using AI and a highly engaging, humor-driven approach to democratize financial health for the 99%.
%. Founder and CEO Barney Hussey-Yeo argues that verticalized AI agents will defeat generic LLMs, while explaining how executives can...

TL;DR

Cleo has scaled to $400 million in ARR by using AI and a highly engaging, humor-driven approach to democratize financial health for the 99%. Founder and CEO Barney Hussey-Yeo argues that verticalized AI agents will defeat generic LLMs, while explaining how executives can leverage AI to bypass middle management and radically flatten organizational structures.

Cleo’s Scale, Architecture, and Product Strategy

  • Unicorn-Scale Financial Advice: Cleo, an AI assistant for personal finance, recently reached $400 million in ARR and achieved unicorn status.
  • The Evolution of its Tech Stack: Founded in 2016—two years before the Transformer model was introduced—Cleo originally relied on manual regexes and traditional supervised machine learning (intent and entity classification). Today, it is a fully LLM-based system that builds a recursive personal knowledge base for every user.
  • Humor as a Product Core: Because personal finance is historically dry and highly stressful, Cleo originally hired 30 female comedians to write its copy. This comedic voice remains a primary engagement driver.
    • Roast Mode: Cleo features a popular setting that lightheartedly criticizes users for their weekend spending habits.
  • Targeting the 99%: While traditional financial advisors target high-net-worth individuals, Cleo focuses on the mass consumer market where 60% of Americans have less than $400 in savings.
    • Objective Function: Rather than optimizing purely for clicks, Cleo’s recommendation system optimizes for long-term user financial health (on a 0 to 100 score), primarily steering users toward spending less than they earn.
    • Monetization Dual Engine: Cleo operates on a freemium model, monetizing through recurring subscriptions and transactional financial products (such as cards, lending, and wealth management tools).

AI-Native Leadership and Flatter Corporate Structures

  • The Executive "Captain’s Log": Hussey-Yeo has altered his daily workflow by using custom AI agents to scan and synthesize internal company data, bypassing filtered reports from division leaders.
    • Direct Pipeline Monitoring: LLMs ingest and summarize every GitHub Pull Request (PR), Slack message, and Notion document across Cleo.
    • Assessing Velocity: This system lets the CEO directly evaluate who is shipping code at the highest velocity and quality, bypassing traditional organizational biases.
  • The Elimination of Middle Management: Hussey-Yeo predicts that AI tools will radically flatten corporate structures, significantly reducing the necessity of middle managers.
  • The Rise of "AI-Pilled" Grads: Rather than a top-heavy mentoring model, Hussey-Yeo advocates hiring highly specialized graduates (e.g., machine learning master's graduates) who can operate as "tastemakers."
    • Fleet Management: These junior employees use AI natively to direct fleets of parallel autonomous agents, enabling a small team to launch thousands of product tests rather than just a few.

The Geopolitics of Tech Capital and Regulatory Friction

  • US Regulatory and Systemic Advantage: Hussey-Yeo intentionally targets the US market, citing its superior economic and legal architecture. The US system of checks and balances (federalism, state laws, and courts) prevents a single regulatory body from stifling business.
  • UK Innovation Bottlenecks: The UK business climate suffers from a "risk-off" mindset where regulators like the FCA take too long to approve fintech projects.
    • Sandbox Failure: By the time early-stage startups navigate slow regulatory sandboxes, their 18-month venture funding cycles have expired, leading them to run out of money before proving product-market fit.
  • Pension Fund Allocation Disparity: Unlike Canada or Australia, where massive pension funds invest aggressively in venture capital, UK and European pension capital is rarely deployed into tech innovation. This forces European tech ecosystems to rely on US institutional LPs (like California pension funds), exporting European GDP growth back to the US.

The Case Against the "SaaS-Pocalypse"

  • The Edge of Domain Craft: Hussey-Yeo dismisses the trend of investors assuming frontier LLMs will make vertical software wrappers obsolete.
    • Deep Primitives: Building a great software business requires extensive industry-specific craft and logic (such as deep payment rail compliance) that generic frontier models cannot master out of the box.
    • Proactive vs. Reactive AI: Consumers do not naturally open reactive LLMs like ChatGPT or Claude to ask about their money. Effective products require proactive, agentic pushes driven by a specialized UX.
  • Poker Bot Proof of Concept: Highlighting his belief in specialized, narrow agents over generalist ones, Hussey-Yeo built a private AI agent that plays online poker. By capitalizing on rakeback opportunities and low-stakes gameplay, his agent generates approximately $200,000 annually.

Notable Quotes

"There should be a world where you don't manage your money by looking at a balance and a list of transactions, and that's coming very soon. You shouldn't have to think about finance, right? It should just happen on your behalf." — Barney Hussey-Yeo
"We've got to go from a culture of trying to conserve our legacy and our past and the greatness of Britain and Ireland, to a place where we go, 'Hey, actually, we're all stagnating, we're all declining. We actually don't have much to protect at the moment. We actually need to take some risk.'" — Barney Hussey-Yeo
"My engineering managers hate me, honestly. Because I know who's shipping, I know who is the highest velocity, highest code quality. You can actually get really rational, clear thought from these LLMs and build it up with context." — Barney Hussey-Yeo
"This kind of idea that someone wrote a prompt and they've just got a frontier model and it's going to kill everything is wildly overblown. Investors are just the silliest, most irrational people I've ever met in my life... they have no idea what's going on." — Barney Hussey-Yeo
Thanks for being here. Thanks for having me. So, Cleo—you guys are presumably familiar with them, a phenom in the consumer financial advice space, 400 million in ARR. As of last month. Yes, a unicorn, and really rapidly growing, one of the top AI startups and based right here in Shoreditch. But before you started Cleo, I want to go back to your prior career. You were a poker shark, is that right? Shark, a fish. I played at university and I funded my university career through playing online poker. I only know what I've seen in *Rounders* and *Molly's Game* and all this kind of stuff. So how does actually playing poker professionally work? This was back before you had GTO and the solvers. It was all online, and you would play on multiple sites. You'd play four to six hours a day, heads up. Actually, really interestingly, I've just built an AI agent that now plays for me and earns rakeback. I make about—I shouldn't say this publicly—but I earn about 200k every year now, just through my little agent that plays and barely beats the low stakes. But yeah, I used to play low-to-mid stakes heads up, and it was quite profitable. And so, GTO is game theory optimal, which is doing the exact right thing that the probability tables would tell you that you should do, right? And you didn't have that in 2012, '13, or '14 when you were playing? Really? It was invented that recently? Only in 2016 or '17 did you have the solvers where we actually learned how you could beat the game. You're still right at the edge. You can probably beat the GTO solvers, but it's basically a solved game, which means online play is kind of dead now. But you should play tournaments. Go play a live tournament, they're always fun. No, because I'd be playing against your bot, probably. Yeah, don't play online against me. You're going to get rinsed. That's very interesting. A lot of these things are discovered later than one would think. Black-Scholes, for example, is a relatively recent invention for valuing financial options, and I hadn't realized that GTO was such a recent invention in poker. Well, you don't want to tell people if you've got an edge, right? So there are probably all these things in hedge funds where you could have a massive economic benefit to GDP, but they are kept secret. Keep it secret. Exactly. This happened in cycling as well, in pro cycling, where people used to inflate their tires up really high, to about 120 PSI. Then, one Tour de France team figured out that everyone was overinflating their tires and you don't need to do that. They kept it a secret from everyone for a long time. Anyway, there's still plenty of room for new inventions, which is what we're taking away here. But we are not here to talk about pro cycling or pro poker; we're here to talk about Cleo. So, maybe just to orient people who aren't familiar or aren't users of the product: what does it do, and how did you guys get here? Well, we started in 2016 as an AI assistant for your money, which was weird in 2016 and now is like the thing to be building. Back then, building an AI assistant was completely weird. Natural language processing was barely working, but it was the really big research output at the time. I did my Master's in machine learning, and we'd just gone from the vision era of beating AlexNet, and then NLP became the thing all my professors were interested in. I had worked in fintech as a data scientist, and I had this fascination with machine learning, knowing it was going in that direction. So, the thesis was that everyone's decisions are wildly unoptimized, and your financial life is the most stressful and important thing in your life. How could you make a billion people make better decisions every day, or make decisions on their behalf? How could you change the economy, the world, and everyone's life through doing that? The GTO of your financial life, if you will. I don't think it would sell, but maybe to you, John. It's a free idea for the opening paragraph of a magazine article on Cleo. But that's interesting, right? Because you were founded in 2016. That's obviously before ChatGPT, but that's also before LLMs. It was two years before the transformer. Even back then, the Meta Lab we were just talking about was really focused on it. They were pushing out a bunch of good research. Back in the day, it was just intent classification. Then, two years later, it was the transformer, then BERT. Everyone in computer science knew this was going to be a thing, and then everyone else just woke up when GPT-3.5 dropped. But you could see it coming, right? I presume today you're heavily LLM-based. But how did it work back then? How were you providing the financial advice? The user inputs some data, and then what? It started with regexes. We had a lot of regexes to start with. But then it was just traditional supervised learning. You had intent classification and entity classification. To be a bit nerdy, intent is what you say in a sentence. So it was big, multi-class supervised learning. Then we had, and still do, recommender systems and reinforcement learning to push information to you at the right time. We've gone from a supervised learning world to an LLM-based world, and that completely changed our architecture and our product, actually. The conceit of Cleo is that you should have personalized financial advice. How personalized does financial advice actually need to be? There's a view that you just need to have the right savings rate, invest in a low-fee index fund, and avoid buying certain financial products. It's like how you shouldn't eat foods advertised on TV—the good foods don't have an ad budget. Similarly, you shouldn't buy financial products that are aggressively sold. You should just buy a low-fee index fund, and you shouldn't have a home-country bias. You shouldn't buy the FTSE 100; you should buy the MSCI World Index. Is that not enough financial advice for everyone? This is showing how out of touch you are, John, with the real consumer and the normal humans on the street. Your life is your money. It's the most stressful thing for the majority of people. You spend so much boring admin time thinking about how you make financial decisions—from where you are going to live, what your mortgage or rent will be, to how big it should be. Every single thing you do every day is a financial decision in reality. You make all these transactions every day, lots of them on Stripe. Imagine a world where everyone was making rational, sensible, more optimized decisions. If you think about how much money goes towards consumer credit or paying interest when it doesn't need to, you could really change things. It's not just the top 1% of people who get a financial advisor; it's the 99% who actually need the financial advice. The people living with one, two, or three months of savings can have a better life and make better decisions if you can do it for them. You shouldn't have to think about finance. It should just happen on your behalf. If you need credit, you should just ask, and it should go get it for you. If you have wealth, it should just go get the best return, tax-free. We should have a world where you don't manage your money by looking at a balance and a list of transactions, and that's coming very soon. Where are you generally trying to steer people? I can imagine it involves avoiding bad financial products, like borrowing on a credit card, or doing price comparisons for a mortgage, or looking for tax efficiencies. It's actually about their daily lives. Think about most consumers in the US: 60% of Americans have less than $400 of savings. It's how they spend their money every single day. How much do they earn? What's their income volatility? How much are they spending on rent, mortgages, or subscriptions? You can really help someone just by laying it out for them and making better decisions on their behalf. On the product side, things are wildly unoptimized, especially at the lower end. They're paying incredibly high interest rates, and they are not getting any returns on savings. Most people don't have investments; their money just sits in a cash ISA in the UK. The amount of GDP growth and financial wealth you could be creating for people that is left on the table is crazy. I think there are trillions of dollars of good you can do for society and humans, but also for the economy as well. If you had this capital moving effectively through the system, you could dramatically increase GDP growth. How do you advise people on spending? There is the online meme about millennials and avocado toast. Are you trying to reduce the amount of avocado toast consumed, or how do you actually steer people in an actionable way? Do you know what's funny? Back in the day, because the AI wasn't as intelligent, we had to rely heavily on humor. We had 30 female comedians on staff. Cleo is a female agent, so we had these comedians write every single line of copy, both what we pushed and what we responded with. We found that humor and money actually go together weirdly well, which is something no traditional financial institution does. Bankers are generally not known for their humor. That was a real unlock for us in terms of engagement. There's a roast mode in Cleo that makes fun of your spending. I don't know what it would make fun of for you, John—maybe the cheeky pints. But it will roast you for what you did over the weekend at the bar. People really like that, and it's a way to engage them in simple, actionable financial advice. Increasingly, it's about taking action on their behalf—moving accounts, moving money, or getting them the best credit for that specific moment. Do you have a way of measuring how Cleo is steering people's financial health? Yes. We have constructed a view of financial health for every individual on Cleo. There is a score from 0 to 100 based on all these variables and features. We know whether you are leveling up your financial health or degrading it based on your actions. The objective function of our recommender system used to just be engagement—like, can we make you click this button because it's fun. Now, it is financial health and engagement combined. We are optimizing for whether a bit of advice or an action leads to their financial health getting better two or three months down the line. That means spending less than they earn, which is the core primitive. Yes, it's the core primitive of every human in that low-to-middle income bracket. Who do you compete with? Is this substituting for existing products, or is it a completely new thing? Think about how people make financial decisions today. It's a vibe-based optimization game for most people. Maybe it's written on the back of a napkin or a cigarette packet. The vast majority of consumers don't budget, and they don't think about their money in a rational way, or they do it too late. There is only a specific group of nerds—probably everyone in this room, actually—who will have an Excel sheet and optimize their own finances. Sorry, I shouldn't make fun of the audience, but we are really in the advice game for people who don't have advice. I think there are going to be loads of avenues like this. Imagine health—we all make terrible decisions for our health all the time. We've all got a Whoop now and we're getting into it. There are going to be these big verticals in your life, whether it's education, health, or finance, where decisions are wildly unoptimized today but will become much more thought through. Do you think you will eventually go broad and expand beyond financial advice? There is still a lot to do in financial advice—it's a big TAM, so we're going to nail that first. I think you're going to see four or five agents that go deeply vertical in specific areas of your life. I don't think they are going to be built by the frontier model companies. I think they will be companies that focus entirely on health, going all the way to the MRI and the blood test, with a whole stack of advice. I'm bullish on that, and I'm less bullish on OpenAI being the interface for everyone for everything. Do you see a competitive dynamic where people might just connect their bank account to Claude or ChatGPT and start asking questions instead of using a vertical tool like Cleo? Well, you can connect your Plaid account to ChatGPT and ask questions today. But I would bet a huge amount of money that they get very little engagement, and it becomes a side project that goes nowhere for that business. To engage people with financial advice, it has to be proactive and agentic. People don't wake up and say, "Ooh, how much did I spend on whatever?" It's just a boring thing to think about. The agent has to be always on, always pushing information. If you think about the frontier model companies, their products are very reactive. It's replacing Google Search for most people today. You have something in your head that you want to research, so you look it up. But there is so much space in the product cycle for a proactive, intelligent, always-on agent. That requires a lot of effort, training data, and an understanding of what to say to a user and when. It feels to me that we're early in personalization. If you look at how something like Claude works under the hood, it's a static model with a bunch of flat files writing notes to itself. It's like the movie *Memento*, where he's writing on his hand and that's the only memory he has. That is basically AI models today. I'm curious about your experience trying to build personalized products when current AI technology is fundamentally a bit resistant to personalization. There's definitely a meta point here about how we store memory, and when we will get to a different form factor for that. But in reality, you can build a structured knowledge base of every single human—their financial aspirations, their health, their goals, and every conversation they've ever had. You might remember Karpathy talking about doing things recursively with LLMs. We've been doing that for years. It is a powerful way to build a structured knowledge base. I was also giving you a bit of grief about Stripe not being intelligent enough; they should be that knowledge base at Stripe. It should connect every card to every user, knowing who they are, their income, and where they spend. We've built that over time for every user, and we have a recommender system that works from that knowledge base. We look for the exact message or action that will move the objective function the most—improving financial health and delivering the right information at the right time. Text works perfectly, as long as it's stored in a thoughtful, clear schema and database. One thing we were talking about backstage is how interesting the concept of personality in AI is. At the beginning, people might not have predicted that personality would be such a big deal. We were talking about the new Anthropic model, Claude 3.5 Sonnet, and what its personality is like. Or famously, GPT-4o, which had a controversial, sycophantic personality that some people loved and others hated. I get the impression that for Cleo, the personality of the agent is a big part of the differentiation. What goes into getting that personality right? Do you tune it for different users, or do you try to suss out what personality people want? Have you moved beyond the army of comedians, or is that still how you engineer it? I think this is one of the biggest things. Maybe the frontier labs don't need to worry about it—maybe they should be boring, generic, and just serve as data with some intelligence. But for any brand or company doing this, personality moves the metrics. The personality and personalization dramatically change engagement and what a user does. Being funny, being concise, and writing well—all of these things move our MAUs and our revenue. We've known since the first few months of building this business that the brand and the tone of voice matter a lot. It used to be a linear, supervised learning model with one tone of voice. Since LLMs, it is now a much more personalized tone of voice based on your income, your financial health, and where you live. Whether you're in Boston or San Francisco, it will talk to you however it thinks you want to be spoken to, and you can craft that as well. Is your general learning that finance is a high-stakes topic that makes people nervous, and you're trying to make it fundamentally more approachable? Yes. All great companies take the inverse approach. Take Stripe—payments used to be completely archaic and complicated, requiring enterprise sales. They did the opposite, making it simple, dev-friendly, and a one-click thing. It's the exact same with Monzo. It used to be a terrible user experience to do anything with a bank, and they made it simple, transparent, and less opaque. We looked at complicated financial advice and decided to make it really simple. It's usually boring, so we made it fun. We just took the inverse position. It's product contrarianism or product differentiation. Exactly. You always want to take it to its most extreme form. Look at the biggest pain point of the incumbent, and go incredibly hard at that pain point to make it a delightful experience. It's a great hack for building your first product. How is the landscape of the financial products you advise people on changing? Borrowing is presumably changing at an industry level with BNPL, but is the mortgage industry changing? There are only so many primitives with money, as you know. You can borrow, or you can spend. There is only a finite set of things you can do, so all innovation is useful but slow, and heavily guided by regulators. Buy now, pay later is a great example of improving on credit cards, which have been a bad deal for years. But it's not that interesting generally. Hopefully, the biggest thing will be stablecoins, changing how we transact and moving away from traditional rails. We build a lot of financial products—and I shouldn't say this because my teams work on them—but I find them slightly boring because of the limited primitives. But the intelligence and the advice around them is where it gets interesting. All of these products are heavily regulated, so they end up in very specific streams. But should people be consuming a lot of BNPL or a little? Well, they shouldn't be maximizing it. What happens generally is consumers take out a credit card, max the limit, and then stay at that max limit paying only the minimums for ten years. It is the most inefficient form of credit in society. BNPL is a better way to pay over a defined period, making it a termed product. But if you just use it to borrow more, that's presumably bad. This has been my whole raison d'être. Credit is an important part of the economy, but it is used poorly by so many people. That is why intelligence is the answer. We need to help people make better decisions about how much credit they consume and how much they spend. If you provide financial products without the advice—I don't want to bash Robinhood—you end up just trying to get the most margin. It leads you down a road of pushing day trading, which isn't inherently bad, but it can be used the wrong way. So you can't be purely revenue-maximizing. One hundred percent. I think that leads to bad outcomes for consumers and the business as a whole. The intelligence, the decision-making, and helping users automate things is critical. If you were in charge of regulating financial products in the UK, what would you change? Where do I start with the UK and its regulators? Innovation is good. Creative destruction is good. It is how you move every industry in the world forward. Creative destruction drives better outcomes for consumers and businesses alike. You need to enable creative destruction and allow a level of risk-taking for upstarts to take on incumbents. All too often in the UK, it's, "Please come to our sandbox and let's work together for two years," when a venture funding cycle is actually 18 months. Their attempts to be helpful normally wind up constraining the company. I've seen so many angel investments where the founders tried to engage the FCA, but it took so long that they ran out of money before proving product-market fit. If you're a founder, I think you need to take some risk early on. Taking early risks is how you build something incumbents can't easily replicate. Is there not enough risk-seeking behavior in the UK, or is it just not enabled in the right way? If you look back at the Federalist Papers, they are a great insight into how you should run both a company and a country. You had checks and balances with state and federal power. We need more of that in the UK. In the UK, Number 10 and the regulators have complete power over yes-or-no decisions across 70 million consumers. That doesn't happen in the US. In the US, you have the courts, the regulators, and the states. Those checks and balances make America and capitalism work. That is why I love building in America, and why all my users are there. It is a better place to do business and leads to more rational outcomes. You get dramatic polarization, but the checks and balances of US institutions are why America is winning versus Europe. So, to synthesize: you think we need a broader embrace of creative destruction in the business climate? Yes. We have to go from a culture of trying to conserve our legacy and the past greatness of Britain and Ireland, to a place where we realize we are stagnating. We don't have much to protect at the moment; we actually need to take risks. We need to go from a conservative, risk-off mindset to a "we're going to build great stuff and deploy capital" mindset. All these pension funds and institutions that could be doing productive things in society need to shift from conservatism to risk-on creative destruction. Does that filter down into how people should invest, both at the individual and institutional levels? If you could tune how people invest, what encouragement would you give? At the individual level, it's a largely solved problem, even if people aren't doing it. The solution is the world index fund, or investing in the S&P 500. It's an almost solved problem if people make rational decisions, and Cleo can help with that. The new baby accounts initiative, or the Trump accounts, is also a good thing to get people investing from a young age. But on an institutional level, there is a dramatic difference between the US, the UK, and Europe. If you look at our pension capital, which should be the big pool going into creative destruction, only a tiny fraction in the UK goes toward venture capital. My VCs are actually backed by California pension funds as LPs. So, we aren't returning GDP growth to the British state; it's all going back to California. It's bad for Europe if we don't have the ability to deploy institutional capital this way, unless we want a completely bipolar world dominated by China and the US. It is an interesting point that US, Canadian, and Australian pension systems are heavily invested in venture, unlike the UK and Europe. And it has worked. Look at Canadian or Australian pension funds—I would love to be an Australian pensioner. Yes, there was a news article about one of those pension funds buying a lot of SpaceX years back. As you can imagine, they are feeling quite good this week. You run a very scaled consumer AI product, and I'm curious to talk about that. But first off, how do you make money? We make money through subscriptions and financial products. We have cards, lending products, buy now pay later, and wage access. We also have wealth products like stocks and shares. The financial products make money, the subscription makes money, and we use a freemium model. What advice do you have for people building consumer AI products? What have you learned about willingness to pay, acquisition, and what works well in this new space? I don't know if I did it the right way. I spent the first four years of the business—maybe this was the ZERP era—just focused on monthly active users (MAUs). We wanted to build a great product and just get users, with zero focus on revenue. Everyone was like, "Financial health, no revenue? This is great!" That worked until I got to the growth funds, and they asked, "Where's your revenue?" So, I don't know if I'd advise that, but in a way, I would. I'd probably do the same thing again: build a product people love, and integrate monetization later. The alternative is to make sure there is one core primitive, like Stripe, where you take a margin on something, or use tokens, and build that from day one. I wouldn't do anything in the middle or be half-hearted. It should either be baked into the product from day one, or you should focus entirely on getting users first. I'm curious about the consumer side of things. Many businesses decide they are going to be one or the other. They either sell on a usage basis, like a normal grocery store, or they use the Costco model. Costco famously makes almost no money on the products they sell; they make their money on the annual membership subscription. Do you view Cleo as both, or are you primarily in the business of selling subscriptions versus selling financial products? We are in both. Subscription has been the dominant form factor to monetize, partly because of payment rails. It is historically hard to do microtransactions, and transaction failures are common. Having a recurring monthly subscription works really well for software businesses. However, I can see a future where stablecoins remove friction and transaction costs disappear. At that point, you might see a shift toward the Chinese model of micro-monetization. For self-improvement products, there is often a "sunk cost" dynamic in consumer psychology. Someone buys a gym membership to get in shape, and because they spent the money, they feel obligated to use it. Is there any of that dynamic with Cleo, where people pay to invest in their financial health? There is a lot of behavioral theory in subscriptions. For example, we just added a third tier because we know that when there are three options, people naturally pick the middle one. You have to learn where humans act irrationally, both to help them and to build a business. You guys are kind of like Duolingo—harnessing people's irrationality for good rather than evil. Let's not write that in the press! But yes, we're just bootstrapping. Speaking of irrationality, poker players talk about going "on tilt," where they abandon their strategy and make irrational decisions after a losing streak. Do you see that behavior in the app, and do you try to get people back on the straight and narrow? It's human psychology, and it's the biggest thing. Money is incredibly personal, emotive, and psychological. People lose a job or go through a breakup, and we see them go completely on tilt and make irrational decisions. We are definitely trying to help in those circumstances, but humans are humans. We build mechanisms and features to address that. You started Cleo before the current wave of AI, but you are still a relatively small and fast-growing company. How AI-native is Cleo internally? What ways of working should others be borrowing from you? My day-to-day has changed dramatically since January or February. I spend my time completely differently now. Please, tell us. Give us the before-and-after. The before was a company of 500 people where you had division leaders. You would talk to them, farm for information, give your opinions, and review pre-reads. The main way to get information was to talk to all these leaders, give feedback, and repeat. You don't need to do that anymore. One of the interesting things is how you can now understand what is happening in your organization day-to-day. You have Slack messages, Notion docs, and pull requests (PRs). Since software is the output of our company, I now know what we are shipping in a visceral, clear way that I didn't know before LLMs. How are you practically synthesizing all this information? Are you using a third-party tool, or did you build something internally to scour Slack and ingest documents? I use the workflow that Karpathy described for a lot of things. I have LLMs go over every PR, summarize what it does, grade it, and tell me what's going on. It builds a knowledge base by scouring all the Slack messages across all channels. You take disparate information from across the entire org and synthesize it into a higher level. It's like assembling a captain's log, going around the organization with a clipboard. Yes, on a dramatic scale that you could never have done manually. And then you get humans to input their pre-reads, dashboards, and daily work into the log, which becomes more aggregated and synthesized over time. Are you doing that at Stripe? I'm not doing specifically that. But organizations are wrestling with this right now. Consumer AI tools are incredibly useful, but they're trained on external corpuses. Internally, companies are trying to open up their data without exposing sensitive information, balancing permissions and security. But as you say, does it drive your internal leaders up the wall that you are going straight to the source for information? My engineering managers hate me, honestly. Because now, I know exactly who is shipping, who has the highest velocity, and who has the highest code quality. Before, I had a filtered view passed down through leaders with their own biases and incentive structures. Now, you can get rational, clear context from these LLMs. The middle layer at Cleo definitely hates me, but organizations are going to become much flatter because of this. It will lead to a much more efficient organizational and information flow. Do you think the average number of people a manager at Cleo manages will be different in two years? Yes. I've never believed that you have to do a one-on-one with every single direct report every day. I really hope we will have fewer middle managers, and organizations will definitely become flatter. The only caveat is that you still need to nurture junior people, giving them advice and helping them grow. But those endless layers of managers—which is probably half the Stripe team in this room—need to change. Instead of hierarchical business units, you might see way more parallel bets being run in a much flatter setup. You can have a huge impact with a small number of people. Many wonder if we'll transition to an apprenticeship model for junior employees. Instead of one senior manager supervising eight juniors, you might have a tighter, one-on-one transmission of knowledge. I actually think the inverse will happen: you'll see one senior person with 36 juniors. We hire grads from UCL and Cambridge who finished their machine learning master's programs. They are completely AI-pilled and have been using these tools since their A-levels. They are incredibly good. You'll want junior people who understand LLMs managing a fleet of agents. In software, you always want more software, but it has to be directed at the right things. You need tastemakers who can run parallel agents and recurse with more and more tokens. I love the idea of taking 2,000 bets next year instead of the 10 we took last year. Last question: what is your most contrarian AI take at the moment? I'm very AI-pilled, so it's hard to be absolutely contrarian. But look at the "SaaS-pocalypse" narrative, where frontier labs are supposedly dominating everything and every tweet claims a new wrapper has destroyed some industry. There is so much work that goes into building a great business like Stripe. There is so much domain expertise required. How much have you learned about payments that no one else in the world should ever have to know? Way too much. Exactly. There is so much craft that goes into building a great consumer or B2B product. You are still going to have great software companies that are completely vertical and really deep. The idea that someone writes a prompt, hooks up a frontier model, and kills off an entire industry is wildly overblown. Investors are some of the silliest, most irrational people I've ever met, and I can't believe they control all this capital. So you think people are over-extrapolating? They have no idea what's going on. They don't have any technical knowledge, so they just sit on the fence and put money into frontier labs—and Stripe, apparently. The reality will play out in a couple of years. The economy is driven by software, and has been for 30 years. We want more software companies because that is where GDP growth comes from. We want more creative destruction, and that is what AI will lead to. I like abundance—it's not a contrarian take, but I'm pro-abundance. That is a great note to end on. Lots of creative destruction to come, the story is not yet written, and it's not just a small number of firms that will be shipping everything useful. With that, our programming for Tour London comes to a close. Thank you all for coming, and we're going to roll into a reception out in the expo hall. We can't wait to see you next year. Thanks, Barney. Thanks, dude. It was fun.

How Mistral Is Building Frontier AI for the Enterprise | NVIDIA AI Podcast Ep. 301

Evolution from Research to Platform: Founded...

TL;DR

Mistral AI is driving enterprise AI adoption by pairing frontier open-weight models with Mistral Forge for deep customization, while collaborating with NVIDIA to leverage Blackwell GPUs and train next-generation open models.

Mistral's Strategic Shift to Enterprise & Customization

  • Evolution from Research to Platform: Founded 2.5 years ago by three former Meta researchers, Mistral has scaled rapidly from 3 to over 700 employees. To move beyond "throwing weights over the wall," the company built a robust enterprise platform featuring Model Context Protocol (MCP) connections, auto-scaling host sandboxes, and Mistral Compute—a proprietary infrastructure initiative launched to build and ship data centers to customers.
  • The Case for Small, Specialized Models: Mistral focuses on tailoring smaller, highly efficient models for repetitive steps in enterprise agentic workflows. By narrowing a model's input/output space, enterprises can run operations faster and more affordably.
  • Mistral Forge: This newly released platform distills Mistral’s in-house training pipelines, evaluation frameworks, and data tools. Key enterprise use cases include:
      • Proprietary Codebases: Fine-tuning coding models on massive, private code repos and domain-specific languages that are absent from public web training data.
      • Multilingual Customization: Continuing pre-training with targeted data mixtures to make models fluent in underserved regional languages, such as Southeast Asian languages.
      • Industrial Specifications: Training models to comprehend massive, proprietary manufacturing and engineering specifications.

Hardware Optimization and the NVIDIA Partnership

  • The Nemotron Coalition: Building on their previous joint release (Mistral NeMo 12B), Mistral and NVIDIA are actively collaborating to train a new frontier open-source model. The project merges NVIDIA’s large-scale data center infrastructure expertise with Mistral’s pre-training and multi-modality research capabilities.
  • Blackwell Training Gains: Mistral has been utilizing NVIDIA GB200 systems, yielding an immediate 2.5x out-of-the-box training speed improvement for large sparse mixture-of-experts (MoE) models. The company is already planning further scaling with GB300 systems.
  • Inference and NVFP4 Precision: Running inference using NVFP4 precision natively on NVIDIA hardware significantly reduces cost and increases throughput. However, Mistral is actively refining attention mechanisms to prevent degradation during ultra-long-context retrieval at this compression level.

The Open-Weight Paradigm & Enterprise Trust

  • Reducing Resource Waste: Pre-training models on raw web data from scratch is highly repetitive across the industry. Releasing open weights allows the academic and open-source communities to build on established baselines rather than duplicating basic data compression efforts.
  • Accepting the "Six-Month Gap": For air-gapped or highly regulated enterprises, running proprietary third-party APIs is not an option. Many enterprise clients are comfortable with open-weight models being slightly behind proprietary models (e.g., six months) in exchange for absolute control over runtimes, data governance, and custom weights.

The Next Frontier: Agentic Systems and Security Hurdles

  • The AI Agent Write-Access Problem: The critical security challenge keeping Mistral’s leadership awake is establishing robust, easy-to-configure permission systems for autonomous AI agents.
  • Data Write Permissions: While defining read permissions is relatively straightforward, governing where an agent writes its output—and managing access controls based on the diverse inputs consumed during its "thought process"—remains a major unsolved industry hurdle.
  • Roadmap Ahead: Through 2026, Mistral is focusing on co-developing frontier models with NVIDIA while executing its agentic platform roadmap to simplify deployment and security configuration for enterprise partners.

Notable Quotes

"By creating models and releasing them as open weights, we're still free to work on the licensing and free to provide a software platform and services around it, and we can still build a business, but we also enable the entire community to really create around those models." — Timothée Lacroix
"We quickly saw a 2.5x improvement at least out of the box when training large sparse mixture-of-experts models especially [using GB200s]... and yeah, I think we're seeing further improvements with the GB300s as well." — Timothée Lacroix
"The main thing that's keeping me awake and thinking is really how do we make the permission system of AI agents something that's not a headache to configure, something that's simple and natural to configure but also robust and something that people can trust." — Timothée Lacroix

So especially around pre-training, I think there is a lot of wasted resources because everyone is taking the same raw data, which is the data that's available on the web, and doing their best to compress it into a fixed amount of weights. There is certainly a lot of know-how into how to select that data, how to curate it, how to do the right optimization procedure, and how to train models at scale. But essentially, everyone is doing the same thing and ending up with pretty much the same artifacts. One of the main frustrations with Guillaume when we were working together at Meta was that the entire world of research couldn't really benefit from this and couldn't really work on top of all of this effort because those models weren't open source. No other academic lab had the resources to create something like this. By creating models and releasing them as open weights, we're still free to work on the licensing and free to provide a software platform and services around it. We can still build a business, but we also enable the entire community to really create around those models. It's one of the most exciting things I've seen: how quickly the open source community around open weight models has grown and the crazy things that it has built, both in terms of the creativity of it, but also the quality of the infrastructure that it now provides. Inside Anthropic, the $965 Billion AI Juggernaut | The Circuit

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M)·47:40·688.7K views·2026-06-10
Emily ChangHost, The Circuit Dario AmodeiCo-founder and CEO, Anthropic Daniela AmodeiCo-founder and President, Anthropic
TL;DRLed by sibling founders Dario and Daniela Amodei, Anthropic has established itself as a major enterprise AI force, but its strict commitment to safety guardrails has put it on a collision course with Silicon Valley rivals and the Pentagon.
Through efforts like withholding...

TL;DR

Led by sibling founders Dario and Daniela Amodei, Anthropic has established itself as a major enterprise AI force, but its strict commitment to safety guardrails has put it on a collision course with Silicon Valley rivals and the Pentagon. Through efforts like withholding its highly powerful "Mythos" cybersecurity model, the company is attempting to prove that a private tech firm can responsibly manage the immense economic, societal, and geopolitical risks of the AI revolution.

The Sibling Partnership and the Exit from OpenAI

  • Co-Founding Dynamics: Siblings Dario Amodei (co-founder & CEO) and Daniela Amodei (co-founder & President) launched Anthropic in 2021.
      • Leadership Structure: Dario serves as the scientific visionary, while Daniela manages day-to-day operations and organizational leadership. All seven original co-founders remain with the company, a rare feat for a high-growth tech startup.
  • The OpenAI Departure: The Amodeis departed OpenAI due to fundamental disagreements over alignment, corporate values, and trust regarding CEO Sam Altman.
      • Scientific Contributions: Before exiting, Dario developed the concept of "scaling laws" at OpenAI, proving that scaling data and compute power exponentially improves model intelligence regardless of algorithm adjustments.
  • Philosophical Foundations: Anthropic trains its chatbot, Claude, using "Constitutional AI"—a set of guiding principles adapted from foundational texts like the UN Declaration of Human Rights and interfaith dialogues to prevent hallucinations, deception, and harmful outputs.

Enterprise Strategy, "SaaSpocalypse," and Rapid Growth

  • Targeting Productivity Over Engagement: Anthropic rejected consumer-facing, advertising-driven business models that encourage screen addiction or generate low-value "slop." Instead, it focused heavily on high-yield enterprise tools like software engineering.
  • The "SaaSpocalypse" Disruption: The release of *Claude Cowork* triggered massive market volatility, wiping out $285 billion in market value from traditional Software-as-a-Service (SaaS) stocks as investors realized AI could soon automate major legacy software pipelines.
  • The Impact of Claude Code: Engineered by Boris Cherny, who returned to the US from rural Japan to join Anthropic in 2024, *Claude Code* has fundamentally altered the software industry.
      • Internal Adoption: Cherny states that Claude Code writes 100% of his engineering team's code, transforming engineers from manual writers to high-level directors of thousands of parallel-running AI agents.
  • Hyper-Growth Metrics: Anthropic's business focus has yielded a 17x year-over-year increase in API volume on the Claude platform, with annualized revenue growth reaching an 80x trajectory in early 2025.

White-Collar Job Displacement and the Economic Outlook

  • Automating White-Collar Work: Dario Amodei maintains his warning that AI could eliminate up to 50% of entry-level white-collar jobs within one to five years.
      • The "Automation Hump": While AI currently acts as a productivity multiplier, Amodei warns that once automation of a role nears 100%, human workers will be displaced entirely rather than merely leveraged.
  • The Future of Human Labor: The co-founders anticipate that human employment will pivot toward physical-world labor, human-centric roles emphasizing interpersonal relationships, and ethical oversight.
      • The Medicine Example: In healthcare, AI will likely handle complex diagnostic analysis, allowing human doctors to pivot completely toward bedside care and patient empathy.
  • Advocating for Macro Policy: To mitigate severe underemployment and high economic inequality, Dario openly advocates for progressive taxation on AI companies and universal basic income (UBI) frameworks.

Geopolitical Stances and the Pentagon Clash

  • Support for National Defense: Despite an early background in anti-war advocacy, Dario believes the democratic block must maintain technological dominance over authoritarian states, strongly advocating for strict US semiconductor export controls on China.
  • The $200 Million Defense Contract: In 2025, Anthropic (alongside OpenAI, xAI, and Google) secured a $200 million contract with the Pentagon to deploy Claude on classified military networks.
  • The Pentagon Red Lines and Blacklisting: Anthropic was banned from the Pentagon after drawing strict red lines, refusing to let Claude be used for mass surveillance or autonomous weapons systems.
      • Political Fallout: The refusal to drop safety guardrails drew intense political fire, with President Donald Trump and Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth publicly branding Dario an "ideological lunatic."
  • Human-in-the-Loop Doctrine: Following a controversial US missile strike on an Iranian school using targeting data from Palantir’s Maven Smart System (which utilizes Claude), Dario defended the technology, asserting that keeping a human responsible for the final lethal military decision prevents a runaway "Doomsday machine" scenario.

The Mythos Superweapon and AI Regulation

  • The Mythos Threat: Anthropic secretly developed *Mythos*, an advanced model that demonstrated a massive leap in cybersecurity capabilities, exposing thousands of critical flaws in major operating systems and web browsers.
      • Superweapon Classification: Early corporate testers warned that Mythos behaved like a cyber superweapon and pleaded with Anthropic not to release it publicly.
  • Project Glasswing: Under *Project Glasswing*, Anthropic took the financially damaging step of withholding Mythos from commercial release, choosing instead to share it only with defensive cybersecurity groups and federal agencies like the NSA.
  • Rejecting Extreme Silicon Valley Stances: Dario criticized Silicon Valley's polarized reaction to AI safety, mocking tech figures who "yo-yo" between extreme anti-regulatory positions and sudden demands for government nationalization once they witness real technological dangers.
      • Moderate Approach: Anthropic advocates for a balanced middle ground: mandatory, proactive, pre-release testing and independent auditing of frontier models.

Notable Quotes

"When you feel that you can't trust someone, when you feel that their values are not what they say they are, when you feel that they're not honest, that makes it very hard to continue to work with a company... The way to resolve it is you go off and do your thing, they go off and do their thing." — Dario Amodei, on the decision to leave OpenAI
"This is more a debate about what the proper use of AI by the government is... We were willing to risk the future of our company to limit how these models are used." — Dario Amodei, on Anthropic's standoff with the Pentagon over military guardrails
"We view it as our job to try and proactively think about all of the things that could go wrong, because if we don't, who's going to? ... We're trying to get this right the first time, instead of waiting for things to go wrong then scrambling to justify why it's all okay." — Daniela Amodei, on the responsibilities of AI creators
"Some of the early companies that we gave this to said things like, 'This is a superweapon. You should have to own a gun license to use it. Please don't release this.' ... We have suffered enormously commercially from not releasing this model." — Dario Amodei, on the decision to restrict the "Mythos" cyber-capable model

The company began as a nonprofit that promised a safe, more open path to superintelligence. At that point in time, I know it sounds crazy now looking back, not a lot of people believed, "Hey, scale up is the way that these models are going to get smarter and better." Lying we call hallucinations, it makes something up. The models are just trained to predict the next word, so sometimes they don't know and they just invent something. Models sometimes, as we've shown in our research, can purposely try to deceive you. We have to make sure that doesn't happen in production models that we expose to customers. I don't think the government should outright take us over. Every previous powerful technology we've seen in history was either built by the government or originated with the government. So, nuclear weapons, obviously, you know initially built by the government. The internet, GPS, cell phones. AI is the first technology that's been built in the private sector and where government has not really had a serious role and is coming in late to the game. I think that's actually a dangerous and unstable situation. It is not the situation I would have chosen. This technology, I'm scared of companies having it, but I'm also scared of government having it. And then, you know, we need basic regulation of the technology. Dan Dreyfus: The Next AI Bottleneck is Copper

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·24:37·48.7K views·2026-06-10
Dan DreyfusFinancial Analyst & Critical Minerals Expert
TL;DRThe United States is transitioning from a "capital light" software-driven economic era to an infrastructure-heavy supercycle spurred by AI data centers, domestic reindustrialization, and grid modernization.
This massive structural shift faces severe supply-side...

TL;DR

The United States is transitioning from a "capital light" software-driven economic era to an infrastructure-heavy supercycle spurred by AI data centers, domestic reindustrialization, and grid modernization. This massive structural shift faces severe supply-side bottlenecks in critical minerals—most notably copper and silver—and a historic shortage of skilled craft labor, positioning hard assets as the premier hedge against imminent currency debasement.

1. The Death of the "Capital Light" Era & The New Infrastructure Supercycle

  • The End of an Economic Miracle: From the early 2000s until recently, the US experienced a hyper-efficient "capital light" growth era. Trillions in market cap were created through software, social media, and offshore manufacturing (e.g., Apple, Meta, Google) without requiring heavy domestic capital investment.
  • Fragile Supply Chains: Moving critical infrastructure to China left supply chains brittle. Recent geopolitical conflicts (COVID-19, Russia-Ukraine, Middle East) exposed this lack of resiliency, sparking persistent inflation.
  • Simultaneous Capital Cycles: Multiple capital-intensive mega-trends are now peaking at the same time:
      • Data Centers: Now a $1 trillion per year capital cycle driven entirely by the hardware demands of AI.
      • Semiconductor Fabs: Domestic chip manufacturing infrastructure is projected to require at least $750 billion, though likely climbing into the trillions.
      • Aerospace & Space: Boeing and Airbus hold a $1 trillion backlog over the next 10 years, which must now compete with the rapidly expanding space economy for the exact same raw materials.
      • Defense Spending: Broad global re-armament (US, Europe, Japan, Taiwan) is placing unprecedented demands on industrial metals.

2. The Critical Mineral Bottleneck: Copper & Silver

  • The King of Metals (Copper): Copper is the fundamental physical bottleneck of the modern transition. Solar power requires 5x more copper per megawatt than gas-fired turbines; wind requires 7x; electric vehicles require 5x to 6x more than internal combustion engines.
      • Historical Scale: Over the last 10,000 years, humanity has mined 700 million tons of copper. To support baseline GDP growth (excluding green energy and AI upside), the world will need another 700 million tons over just the next 18 years.
      • The AI Data Center Impact: A 1-gigawatt (GW) AI data center requires 50,000 tons of copper. At an expected build rate of 15 GW of capacity per year, data centers alone will require 750,000 tons of copper annually—far outstripping last year's total global supply growth of 500,000 tons.
      • The Mine Shortage: Meeting demand requires five new world-class, tier-one mega-mines to come online every year. In reality, only a handful are scheduled to launch by 2030, and building a copper mine takes 7 to 12 years.
  • The Silver Cliff: Photovoltaic solar cells and space-bound systems are depleting silver reserves.
      • Global demand stands at 1.2 billion ounces per year, while supply is only 1 billion ounces.
      • With only 600 million ounces of above-ground inventory remaining, the global supply faces a potential total stock-out within three years.

3. Geopolitical Vulnerabilities & US Government Intervention

  • China’s Grip on Minerals: China maintains an absolute monopoly on critical mineral extraction and processing. In April, China cut off US exports of crucial minerals (including samarium, terbium, and dysprosium). This cut-off of samarium-cobalt magnets left major defense and automotive manufacturers, such as Ford and McDonnell Douglas, within days of total production shutdowns.
  • The "Three Pieces of Paper" Strategy: To counter China’s leverage, the US Department of Defense and Department of Energy are actively bypassing decades-old bureaucracy by offering small domestic and Canadian resource owners three fast-track tools:

1. An immediate equity check to fund resource conversion into active mines.

2. An immediate environmental/operating permit to bypass 10-to-20-year red tape.

3. A take-or-pay offtake agreement with guaranteed minimum floor prices to ensure project bankability.

4. Grid Fragility and the Craft Labor Crisis

  • An Outdated Grid: The US electrical grid has not seen meaningful modernization since post-World War II, with some operational power lines in California dating back over 106 years. Baseline electrification (EVs, heat pumps) is already straining this infrastructure to the point of blackouts and brownouts.
  • Solar Scale Realities: Relying entirely on solar to power a 1-GW data center requires 5 GW of solar capacity (due to a 20% solar capacity factor). This would require 35,000 acres of land—an area larger than the city of San Francisco.
  • The Craft Labor Deficit: Decades of directing youth exclusively toward white-collar tech jobs have gutted the domestic skilled trade workforce.
      • Craft labor is now the single greatest bottleneck to deploying the physical infrastructure required for reindustrialization.
      • Due to severe scarcity, top graduates from technical trade institutions are currently securing starting salaries of $150,000 straight out of high school.

5. Currency Debasement & Asset Allocation

  • Federal Debt Explosions: The US has $40 trillion in government debt growing at $2.5 trillion annually, coupled with $100 trillion in discounted future social liabilities (Medicare, Social Security) also growing at $2.5 trillion annually.
  • The Printing Trap: With only $5.5 trillion in annual tax receipts, any future economic recession will force the government to print "gigadollars," heavily debasing the US dollar.
  • The 1970s Playbook: During the currency debasement of the 1970s, the US dollar lost 70% of its purchasing power. Historically, hard assets, commodities, and physical infrastructure are the premier asset classes for preserving capital in this macroeconomic environment.
  • Investment Directives: Investors should prioritize exposure to physical critical minerals (copper, silver), domestic resource developers, and the industrial service/labor providers that facilitate heavy infrastructure construction.

***
